Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Ruling of Fasting (Sawm)
Defines sawm and states ruling of obligatory fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of fasting (sawm) and distinguish the ruling of Ramadan (fard) from a specified vow (wajib) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Fasting is intentional abstention from dawn to sunset; Ramadan is fard, a specified vow is wajib.

This lesson opens the Book of Fasting in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author defines fasting legally and immediately distinguishes two levels of obligation: the Ramadan fast is fard, established by definitive evidence, while a specified vow is wajib, based on interpretable verses. This distinction is central to Hanafi epistemology and affects how compensations are handled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of sawm and its key components
From the text, extract the four components: refraining from eating, drinking, sexual intercourse, from dawn to sunset, with intention. Note the linguistic vs. legal difference.
The commentary emphasizes that intention distinguishes worship from habit.
2
Distinguish the ruling between Ramadan fast and specified vow
The text explicitly states 'صوم رمضان وهو فرض' and 'النذر المعين وهو واجب'. Understand that fard is based on qat'i evidence, wajib on zanni evidence.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school; other schools may not differentiate.
3
Recognize the evidential basis for the ruling
The commentary explains that the verses used for the specified vow are open to interpretation and therefore only yield wujub, not fard. Compare with the definitive evidence for Ramadan.
The argument involves the concept of 'takhsis' and 'zanni al-dalala'.
4
List the conditions, pillars, and validity requirements for a valid fast
The text provides: cause (shuhud al-shahr), pillar (imsak), conditions of obligation (Islam, sanity, puberty), conditions of performance (health, residence), conditions of validity (intention, purity).
Note that intention is required for each day in Hanafi (except Zufar's view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
تعريف الصوم وحكمه
كتاب الصوم
أي في بيان أحكامه
هو ترك الأكل والشرب والجماع من الصبح إلى الغروب بنية من أهله، وصح
أي عمدا أي من طلوع الفجر الصادق أي إلى غروب الشمس
صوم رمضان وهو فرض والنذر المعين وهو واجبٌ.
حكماً،
أي شهر رمضان
أي وصح أيضاً صوم النذر جملة حالية أو معترضة
كتاب الصوم: كان ينبغي أن يذكر كتاب الصوم عقيب كتاب الصلاة؛ لأن كلا منهما عبادة بدنية، إلا إنه تبع القرآن وذكر الزكاة بعد الصلاة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ ﴾ (البقرة:٤٣) ولحديث: "بني الإسلام على خمس إلخ؛ لأن فيه ذكر الصوم بعد الزكاة، وفرض الصوم بعد صرف القبلة إلى الكعبة لعشر في شعبان بعد الهجرة بسنة ونصف، والله سبحانه وتعالى شرع الصوم بفوائد: أعظمها إيجابه بشيئين ينشأ أحدهما من الآخر: سكون النفس الأمارة بالسوء وكسر سوءتها في الفضول المتعلقة بجميع الجوارح من العين واللسان والأذن والفرج فإن به تضعف حركتها في محسوساتها؛ ولهذا قيل: إذا جاعت النفس شبعت جميع الأعضاء، وإذا شبعت النفس جاعت الأعضاء كلها، وهو لغة: الإمساك مطلقاً، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنِّي نَذَرْتُ لِلرَّحْمَنِ صَوْمًا (مريم: ٢٦) أي صمتاً. (فتح) هو : أي الصوم لغة الإمساك وشرعًا ما ذكره المصنف. (ع) ترك الأكل إلخ الأولى أن يعرف بأنه الإمساك عن الفطر إما حقيقة أو حكماً كمن أكل ناسياً، فإنه ممسك ثم اعلم أن شهود الشهر سبب لصوم كله، ثم كل يوم سبب لوجوب أدائه، وركنه الإمساك، وشرط وجوبه العقل والبلوغ والإسلام وشرط أدائه الصحة والإقامة وشرط صحته النية والطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس، وقوله: "والجماع" أي ولو معنى، فدخل لو أنزل بلمس أو قبلة، وقوله: "بنية" أي بنية ترك الأكل، وفي "الكافي": بنية التقرب، وقال زفر صوم رمضان يتأدى بنية واحدة كمذهب الإمام مالك، والتسحر نية في رمضان وغيره (فتح) والشرب أي إدخال الشيء إلى الباطن أو ما له حكم الباطن. (ط،ع) بنية: أي بنية ترك الأكل لتمييز العبادة عن العادة. (ع) من أهله أي تكون النية حاصلة من أهل الصوم، وأهل الصوم مسلم عاقل بالغ طاهر من الحيض والنفاس. (ط، ع) وهو فرض [جملة حالية أو معترضة] الصيامات اللازمة فرضاً ثلاثة عشر سبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع، وهي رمضان وكفارة القتل وكفارة اليمين والاعتكاف الواجب وكفارة الظهار وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين كقوله : الله علي صوم غرة رجب سنة كذا، وستة لا يجب فيها التتابع، وهي قضاء رمضان وصوم وصوم كفارة الحلق وصوم النذر المطلق وصوم اليمين بأن قال: والله لأصومن شهراً، وقوله: "وهو واجب" أي صوم النذر المعين؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ ﴾ (الحج: ٢٩) وقوله: ﴿وَأَوْفُوا بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ إِذَا عَاهَدْتُمْ ﴾ (النحل:91) فَإِن قلت: فعلى هذا لا فرق بين صوم رمضان وصوم النذر المعين في الفرضية؛ لأن كلا منهما ثبت بالكتاب [فلم أطلق الوجوب عليه؟ قلت: خص من الآية ما ليس من جنسه واجباً كعيادة المريض وتجديد الوضوء عند كل صلاة
ونحو ذلك، فلا يكون قطعياً، كالآية المؤولة وخبر الواحد، وبمثله لا يثبت إلا الوجوب. [رمز الحقائق ١٣٥/١]
المتعة
Book of Fasting. Definition of Fasting and Its Ruling. Book of Fasting: i.e., in explanation of its rulings. It is refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention from its people. Correct. i.e., intentionally, from true dawn to sunset. The fast of Ramadan is fard, and the specified vow is wajib. Ruling-wise, i.e., the month of Ramadan. i.e., also the fast of a vow is valid, as a circumstantial or parenthetical clause. Book of Fasting: It would have been appropriate to mention the book of fasting after the book of prayer, because each is a bodily worship, but he followed the Quran and mentioned zakat after prayer, due to the verse 'Establish prayer and give zakat' (2:43) and the hadith 'Islam is built on five...', because fasting is mentioned after zakat. Fasting was made obligatory after the change of qibla to the Kaaba, on the tenth of Sha'ban, a year and a half after the Hijra. Allah legislated fasting with benefits, the greatest being its obligation through two things that engender one from the other: the calming of the soul commanding evil and breaking its control over excesses related to all limbs from the eye, tongue, ear, and private parts, for it weakens their movement in their senses. Hence it is said: When the soul is hungry, all limbs are satisfied; when the soul is full, all limbs are hungry. Linguistically, it is absolute restraint, including the verse: 'I have vowed to the Most Merciful silence' (19:26), i.e., silence. (Fath) It: i.e., fasting linguistically is restraint, and legally what the author mentioned. (Ayni) 'Refraining from eating etc.' – it is better defined as restraint from anything that breaks the fast, either actually or legally, like one who eats forgetfully, for he is still refraining. Know that witnessing the month is a cause for fasting all of it, then each day is a cause for the obligation of performing it. Its pillar is restraint. Conditions of obligation: sanity, puberty, Islam. Conditions of performance: health and residence. Conditions of validity: intention and purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. His statement 'and sexual intercourse' means even figuratively, so it includes if he emits due to touching or kissing. His statement 'with intention' means intention of refraining from eating, and in al-Kafi: intention of drawing near. Zufar said that the fast of Ramadan is fulfilled with one intention like Imam Malik's madhhab, and the pre-dawn meal is an intention for Ramadan and other fasts. (Fath) 'Drinking' means introducing something into the interior or what has the ruling of the interior. (T, A) 'Intention' means intention of refraining from eating to distinguish worship from habit. (Ayni) 'From its people' means the intention must come from those qualified for fasting, who are Muslim, sane, adult, free from menstruation and postpartum. (T, A) And it is fard [circumstantial or parenthetical clause]. The required fasts that are fard are thirteen: seven require consecutiveness: Ramadan, the expiation for murder, expiation for a vow, the obligatory i'tikaf, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking a fast in Ramadan, and the specified vow like saying 'For Allah I shall fast the first of Rajab of such year.' Six do not require consecutiveness: making up missed Ramadan, fasting for shaving the head, fasting for an absolute vow, fasting for a vow of a month, and fasting for a vow of a month. And his statement 'and it is wajib' means the specified vow, due to the verse 'And let them fulfill their vows' (22:29) and 'Fulfill the covenant of Allah when you make a covenant' (16:91). If you say: Then there is no difference between Ramadan and the specified vow in being fard, because both are established by scripture, why did he call it wajib? I say: The verse is restricted to what is of the same category as wajib, like visiting the sick and renewing wudu for every prayer, etc., so it is not definitive, like an interpretable verse and a hadith of a single narrator, and with such only obligation (wujub) is established. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1/135) al-Muta'.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the beginning of the Book of Fasting. It opens by defining fasting (sawm) legally as refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn to sunset with intention. The author then states the ruling: the fast of Ramadan is fard (obligatory), and a specified vow (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan) is wajib (obligatory but of a lower degree). The commentary explains why the author placed the Book of Fasting after zakat, following the order in the Quran and hadith. It then details the definition, noting that the legal definition is more precise than the linguistic one. The causes, pillars, and conditions of fasting are enumerated: the month of Ramadan is the cause; each day is a cause for performance; the pillar is restraint; conditions of obligation are sanity, puberty, and Islam; conditions of performance are health and residence; conditions of validity are intention and purity from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The commentary also discusses the type of intention (whether for eating or for drawing near) and mentions Zufar's view that one intention suffices for the whole month. It then lists thirteen types of obligatory fasts, distinguishing those requiring consecutiveness (like Ramadan) from those not (like make-up fasts). Finally, it addresses why the specified vow is called wajib rather than fard, explaining that the evidence from the Quran is not definitive (qat'i) because it is subject to interpretation and is like a hadith ahad, which establishes wujub, not fard.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Linguistically: absolute restraint. Legally: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from true dawn to sunset with intention.
نية (niyyah): Intention of refraining from eating (or intention to draw near) to distinguish worship from habit. Must come from a qualified person (Muslim, sane, adult, pure).
فرض (fard): A level of obligation established by definitive evidence (Quran or mutawatir sunnah).
واجب (wajib): A level of obligation established by zanni evidence (e.g., ahad hadith or interpretable verse). It is binding but not as strong as fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of a specified vow (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan)?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Fasting is defined as intentional abstention from dawn to sunset; Ramadan is fard, a specified vow is wajib, a distinction rooted in the certainty of evidence.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Definition of Sawm and the Fard/Wajib Distinction
Defines sawm and states ruling for two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of sawm and distinguish the ruling of Ramadan fasting (fard) from that of the specified vow (wajib) based on evidence strength.

Core Idea
Fasting Ramadan is fard (definitive); a vowed fast is wajib (probable) — evidence strength determines the category.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq introduces the legal definition of sawm and then immediately classifies two types of fasting: Ramadan and the specified vow. The author uses different terms — fard for Ramadan, wajib for the vow — because the Hanafi school distinguishes obligations by the certainty of their evidence. Understanding this distinction is key to navigating the Hanafi hierarchy of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
First locate the definition of sawm: linguistically 'absolute restraint', legally 'refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention'. Note that intention is a required element.
The legal definition specifies the act, time, and condition of intention.
2
Identify the two types of fasting mentioned
The passage explicitly states two types: 'صوم رمضان' (fasting of Ramadan) and 'النذر المعين' (specified vow). Note the different rulings assigned.
3
Classify the ruling (hukm) for each
Ramadan fasting is labeled 'فرض' (fard) while the specified vow is labeled 'واجب' (wajib). In the Hanafi school, fard is established by definitive evidence (qat'i), wajib by probable evidence (zanni).
Other schools may not distinguish; they use fard for both.
4
Understand the basis for the distinction
The commentary explains that the evidence for Ramadan is from the Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, and ijma — all qat'i. The evidence for vows is from general verses (e.g., 22:29) which are subject to interpretation (ta'wil) and thus zanni, yielding wujub not furudiyyah.
This shows the Hanafi principle that the strength of the dalil determines the category of obligation.

Source Text

كتاب الصوم
تعريف الصوم وحكمه
كتاب الصوم أي في بيان أحكامه
هو ترك الأكل والشرب والجماع من الصبح إلى الغروب بنية من أهله، وصح
أي عمدا أي من طلوع الفجر الصادق أي إلى غروب الشمس
صوم رمضان وهو فرض والنذر المعين وهو واجبٌ.
حكماً،
أي شهر رمضان
أي وصح أيضاً صوم النذر جملة حالية أو معترضة
كتاب الصوم: كان ينبغي أن يذكر كتاب الصوم عقيب كتاب الصلاة؛ لأن كلا منهما عبادة بدنية، إلا إنه تبع القرآن وذكر الزكاة بعد الصلاة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ ﴾ (البقرة:٤٣) ولحديث: "بني الإسلام على خمس إلخ؛ لأن فيه ذكر الصوم بعد الزكاة، وفرض الصوم بعد صرف القبلة إلى الكعبة لعشر في شعبان بعد الهجرة بسنة ونصف، والله سبحانه وتعالى شرع الصوم بفوائد: أعظمها إيجابه بشيئين ينشأ أحدهما من الآخر: سكون النفس الأمارة بالسوء وكسر سوءتها في الفضول المتعلقة بجميع الجوارح من العين واللسان والأذن والفرج فإن به تضعف حركتها في محسوساتها؛ ولهذا قيل: إذا جاعت النفس شبعت جميع الأعضاء، وإذا شبعت النفس جاعت الأعضاء كلها، وهو لغة: الإمساك مطلقاً، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنِّي نَذَرْتُ لِلرَّحْمَنِ صَوْمَا (مريم: ٢٦) أي صمتاً. (فتح) هو : أي الصوم لغة الإمساك وشرعًا ما ذكره المصنف. (ع) ترك الأكل إلخ الأولى أن يعرف بأنه الإمساك عن الفطر إما حقيقة أو حكماً كمن أكل ناسياً، فإنه ممسك ثم اعلم أن شهود الشهر سبب لصوم كله، ثم كل يوم سبب لوجوب أدائه، وركنه الإمساك، وشرط وجوبه العقل والبلوغ والإسلام وشرط أدائه الصحة والإقامة وشرط صحته النية والطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس، وقوله: "والجماع" أي ولو معنى، فدخل لو أنزل بلمس أو قبلة، وقوله: "بنية" أي بنية ترك الأكل، وفي "الكافي": بنية التقرب، وقال زفر صوم رمضان يتأدى بنية واحدة كمذهب الإمام مالك، والتسحر نية في رمضان وغيره (فتح) والشرب أي إدخال الشيء إلى الباطن أو ما له حكم الباطن. (ط،ع) بنية: أي بنية ترك الأكل لتمييز العبادة عن العادة. (ع) من أهله أي تكون النية حاصلة من أهل الصوم، وأهل الصوم مسلم عاقل بالغ طاهر من الحيض والنفاس. (ط، ع) وهو فرض [جملة حالية أو معترضة] الصيامات اللازمة فرضاً ثلاثة عشر سبعة منها يجب فيها التتابع، وهي رمضان وكفارة القتل وكفارة اليمين والاعتكاف الواجب وكفارة الظهار وكفارة الإفطار في رمضان والنذر المعين كقوله : الله علي صوم غرة رجب سنة كذا، وستة لا يجب فيها التتابع، وهي قضاء رمضان وصوم وصوم كفارة الحلق وصوم النذر المطلق وصوم اليمين بأن قال: والله لأصومن شهراً، وقوله: "وهو واجب" أي صوم النذر المعين؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ ﴾ (الحج: ٢٩) وقوله: ﴿وَأَوْفُوا بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ إِذَا عَاهَدْتُمْ ﴾ (النحل:91) فَإِن قلت: فعلى هذا لا فرق بين صوم رمضان وصوم النذر المعين في الفرضية؛ لأن كلا منهما ثبت بالكتاب [فلم أطلق الوجوب عليه؟ قلت: خص من الآية ما ليس من جنسه واجباً كعيادة المريض وتجديد الوضوء عند كل صلاة
ونحو ذلك، فلا يكون قطعياً، كالآية المؤولة وخبر الواحد، وبمثله لا يثبت إلا الوجوب. [رمز الحقائق ١٣٥/١]
المتعة
The Book of Fasting: The definition of fasting and its ruling. The Book of Fasting: meaning regarding the explanation of its rulings. It is refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn until sunset with an intention from its people, and valid. Meaning intentionally, i.e., from the true dawn until sunset. Fasting of Ramadan is fard, and the specified vow is wajib. As a ruling, meaning the month of Ramadan. Meaning, also the fast of a vow is valid, either as a circumstantial clause or parenthetical. The Book of Fasting: It should have been mentioned after the Book of Prayer, since both are physical acts of worship; however, he followed the Quran and mentioned Zakat after Prayer, due to His statement: "Establish prayer and give Zakat" (2:43) and the hadith: "Islam is built on five..." because it mentions fasting after Zakat. Fasting was made obligatory after the change of Qibla to the Kaaba, on the tenth of Sha'ban, a year and a half after the Hijra. Allah the Exalted legislated fasting with benefits, the greatest of which is that it requires two things, one derived from the other: stilling the soul that commands evil and breaking its evil in excesses related to all limbs—eye, tongue, ear, and private parts—for it weakens their movement in their senses. Hence it is said: when the soul is hungry, all limbs are fed; when the soul is fed, all limbs are hungry. Linguistically, it is absolute restraint, including His statement: "I have vowed a fast to the Most Merciful" (19:26) meaning silence. (Fath) He said: Fasting linguistically is restraint, and legally what the author mentioned. (A) The first definition should be that it is refraining from what breaks the fast, either actually or legally, like one who eats forgetfully—he is still considered refraining. Know that witnessing the month is the cause for fasting all of it, then each day is a cause for the obligation of performing it. Its pillar is restraint. The conditions for its obligation are sanity, puberty, and Islam. Conditions for its performance are health and residency. Conditions for its validity are intention and being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. His statement: "and sexual intercourse" includes even metaphorical, so it includes if he ejaculates by touch or kiss. His statement: "with intention" means with the intention of refraining from eating; in al-Kafi: with the intention of drawing near. Zufar said: The fast of Ramadan is fulfilled with a single intention, like the School of Imam Malik. Suhur (pre-dawn meal) is intention in Ramadan and other fasts. (Fath) and drinking means introducing something into the interior or what has the ruling of the interior. (T, A) With intention: meaning with the intention of refraining from eating to distinguish worship from habit. (A) From its people: meaning the intention should come from the people of fasting, and the people of fasting are a Muslim, sane, adult, free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. (T, A) And it is fard [parenthetical or circumstantial clause]. The necessary fasts as fard are thirteen: seven require continuity—Ramadan, expiation for killing, expiation for an oath, obligatory i'tikaf, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan, and the specified vow like saying: I have made a vow to Allah to fast the beginning of Rajab of such-and-such year. Six do not require continuity—making up missed Ramadan fasts, fast for expiation of shaving the head, the absolute vow, and the fast for an oath such as saying: By Allah I will fast a month. His statement: "and it is wajib" refers to the specified vow, due to His statement: "And let them fulfill their vows" (22:29) and His statement: "And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have made a covenant" (16:91). If you say: in that case, there is no difference between the fast of Ramadan and the specified vow in obligation, since both are established by the Book, why did he specify wujub for the vow? I say: the verse is excluded for what is not from its type as wajib, such as visiting the sick and renewing wudu at every prayer and the like, so it is not definitive, like interpreted verse and solitary hadith. With such, only wujub is established. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/135] The mut'ah.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sawm' (fasting) both linguistically and legally, then states the legal ruling for two specific types: Ramadan fasting and the specified vow (nadhr mu'ayyan). Linguistically, sawm means absolute restraint; legally, it is refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention. Ramadan fasting is classified as fard (obligatory with definitive evidence), while the specified vow is classified as wajib (obligatory with probable evidence). The commentary explains why the author used 'fard' for Ramadan and 'wajib' for the vow: the evidence for Ramadan is from the Quran and mutawatir Sunnah, which are definitive (qat'i), while the evidence for the vow is from verses that are interpreted (mu'awwal) and are of probable (zanni) transmission, thus establishing wujib rather than fard. The passage also lists the thirteen types of necessary fasts, distinguishing those requiring continuous days from those that do not.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Linguistically: absolute restraint. Legally: refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention.
الفرض (al-fard): Obligatory with definitive evidence (qat'i al-thubut), e.g., Quran or mutawatir hadith.
الواجب (al-wajib): Obligatory with probable evidence (zanni al-thubut), e.g., solitary hadith or interpreted verse.
النذر المعين (al-nadhr al-mu'ayyan): A specific vowed act, e.g., vowing to fast a particular day or month.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the legal status (hukm) of the specified vow (nadhr mu'ayyan) concerning fasting?
A) It is fard, like Ramadan.
B) It is wajib, a category distinct from fard.
C) It is sunnah mu'akkadah.
D) It is mubah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies obligations by the strength of their evidence: Ramadan fasting is fard (qat'i), while a specified vow is wajib (zanni), a distinction that has practical legal consequences.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate

Ruling of Intention for Fasting: The Case of Determined Acts
Specifies when intention is required for different types of fasts and addresses conflicting opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the timing and specificity of intention for different fasts and explain the proof from Quran 2:187.

Core Idea
For determined acts like Ramadan, any intention suffices; for non-determined obligations, specific intention at night is required.

This passage clarifies when intention (niyyah) is required for fasting according to the Hanafi school. It distinguishes between voluntary fasts, the determined obligation of Ramadan, and other obligatory fasts like make-up and expiations. The discussion includes proofs from the Quran and responses to the views of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of fasts
The passage categorizes fasts into voluntary (nafl), determined obligatory (Ramadan, specified vow), and other obligations (make-up, expiations). For each, different rules apply for the timing and specificity of intention.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The primary proof is Quran 2:187. The word 'thumma' (then) is used to argue that the resolve to fast can occur after dawn, permitting intention before noon for Ramadan.
The Hanafis also rely on the concept of the determined act (muta'ayyin) for Ramadan.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'then complete the fast' after the permission to eat/drink until dawn implies that the obligation begins after dawn, so the intention can be made after dawn for a fast that is already determined.
The Hanafis interpret 'thumma' as indicating delay (tarakhi), not immediate sequence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Malik requires intention at night for all fasts, citing a hadith. Shafi'i requires it for obligatory fasts but allows after noon for voluntary. The passage provides Hanafi responses.
The hadith is interpreted as negating virtue, not validity, or as applying to non-determined fasts.
5
Apply the principle of determined acts
Ramadan is muta'ayyin (determined), meaning no other fast can be performed on that day. Hence, any intention (even absolute or for nafl) fulfills the obligation.
For sick/traveler, Ramadan is not determined, so they must specify.

والنفل: أي و وصح أيضاً صوم التطوع، وهو ما زاد على الفرض والواجب سواء كان سنة أو مندوبا. (ط) من الليل: أي تصح هذه الصيامات بينة من الليل. (ط) ما قبل نصف النهار: وقيل: إذا صام رمضان بنية إلى ما قبل الزوال جاز، وقال مالك: يشترط التبييت أيضاً؛ لقوله علي: "لا صيام لمن لم يبيت الصيام من الليل ويعزم"، وقال الشافعي يشترط في صوم الفرض التبييت، وفي النفل يصح بنيته بعد الزوال، ولنا قوله تعالى: وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا حَتَّى يَتَبَيَّنَ لَكُمُ الْخَيْطُ الْأَبيضُ مِنَ الْخَيْطِ الْأَسْوَدِ مِنَ الْفَجْرِ ثُمَّ أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اللَّيْلِ ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۷) أباح الأكل والشرب إلى طلوع الفجر، ثم أمر بالصيام بعده بكلمة "ثم" وهي للتراخي، فتصير العزيمة بعد الفجر لا محالة، والحديث محمول على نفي الفضيلة كحديث التسمية، أو على غير المتعين من الصيام كالقضاء والكفارات، وقول أحمد كالشافعي في النفل، فإنه يجوز بنية بعد الزوال أيضاً؛ لأن مبناه على التخفيف، ولنا: أن النية إنما تصح إذا وقعت في الليل أو في أكثر النهار؛ لأن للأكثر حكم الكل. (فتح)
نصف النهار : الشرعي، وهو من طلوع الفجر إلى الضحوة الكبرى (ط) وبمطلق أي تصح بمطلق نية الصوم فقط. (ط) بنية النفل: [ أي يصح صوم رمضان والنذر المعين بنية النفل؛ لعدم المزاحم. (ط،ع)] بأن يقول: نويت أن أصوم غدا النفل، سواء علم أنه من رمضان أم لم يعلم، وفي رواية يكون عن النفل، وقال مالك: إن علم أنه يوم رمضان فنوى النفل لم يكن صائماً، وإن لم يعلم صح عن النفل، وقال الشافعي: لا يصح بنية النفل؛ لأن المأمور به صوم معلوم، فلابد من تعيينه؛ ليخرج عن العهدة كما في الصلاة ولنا أن رمضان لم يشرع فيه صوم آخر فكان متعيناً للفرض، والمتعين لا يحتاج إلى التعيين، فيصاب بمطلق النية وبنية غيره ومع الخطأ في الوصف إلا إذا وقعت النية من مريض ومسافر حيث يحتاج إلى التعين لعدم تعينه في حقهما، فلا يقع عن رمضان، بل عما نوى من نفل أو واجب، ويصح صوم رمضان بنية واجب آخر أيضاً، وكذلك النذر المعين بجميع . ذلك إلا بنية واجب آخر فيكون عما نوى، ولا يكون عن النذر. (عيني، فتح) وما بقي: وهو صوم قضاء رمضان والنذر المطلق والكفارات كلها وقضاء ما أفسده من نفل. (ط، ع) مبيتة: [من التبييت وهو فعل الشيء ليلا فلا يصح بنية من النهار. (ع، مسكين)] ليس المراد بالتبييت خصوص تقديم النية على طلوع الفجر، بل المراد عدم تأخيرها عن طلوع الفجر، أي لا يصح بنية من النهار؛ لأن الوجوب ثابت في الذمة والزمان غير متعين لها فلم يكن بد من التعيين ابتداء، ثم قال أصحابنا: تجب على الصائم النية لكل يوم، وقال مالك: يصح صوم جميع الشهر بنية واحدة؛ لأن الشهر عبادة واحدة كالصلاة، قلنا: فساد البعض لا يوجب فساد الكل في الصوم بخلاف الصلاة، فلهذا اشترطنا النية لصوم كل يوم؛ لأن صوم كل يوم عبادة على حدة؛ لتخلل وقت لا يصح الصوم فيه وهو الليل. (فتح)
يتأدي
صوم
And the voluntary (fast) with intention from the night until before noon, and with absolute intention and with intention of voluntary, and the rest is not permissible except with a specific intention made at night, due to the lack of determination of time. Al-Nafl: meaning, also the voluntary fast is valid, which is what increases over fard and wajib, whether it is sunnah or mandub. (T) From the night: meaning these fasts are valid with intention from the night. (T) Before noon: and it is said: if one fasts Ramadan with intention before midday, it is permissible. Malik said: making intention at night is also required, due to his statement: "No fast for one who does not make intention for the fast from the night and determine." Al-Shafi'i said: making intention at night is required for obligatory fasts, and for voluntary fasts it is valid with intention after midday. Our proof is His statement: "And eat and drink until the white thread becomes distinct to you from the black thread of dawn. Then complete the fast until the night" (2:187) — He permitted eating and drinking until dawn, then commanded fasting after it with the word "thumma" which indicates delay, so the determination becomes after dawn inevitably. The hadith is interpreted as negating virtue, like the hadith of tasmiyah, or regarding non-specific fasts like makeup and expiations. The statement of Ahmad is like al-Shafi'i regarding voluntary: it is permissible with intention after midday as well, because it is based on leniency. Our proof: intention is only valid if it occurs in the night or in most of the day, because the majority has the ruling of the whole. (Fath) Noon: the legal noon, which is from dawn to the greater forenoon. (T) And with absolute: meaning it is valid with absolute intention of fasting only. (T) With intention of voluntary: [meaning the fast of Ramadan and the specified vow are valid with intention of voluntary, due to absence of conflict (T, A)] as in saying: I intend to fast tomorrow as voluntary, whether he knows it is Ramadan or not. In one narration, it counts as voluntary. Malik said: if he knows it is Ramadan and intends voluntary, he is not fasting; if he does not know, it is valid as voluntary. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not valid with intention of voluntary, because what is commanded is a specific fast, so specification is necessary to discharge the duty, as in prayer. Our proof: in Ramadan, no other fast is prescribed, so it is determined for the obligatory; the determined does not need specification, so it is realized with absolute intention, with intention of something else, and with error in description, except if the intention comes from a sick or traveler, where specification is needed due to lack of determination in their case; then it does not count for Ramadan, but for what he intended of voluntary or obligatory. Also, the fast of Ramadan is valid with intention of another obligatory, and likewise the specified vow with all that, except with intention of another obligatory, then it counts for what he intended, not for the vow. (Ayni, Fath) And the remainder: which is the makeup of Ramadan, absolute vow, all expiations, and makeup of what he corrupted of voluntary. (T, A) Made at night: [from tabyeet, which is doing something at night; so it is not valid with intention from the day. (A, Miskeen)] It is not meant by tabyeet specifically the advance of intention before dawn, rather the intention is not to delay it past dawn; i.e., it is not valid with intention from the day, because the obligation is established in the conscience and the time is not determined for it, so specification is necessary from the start. Then our associates said: the fasting person must have intention for each day. Malik said: the fast of the entire month is valid with one intention, because the month is a single act of worship like prayer. We said: the invalidity of part does not necessitate invalidity of the whole in fasting, unlike prayer. For this reason, we require intention for each day's fast, because each day's fast is a separate act of worship, since a time intervenes in which fasting is not valid, and that is the night. (Fath) One performs the fast.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the intention (niyyah) for fasting, distinguishing between voluntary (nafl) and obligatory fasts. It begins by stating that voluntary fasts are valid with intention made from the night until before noon (i.e., before half of the day, which is defined as the legal noon from dawn to the forenoon). Moreover, for Ramadan and the specified vow, intention can be absolute (just intending to fast) or even with the intention of nafl; the fast still counts as Ramadan or the vow because no other fast competes. For other obligatory fasts (like makeup, expiations, etc.), a specific intention (e.g., 'I intend to make up Ramadan') must be made at night (mubayyitah). The commentary then presents inter-madhhab disputes: Malik requires intention at night for all fasts, citing a hadith; Shafi'i requires it for obligatory fasts but allows intention after noon for voluntary fasts. The Hanafi response is based on the Quranic verse (2:187): the word 'thumma' (then) implies delay, so the firm resolve (azimah) can be after dawn. Additionally, they argue that intention is valid if made in the night or most of the day. The passage also addresses the case of a sick person or traveler: they need to specify because Ramadan is not determined for them (they have the option not to fast). Finally, the Hanafis require intention for each day separately, unlike Malik who permits a single intention for the whole month, because each day's fast is an independent act of worship separated by the night when fasting is not valid.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform an act of worship, distinguishing it from habit. It is a condition for the validity of fasting.
التبييت (al-tabyeet): Making an intention at night, i.e., before dawn. Required for all obligatory fasts except Ramadan and specified vow (which can be intended up to before noon with absolute or nafl intention).
النفل (al-nafl): Voluntary fasts, which are recommended but not obligatory. They can be intended up to before noon.
المتعين (al-muta'ayyin): A specific act that is determined by time, such that no other act can replace it. Ramadan fasting is muta'ayyin because only the Ramadan fast can be performed on that day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person intends a voluntary (nafl) fast on a day that is actually part of Ramadan, but they are unaware it is Ramadan?
A) The fast counts as the voluntary fast, and they must make up Ramadan later.
B) The fast counts as Ramadan, and the voluntary intention is ineffective.
C) The fast is invalid and must be made up as Ramadan.
D) It depends on whether they intended at night or during the day.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the intention for fasting depends on whether the fast is determined by time: determined acts like Ramadan do not require specification, while non-determined obligations require specific intention at night.

Confirmation of Ramadan and Fasting on the Day of Doubt
Rulings on establishing Ramadan and the various intentions for fasting on the day of doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six scenarios of intention for fasting on the day of doubt and identify the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Ramadan is established by sighting or completing Sha'ban; fasting the day of doubt as Ramadan is makruh tahrimi.

This lesson explores how Ramadan is confirmed and the complex rulings on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk). The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on permissible and impermissible intentions for fasting on this ambiguous day, balancing the prophetic command and scholarly consensus. Understanding these scenarios is crucial for correctly observing the fast and avoiding prohibited actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof for establishing Ramadan
Identify the hadith cited ("Fast upon seeing it...") and how it establishes the rule: Ramadan begins only by sighting or completing 30 days of Sha'ban. Note the consensus and the obligation to seek the crescent on the 29th.
2
Define yawm al-shakk (day of doubt)
Grasp when doubt occurs: the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent is obscured, causing equal probability it is Sha'ban or Ramadan. Recognize the general rule: fasting this day as Ramadan is prohibitively disliked.
Sha'ban may be 29 or 30 days; this uncertainty triggers the day of doubt.
3
Analyze the six scenarios of intention
Work through each of the six scenarios described in the text. For each, identify: (1) the type of intention, (2) the ruling (makruh tahrimi/tanzihi, permissible, or invalid), and (3) the consequence if it later becomes clear that the day was Ramadan or Sha'ban.
Use the text's numbering: first (intending Ramadan), second (other obligatory), third (supererogatory), fourth (hesitation in origin), fifth (hesitation in description), sixth (conditional on Ramadan/nafl).
4
Identify the mufta bihi (al-mukhtar)
Recognize that the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) is for the mufti to fast himself and instruct the public to wait until zawal (noon) and then break fast if still in doubt. Note the rejection of the spurious hadith about fasting the day of doubt.
This is a practical fatwa for those in authority.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Compare with the Shafi'i position: they only dislike fasting on the day of doubt if it does not accord with one's habit. Understand that the difference arises from variant prioritizing of hadith and principles.
This highlights how madhhab-specific rules emerge from methodological differences.

ويثبت رمضان برؤية هلاله، أو بعد شعبان ثلاثين يوما، ولا يصام يوم الشك إلا تطوعا، ويكره غيره. ويثبت رمضان إلخ لقوله عليه: صوموا لرؤيته وأفطروا لرؤيته، فإن غم الهلال عليكم فأكملوا عدة شعبان ثلاثين يوما، وهذا بالإجماع، ويجب التماس الهلال في التاسع والعشرين من شعبان؛ لأن الشهر قد يكون تسعة وعشرين يوما كما ورد في الحديث فيجب طلبه لإقامة الواجب، وفي قوله: "بروية هلاله" إيماء إلى أن رمضان لا يلزم بقول أهل النجوم وإن كانوا عدولا هو الصحيح. (فتح) هلاله لقوله عليها: "صوموا لرؤيته" الحديث. (ع) أو بعد شعبان: أي بتكميل عدد شهر شعبان إذا غم الهلال؛ لقوله عليه: " فإن غم الهلال عليكم فأكملوا عدة شعبان ثلاثين يوما". (فتح) ولا يصام يوم الشك : [وهو يوم ثلاثين من شعبان إذا وقع الشك أنه منه أو من رمضان. (ط)] الشك ما استوى فيه طرف الإدراك من النفي والإثبات، وذا بأن غم هلال رمضان في اليوم التاسع والعشرين من شعبان فوقع الشك في اليوم الثلاثين أنه شعبان أو من رمضان وهذه المسألة على وجوه: أحدها: أن ينوي صوم رمضان وهو مكروه تحريما؛ لحديث: "لا تقدموا لرمضان بصوم يوم أو يومين، ثم إن ظهر أن اليوم من رمضان يجزئه؛ لأنه شهد الشهر وصامه، وإن ظهر أنه من شعبان كان تطوعا، وإن أفطر لم يقضه؛ لأنه ظان، والثاني: أن ينوي عن واجب آخر وهو مكروه أيضا تنزيها، ثم إن ظهر أنه من رمضان يجزئه، وإن ظهر أنه من شعبان فقد قيل: يكون تطوعا؛ لأنه منهي عنه، فلا يتأدى به الكامل من الواجب، وقيل: أجزأه عن الذي نواه، وهو الأصح، والثالث: أن ينوي التطوع، وهو غير مكروه، وعند البعض مكروه، ويقول: إنه ورد في الحديث: "من صام يوم الشك فقد أبا القاسم"، ولا أصل لهذا الحديث. وقال الشافعي: ابتداء يكره بأن لم يوافق عادة له والمختار أن يصوم المفتي بنفسه، ويفتي العامة بالانتظار إلى وقت الزوال، ثم يأمر بالإفطار، والرابع: أن يتردد في أصل النية بأن ينوي أن يصوم غدا إن كان من رمضان ولا يصوم إن كان من شعبان، وفي هذا الوجه لا يكون صائما؛ لعدم الجزم في العزيمة، وكذا إذا نوى إن لم أجد غذاء فأنا صائم من وإلا فمفطر، وكذلك لو نوى إن لم أجد سحورا فمفطر، وإلا فصائم والخامس أن يتردد في وصف النية بأن ينوي إن كان غداً من رمضان يصوم عنه، وإن كان من شعبان فمن واجب آخر، وهذا مكروه؛ لتردده بين أمرين مكروهين، ثم إن ظهر أنه من رمضان أجزأه؛ لوجود الجزم في أصل النية، وإن ظهر أنه من شعبان لا يجزئه عن واجب آخر؛ لعدم الجزم به ويكون تطوعا غير مضمون بالقضاء، والسادس: أن ينوي عن رمضان إن كان غداً منه، وعن التطوع إن كان من شعبان، وهذا مكروه أيضا أي تنزيها ثم إن ظهر أنه من رمضان أجزأه عنه، وإن ظهر أنه من شعبان جاز عن النفل (مسكين، فتح أو الفطر : أي هلال الفطر وشهد عند القاضي. (ط)
And Ramadan is established by sighting its crescent, or after Sha'ban [completing] thirty days. And the day of doubt is not fasted except as a supererogatory [fast], and other than it is disliked. Ramadan is established etc. due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Fast upon seeing it and break fast upon seeing it, and if the crescent is concealed from you, then complete the count of Sha'ban as thirty days.' This is by consensus. And it is obligatory to seek the crescent on the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban because the month may be twenty-nine days as reported in the hadith, so it is obligatory to seek it to perform the obligatory. And in his saying 'by sighting its crescent' there is an indication that Ramadan is not binding by the statement of the astrologers, even if they are upright; this is the correct view. (Fath) Its crescent due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Fast upon seeing it' the hadith. (A) Or after Sha'ban: i.e. by completing the number of the month of Sha'ban if the crescent is concealed; due to his saying: 'If the crescent is concealed from you, complete the count of Sha'ban as thirty days.' (Fath) And the day of doubt is not fasted: [it is the thirtieth of Sha'ban when doubt occurs whether it is from it or from Ramadan. (T)] Doubt is when the two sides of perception are equal in negation and affirmation. This occurs when the crescent of Ramadan is obscured on the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban, so doubt occurs on the thirtieth day whether it is Sha'ban or Ramadan. This issue has several aspects: First: that he intends the fast of Ramadan, and this is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahriman) due to the hadith: 'Do not precede Ramadan by fasting a day or two.' Then if it appears that the day is from Ramadan, it suffices him because he witnessed the month and fasted it. If it appears that it is from Sha'ban, it becomes a supererogatory fast. If he broke his fast, he does not make it up because he was uncertain. Second: that he intends for another obligatory [fast], and this is also disliked (makruh tanzihan). Then if it appears that it is from Ramadan, it suffices him. If it appears that it is from Sha'ban, it is said: it becomes supererogatory because it is prohibited, so the complete obligatory is not performed by it; and it is said: it suffices for what he intended, and this is the more correct opinion. Third: that he intends supererogatory, and this is not disliked, but according to some it is disliked, and they say that a hadith states: 'Whoever fasts the day of doubt has [acted against] Abu al-Qasim' – but this hadith has no basis. And al-Shafi'i said: it is initially disliked if it does not agree with his habit. The chosen view is that the mufti should fast himself and give fatwa to the public to wait until the time of zawal, then command to break fast. Fourth: that he hesitates in the origin of the intention, such that he intends to fast tomorrow if it is from Ramadan but not to fast if it is from Sha'ban. In this case he is not fasting due to lack of determination in resolve. Similarly if he intends: 'If I do not find food, then I am fasting from ... otherwise I am breaking fast', and likewise if he intends: 'If I do not find suhur, then I am breaking fast, otherwise I am fasting.' Fifth: that he hesitates in the description of the intention, such that he intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, I fast for it; if it is from Sha'ban, then for another obligatory.' This is disliked due to his hesitation between two disliked matters. Then if it appears that it is from Ramadan, it suffices him due to the presence of determination in the origin of intention. If it appears that it is from Sha'ban, it does not suffice for another obligatory due to lack of determination, and it becomes a supererogatory fast not guaranteed by make-up. Sixth: that he intends for Ramadan if tomorrow is from it, and for supererogatory if it is from Sha'ban. This is also disliked, i.e., tanzihan. Then if it appears that it is from Ramadan, it suffices him for it; if it appears that it is from Sha'ban, it is valid as a supererogatory fast. (Miskeen, Fath) Or al-fitr: i.e., the crescent of Fitr, and he testifies before the judge. (T)
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with its commentary covers the confirmation of Ramadan and the rulings on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk). The matn states that Ramadan is established by sighting the crescent or completing 30 days of Sha'ban. Fasting on the day of doubt (the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent is obscured) is only permissible as a supererogatory fast; otherwise it is disliked. The commentary elaborates: the hadith 'Fast upon seeing it' is the proof, and it is obligatory to seek the crescent on the 29th. The commentary then details six scenarios of intention for the day of doubt, each with its ruling. The first: intending Ramadan is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahriman) due to the hadith prohibiting anticipation. If it turns out to be Ramadan, the fast is valid; if Sha'ban, it counts as supererogatory. The second: intending another obligatory fast is also disliked (makruh tanzihan), and if it's Sha'ban, it's debated whether it suffices for the intended obligation or becomes nafl. The author notes the more correct opinion is that it suffices for what was intended. The third: intending supererogatory is not disliked (though some say it is). The fourth and fifth involve hesitation in intention—these invalidate the fast due to lack of resolve. The sixth: conditional intention (if Ramadan then for Ramadan, if Sha'ban then for nafl) is disliked but valid if it turns out to be Ramadan. The commentary also mentions the Shafi'i view and notes that the mufti should fast himself but instruct the public to wait until zawal.

Key Terms
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The day of doubt: the 30th of Sha'ban when the crescent is not visible, causing doubt whether it is the last day of Sha'ban or the first of Ramadan.
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen opinion; the relied-upon view in the school. Here it refers to the view that the mufti should fast on the day of doubt and instruct the public to wait until zawal.
مكروه تحريما (makruh tahriman): Prohibitively disliked; an action that is closer to haram and should be avoided. In the Hanafi school, it is sinful to commit such an act, but it is not invalid per se.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone on the day of doubt intends: 'If tomorrow is from Ramadan, I fast for Ramadan; if from Sha'ban, I fast as supererogatory,' what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The fast is invalid because the intention is conditional.
B) The fast is makruh tahrimi and if it turns out to be Ramadan, it is valid; if Sha'ban, it is valid as supererogatory.
C) The fast is valid only if it is actually Ramadan.
D) The fast is invalid under all circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits fasting on the day of doubt as supererogatory but prohibits intending it as Ramadan; six distinct scenarios of intention each carry their own ruling and validity conditions.

Testimony for Sighting the Crescent of Ramadan and Eid
Ruling on testimony for crescent sighting with proof and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for accepting testimony for the crescent of Ramadan and Eid, explain the distinction between khabar and shahada, and compare the Hanafi ruling with opposing schools.

Core Idea
For Ramadan, one just report suffices; for Eid, two free men or one man and two women testify.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on accepting testimony for the sighting of the crescent moon to begin Ramadan and Eid al-Fitr. The passage distinguishes between a mere report (khabar) and formal testimony (shahada), leading to different requirements for each occasion. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi differences and the arguments of other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
Recognize that the passage treats Ramadan and Eid al-Fitr separately. Understand the different rulings for each.
Ramadan: acceptance of one just person. Eid: stricter requirement.
2
Understand the distinction between khabar and shahada
The Hanafi school considers this a report (khabar) for Ramadan, not a judicial testimony, so fewer conditions apply. For Eid, it is considered testimony and thus requires more conditions.
No claim or formal wording needed for khabar.
3
Examine the conditions for acceptance
Note the specific conditions: justice ('adl), condition of the sky (cloudy vs clear), number, gender, and freedom status.
For clear sky, a large crowd is needed.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Compare the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the confirmation of Eid and the obligation to fast when testimony is rejected.
The text prefers the majority view of the two sheikhs.
5
Compare with other schools
Identify the positions of Shafi'i and Maliki, and understand the Hanafi response using the Bedouin hadith and the concept of religious reports.
Thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in confirming Ramadan.

And on a cloudy night, the report of a just person is accepted, even if a slave or a woman, after the judge has rejected it, any day without expiation for Ramadan, and two freemen or one freeman and two freewomen for Eid. That is, the report of two freemen or one freeman is accepted, i.e., if the informant... and his statement 'he fasted': because he did not hear the judge's statement due to his solitary sighting. (Ayn) 'He fasts' meaning it is obligatory because he has witnessed the month, and for the crescent of Eid as a precaution. (Ayn) Then if he breaks his fast, he only makes it up without expiation, and it is said he makes it up and expiates, but the first is correct. The reason for no expiation is that the judge rejected his testimony by a legal proof, which is the suspicion of error, thus creating doubt. Expiation is repelled by doubts, contrary to al-Shafi'i who requires expiation if the break is by intercourse because Ramadan is certain for him, and doubt from another does not negate his certainty. Our evidence: what he saw could be a mirage and not a crescent, so it is not certainty in his case; and the judge's rejection of his testimony creates a doubt that repels expiation because expiation is considered a penalty, and the meaning of penalty is predominant. In other expiations, the meanings of worship and penalty combine, and worship is predominant. (Fath) 'And before a cloud' etc.: i.e., without a claim, without saying 'I testify', without a ruling and a court session, because it is a report, not a testimony, whether he explains the manner of sighting or not. The testimony of one person is accepted from another, like slave and woman even against their like. And because in matters of religion, the word of a single person is accepted. (Miskin, Fath) By consensus. 'Due to a cloud': i.e., clouds, dust or the like in the sky. (Ayn) 'Report of a just person': i.e., his report is accepted absolutely, but the report of a wicked person is not accepted. (Ta) 'Even if a slave': i.e., a bondsman, and he chose this expression to include the mukatab, mudabbar, and one partly freed; similarly his saying 'or a female' to include the slave girl and others. (Ayn) 'For Ramadan' relates to his saying 'accepted' i.e., accepted for the month of Ramadan and for its fasting. (Ta, Ayn) 'Two freemen or one freeman and two freewomen': i.e., the testimony of two freemen or one freeman and two freewomen is accepted for the crescent of Eid as in other legal matters because it involves the benefit of the servants, which is breaking the fast. Therefore, justice, freedom, number, and the expression of testimony are required, but a claim is not required, like the emancipation of a slave girl and the divorce of a free woman. The testimony of one flogged for slander is not accepted in it because it is a testimony. Symbol of realities: 137/1. And his statement: 'otherwise a great multitude for both': i.e., if there is no cloud in the sky, the testimony of a large crowd whose report yields knowledge is not accepted for the crescent of Ramadan and Eid. Then it is said the limit of majority is the people of the locality, and from Abu Yusuf: fifty men like in qasama, and from Muhammad: =

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues the discussion on establishing the month, focusing on the acceptance of testimony for sighting the crescent. The author explains that for Ramadan, the report of a single just person (even a slave or woman) is accepted if there is an excuse like cloudiness. For Eid al-Fitr, the testimony of two freemen or one freeman and two freewomen is required. The text then discusses the case of a person whose testimony is rejected by the judge: if he then breaks his fast, he only has to make it up without expiation, contrary to the Shafi'i view. The reasoning is that the judge's rejection creates a doubt that precludes expiation, as expiation is repelled by doubts. The commentator (likely from al-Fath) elaborates on the conditions: no claim, no formal testimony wording, no ruling needed; it is a report. The testimony of a slave and a woman is accepted. There is a discussion on the acceptance of testimony from a person flogged for slander: Abu Hanifa holds that it is not accepted even after repentance. The passage also mentions that if there is no cloud and many people claim to have seen the crescent, a large number whose report yields certain knowledge is required; some say the entire neighborhood, and Abu Yusuf said fifty men like in qasama. The text ends with 'and from Muhammad' incomplete.

Key Terms
خبر عدل (khabar 'adl): A report from a just (trustworthy) person; accepted for establishing the start of Ramadan without formal testimony requirements.
علة ('illa): An obstacle in the sky such as clouds or dust that prevents clear sighting.
المثنى (al-muthanna): Two witnesses; required by Maliki school for crescent sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to Kanz al-Daqa'iq, for establishing the crescent of Eid al-Fitr, the testimony of which of the following is accepted?
A) One just slave
B) Two just freemen or one freeman and two freewomen
C) One just woman
D) Any Muslim regardless of justice

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires only one just report for Ramadan (when cloudy) but demands two free men or one free man and two women for Eid.

Accepting a Single Witness for Ramadan and the Kaffarah Dispute
Ruling on accepting a single witness and the kaffarah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a single just witness for Ramadan due to cloud cover and explain why no kaffarah is required if the judge rejects the testimony and the person breaks fast.

Core Idea
A single just witness suffices for Ramadan with clouds; if judge rejects, no expiation because doubt averts punishment.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi ruling: when the sky is overcast, the testimony of one just person is enough to establish Ramadan. But what happens if the judge rejects that testimony and the person breaks his fast? The Hanafi school holds that only makeup is required, not expiation, due to legal doubt. We will trace the proof from the hadith and analyze the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is establishing the start of Ramadan via moon sighting. The ruling: one just witness is accepted if there is an excuse (clouds).
2
Analyze the consequence of rejection
If the judge rejects the witness's testimony on valid grounds and the person then breaks his fast, the Hanafi school rules only qada (makeup) is required, not kaffarah.
Due to the legal doubt (shubha) created by the judge's rejection.
3
Understand the principle of shubha averting kaffarah
Kaffarah is treated like a punishment and doubts avert it. The judge's rejection introduces doubt about the certainty of Ramadan for that person.
Contrasts with other expiations where worship predominates.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kaffarah is required because Ramadan is certain for the person and the doubt of another does not negate his certainty.
Hanafi response: the sighting may be an illusion, so it is not true certainty.
5
Apply to a practical scenario
A Hanafi follower reports the moon, the judge rejects, and he breaks his fast. He only needs to make up one day. No kaffarah.

Book of Fasting. Establishment of the month of Ramadan. And it is reported his statement: 'he fasted'. Then if he breaks his fast, he only makes it up. And is accepted due to an excuse the news of a just person, even if a slave or a female, i.e., after the judge rejected [the testimony], he makes up that day without expiation for Ramadan. (Ayni) Then if he breaks his fast etc. i.e., by intercourse, he only makes up without expiation, and it was said: he makes up and expiates, and the correct is the first. The reason for no expiation is that the judge rejected his testimony by a legal proof, which is suspicion of error, thus it created a doubt. And this expiation is averted by doubts, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he holds expiation obligatory if he breaks fast by intercourse because Ramadan is certain for him and the doubt of another does not negate his certainty. Our proof: that what he saw could be an illusion and not a moon, so it is not certainty for him, and with the judge's rejection of his testimony there is a doubt that averts expiation because it has been attached to punishments in that the meaning of punishment is predominant in it. He said: 'Do you testify that there is no god but Allah?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Do you testify that Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'O Bilal! Announce to the people that they should fast tomorrow.' And because in matters of religious reporting (akhbar al-diyana), the statement of one is accepted. (Miskeen, Fath) Unanimously. Due to an excuse: due to clouds, dust, or the like in the sky. (Ayni) News of a just person i.e., his news is accepted absolutely, and the news of a corrupt person is not accepted (Tahawi). Even if a slave i.e., a non-free person, and he chose this wording to include the mukatab, mudabbar, and one partially freed. Similarly his saying 'or a female' to include a slave woman and others. (Ayni) For Ramadan is related to 'accepted' i.e., accepted for the month of Ramadan and for its fast. (Tahawi, Ayni) Two free men or one free man and two free women: i.e., the testimony of two free men or one free man and two free women is accepted for the crescent of Eid al-Fitr as in other legal matters, because it benefits the people, which is breaking fast. Therefore, integrity, freedom, number, and the wording of testimony are stipulated, but a claim is not required like manumission of a slave woman and divorce of a free woman. And the testimony of one subject to hadd of slander is not accepted because it is a testimony. Ramz al-Haqaiq: 137/1.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings for establishing the month of Ramadan according to the Hanafi school. It begins by stating that if a single just person reports seeing the new moon due to cloud cover (b'illa), his report is accepted for Ramadan, even if he is a slave or female. If after his testimony is rejected by the judge he breaks his fast, he only needs to make up that day and does not owe kaffarah. The commentary explains that this is because the rejection creates a legal doubt (shubha) that averts the kaffarah, which is treated like a punishment. The Shafi'i school holds that kaffarah is still required because Ramadan is certain for him, but the Hanafi response is that his own sighting is not certain (it could be an illusion), and the judge's rejection creates a doubt. The hadith of the Bedouin is cited as proof that a single witness suffices for Ramadan when there is an excuse. The passage then discusses conditions: the witness must be just ('adl), not a known sinner; the testimony is accepted even without a formal legal claim or the wording 'I testify'; a slave and a female are accepted; if the witness is a secluded slave girl, she must go out to testify without her master's permission. For Eid al-Fitr, however, two free men or one free man and two free women are required, as in other legal matters, because it benefits the people by allowing them to break fast. The number requirement is for the sake of caution. The passage also notes that a person convicted of false slander (qadhf) is not accepted as a witness, even after repentance, according to Abu Hanifa, though this is a matter of intra-Hanafi difference.

Key Terms
خبر عدل (khabar 'adl): News reported by a just person (one who refrains from major sins and does not persist in minor sins).
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity in evidence that prevents the application of a legal penalty or expiation.
كفارة (kaffarah): An expiatory act (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting two months, feeding sixty poor) required for certain violations like breaking a Ramadan fast with intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a single just witness reports the Ramadan moon, but the judge rejects his testimony due to suspicion of error, and the witness then breaks his fast with intercourse, what is required?
A) Qada only
B) Qada and kaffarah
C) Only kaffarah
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
When the judge rejects a single witness's testimony for Ramadan due to suspicion, any subsequent fast-breaking only requires makeup, not expiation, because legal doubt averts kaffarah.

Conditions for Witnesses: Ramadan vs. Eid and the Qadhf Dispute
Conditions for witnesses and the qadhf dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions for a valid witness for Ramadan and for Eid according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding a witness who was convicted of slander (qadhf).

Core Idea
One just witness suffices for Ramadan without formalities, but Eid requires two free men or a man and two women due to public benefit; a qadhf convict's testimony is disputed within the school.

In this lesson, we explore the specific conditions for accepting moon sighting witnesses. For Ramadan, the Hanafi school is lenient: one just person, even a slave or female, is enough, and no court formalities are needed. For Eid al-Fitr, stricter rules apply because it benefits the entire community. We also examine an intra-Hanafi disagreement: is the testimony of a person convicted of slander (qadhf) accepted after repentance? Abu Hanifa rejects it, while the Sahibayn accept it. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying the rulings on sighting the moon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qualifications for a Ramadan witness
The witness must be just ('adil). A slave or female is accepted. No formal claim or wording 'I testify' is required. The witness does not need to explain how he sighted the moon.
This is because it is religious reporting (khabar), not court testimony (shahada).
2
Note the exception: Eid al-Fitr
For the moon of Eid, two free male witnesses or one free male and two free females are required. This is like other legal matters because it involves benefit to the people (breaking fast).
Number, freedom, and the wording 'I testify' are required.
3
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith of the Bedouin shows the Prophet accepted a single witness's report without requiring any of these formalities for Ramadan.
This is the primary evidence.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the qadhf convict
Abu Hanifa does not accept the testimony of someone who was convicted of false slander (qadhf), even after repentance, because his testimony is considered tainted. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) accept it absolutely.
The text notes that this is because the conviction creates a shadow of doubt.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a slave woman reports seeing the Ramadan moon, her report is accepted. If a man convicted of qadhf reports the Eid moon, according to Abu Hanifa it is rejected; according to Sahibayn, accepted.

Moon Sighting in Clear Weather: Requirement of a Large Crowd
Ruling on moon sighting without excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for establishing the crescent when the sky is clear, explain the concept of jami' athim, and identify the mufta bihi view from the intra-Hanafi opinions.

Core Idea
When the sky is clear, a large crowd (jami' athim) is required; the number is left to the imam's discretion.

The previous lesson covered the case when there is an excuse (clouds) – a single witness suffices. Now we turn to the opposite: a clear sky. Here the Hanafi school demands a higher level of certainty to avoid error. The passage presents various opinions on what constitutes 'a large crowd', and then settles on the view that the imam decides. Understanding this principle is crucial for any community organising moon sighting on clear nights.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the premise
The text begins with 'And otherwise' (wa illa) – meaning if there is no excuse (cloud, smoke, etc.) in the sky. Establish that this condition triggers a different ruling.
The excuse can be anything that obstructs clear sighting.
2
2. Note the requirement
When the sky is clear, a 'large crowd' (jami' athim) is required for both Ramadan and Eid. This means the news must be widespread (tawatur) from all sides.
The same applies to Eid al-Adha, as stated: 'al-Adha ka al-Fitr'.
3
3. Understand what does not work
If only one person comes from outside the city, he is not accepted (zahir al-riwaya). Similarly if the witness is on a high place inside the city – a single witness is insufficient.
This is the default ruling; exceptions follow.
4
4. Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Several opinions exist: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that two men or a man and two women suffice (like Eid); Khalaf ibn Ayyub said 500 in Balkh is few; Abu Hafs said 1000; Muhammad al-Shaybani said the number is at the imam's discretion.
The commentator declares Muhammad's view as the most correct (al-ashahh).
5
5. Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i accepts the testimony of a single person even in clear weather. The Hanafi rejection is based on ihtiyat (caution) to avoid error.
Practical consequence: Hanafi communities require multiple witnesses; Shafi'i ones suffice with one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا فجمع عظيم لهما، والأضحى كالفطر ، ولا عبرة لاختلاف المطالع. أي لرمضان والفطر أي هلاله وبقية الشهور حتى يتواتر الخبر من كل جانب، فلو جاء واحد من خارج المصر فظاهر الرواية أن لا يقبل، وكذا إذا كان على مكان مرتفع في المصر ، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه تقبل شهادة رجلين ورجل وامرأتين، وعن خلف ابن أيوب قال: خمس مائة ببلخ قليل، وعن أبي حفص الكبير أنه ألفاً، يعتبر وعن محمد أنه قال: القلة والكثرة على رأي الأمام وهو الأصح وقال الشافعي تقبل شهادة الواحد. (مسكين) وإلا : أي وإن لم يكن بالسماء علة كالفطر أي كهلال الفطر في الثبوت بشهادة حرين أو حر وحرتين وهو الأصح. (ط، ع)
And otherwise, a large crowd for both [Ramadan and Eid], and Eid al-Adha is like al-Fitr, and there is no consideration of differing rising places. i.e., for Ramadan and al-Fitr, i.e., its moon and the rest of the months, until the news becomes widespread from every side. So if one person comes from outside the city, in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) he is not accepted. Similarly if he is on a high place in the city. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the testimony of two men or one man and two women is accepted. And from Khalaf ibn Ayyub: five hundred in Balkh is few. And from Abu Hafs al-Kabir: a thousand is to be considered. And from Muhammad that he said: fewness and manyness are according to the opinion of the imam, and that is the most correct. And al-Shafi'i said: the testimony of one is accepted. (Miskeen) And otherwise: i.e., if there is no excuse in the sky, it is like al-Fitr i.e., like the moon of al-Fitr in being established by the testimony of two free men or one free man and two free women, and that is the most correct. (Tahawi, Ayni)
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when there is no cloud cover or similar excuse (i.e., a clear sky). In that case, a large crowd (jami' athim) is required to establish the crescent for both Ramadan and Eid. The commentary clarifies that this means the news must be widespread (tawatur) from all sides. If only one person comes from outside the city, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) he is not accepted. Similarly if the witness is on a high place within the city. There are various opinions on the number constituting a 'large crowd'. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that two men or one man and two women suffice (similar to Eid requirement). Khalaf ibn Ayyub said five hundred men in Balkh is few; Abu Hafs al-Kabir said a thousand is the threshold. Muhammad al-Shaybani said the number is left to the imam's discretion, which is the most correct view (al-ashahh). The Shafi'i school, by contrast, accepts the testimony of a single person even without an excuse. The preferred Hanafi view (al-ashahh) from the text is that when there is no cloud, the matter is like Eid: requiring two free men or a man and two women, but the crowd requirement is for additional certainty. The passage also notes that Eid al-Adha follows the same rule as Eid al-Fitr.

Key Terms
جمع عظيم (jami' athim): A large gathering of people whose report produces certain knowledge (tawatur).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent or well-known narration from the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as compiled in the six core books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two men or a man and two women
B) Five hundred men
C) One thousand men
D) The number is left to the imam's discretion

Key Takeaway
In clear weather, the Hanafi school requires a widespread report (jami' athim) to establish the crescent; the exact number is at the imam's discretion.

Moonsighting Across Horizons: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on moonsighting across horizons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on differing matali', distinguish it from the intra-school opposing view, and apply the ruling to daytime sighting scenarios.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, once the moon is sighted anywhere, it is obligatory for all Muslims to fast, regardless of distance—precaution trumps astronomical differences.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on whether moonsighting in one city obligates fasting in another city with a different horizon. The passage presents the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) that differing rising places have no legal effect, along with an intra-school dispute. It also addresses the sub-issue of daytime sightings and the divergent opinions of the early imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Find the ruling that 'there is no consideration of differing rising places.' This is the core legal statement from the text.
The text also says 'wa 'alayhi al-fatwa'—this is the fatwa position.
2
Note the intra-school dispute
Some later Hanafi scholars said it is not binding. Understand the two positions and which is relied upon.
The first view is considered 'asahh' (most correct) due to ihtiyat (precaution).
3
Understand the qiyas employed
The ruling is based on qiyas: analogy to the unity of the lunar month during the Prophet's time, where sighting in one place obligated all.
The illa (effective cause) is the need for unity and avoidance of disagreement.
4
Analyze the daytime sighting sub-issue
The text then discusses seeing the moon during the day. Identify the relied-upon view and how it differs from Abu Yusuf and a narration from Abu Hanifa.
The mufta bihi is that daytime sightings are always for the next night, regardless of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا عبرة لاختلاف المطالع. أي إذا رأى الهلال أهل بلدة يلزم ذلك أهل بلدة أخرى في ظاهر الرواية مطلقاً سواء كان بين البلدتين تفاوت أو لا، وعليه الفتوى وقال بعضهم: لا يلزم وهو الأشبه وإن كان الأول هو الأصح للاحتياط؛ لأن انفصال الهلال من شعاع الشمس يختلف باختلاف الأقطار كما في دخول الوقت وخروجه، حتى إذا زالت الشمس في الشرق لا يلزم منه أن تزول في الغرب، وكذا طلوع الفجر وغروب الشمس، بل كلما تحركت الشمس درجة فتلك طلوع فجر لقوم وطلوع شمس لآخرين وغروب لبعض ونصف ليل لآخرين، وهذا مثبت في علم الأفلاك والهيئة رمز الحقائق: (۱۳۷۱، ۱۳۸] وقال بعضهم: إذا لم يكن بين بلدتين تفاوت لا تختلف المطالع، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت أي مسيرة شهر فصاعداً تختلف المطالع، ولا يلزم حكم إحدى البلدتين بالبلدة الأخرى، ولا عبرة أيضاً برؤية الهلال نهاراً قبل الزوال وبعده وهو الليلة المستقبلة، وعند أبي يوسف: إذا كان قبل الزوال فهو الليلة الماضية، فيحكم بوجوب الفطر، وعن أبي حنيفة في رواية إن كان مجراه أمام الشمس تتلوه، فهو من الليلة الماضية، فيحكم بوجوب الفطر، وإن كان مجراه خلف الشمس فهو من الليلة المستقبلة. (مسكين، عيني) المطالع بل إذا ثبت في مصر لزم سائر الناس، فيلزم أهل المشرق برؤية أهل المغرب وعليه الفتوى، ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً مطلقاً. (ط،ع)
And there is no consideration of differing rising places. i.e., if the inhabitants of a town see the moon, it becomes binding on the inhabitants of another town, in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) absolutely, whether there is a difference between the two towns or not, and upon this is the fatwa. And some said: it is not binding, and that is more fitting (ashbah), although the first is the most correct (asahh) due to precaution. Because the separation of the moon from the sun's rays differs according to different regions, as in the entry and exit of times. So much so that if the sun declines in the east, it does not necessitate its decline in the west. Similarly with the break of dawn and sunset. Rather, whenever the sun moves a degree, there is dawn for some people, sunrise for others, sunset for some, and midnight for others. This is established in the science of astronomy and celestial bodies. Ramz al-Haqaiq: (1371, 138). And some said: if there is no difference between the two towns, the rising places do not differ, but if there is a difference, i.e., a month's journey or more, then the rising places differ, and the ruling of one town does not bind the other. And there is also no consideration of seeing the moon during the day before or after noon; it is for the next night. According to Abu Yusuf: if it is before noon, it is for the past night, so the ruling of obligatory breaking of fast (iftar) applies. And from Abu Hanifa in one narration: if its course is ahead of the sun following it, it is from the past night, so the ruling of obligatory breaking of fast applies; if its course is behind the sun, it is from the next night. (Miskeen, Ayni) The rising places: rather, if it is established in one city, it binds all people, so the people of the east are bound by the sighting of the people of the west, and upon this is the fatwa. And there is no consideration of seeing the moon during the day absolutely. (Tahawi, Ayni)
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the sighting of the moon in one city obligates fasting in another city with a different horizon. The relied-upon view (al-mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), is that the sighting in one city is binding on all other cities regardless of the distance between them. This is the position adopted for fatwa (wa 'alayhi al-fatwa). Some later scholars held the opposite view (that it is not binding), which they considered more fitting (ashbah), but the first is considered more correct (asahh) due to the principle of precaution (ihtiyat). The reasoning is based on the unity of the lunar month and the need for uniformity among Muslims. The commentary also discusses the astronomical reality that the moon's separation from the sun varies by region, like the timings of dawn and sunset. But despite that, the school's relied-upon view sticks to the general obligation. Another sub-issue is seeing the moon during the day. The text says such a sighting is always considered for the next night (i.e., the coming night), not the past night. However, Abu Yusuf holds that if seen before noon, it is for the past night, meaning that the day in question is considered the first of the month, so fasting or breaking fast may be required. Abu Hanifa, in one narration, distinguishes based on whether the moon's course is ahead of or behind the sun. But the relied-upon view is the absolute statement that there is no consideration for daytime sightings for the past night.

Key Terms
المطالع (al-matali'): The rising places of the moon; different longitudes and latitudes causing different local horizon positions.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known transmitted position from the Hanafi imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (zahir al-riwaya), if the moon is sighted in Madinah, does it obligate fasting for Muslims in Makkah, assuming a distance of several hundred kilometers?
A) Yes, because there is no consideration of differing rising places
B) No, because each city has its own horizon
C) Yes, but only if the distance is less than a month's journey
D) No, except for cities in the same latitude

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school disregards differences in lunar horizons for the purpose of moonsighting; a verified sighting anywhere obligates all Muslims, and daytime sightings are always for the coming night.
