Establishing the Crescent of Ramadan and Differences in Matali'
Rulings on confirming Ramadan crescent and the effect of differing sighting locations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for confirming the crescent of Ramadan, explain the dispute over differing rising places, and apply the fatwa regarding binding of sighting across cities.

Core Idea
Once the crescent is confirmed in any city, all Muslims are bound to fast — differences in rising places are disregarded.

Determining the start of Ramadan requires reliable testimony of the crescent sighting. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the rules of testimony, the dispute over whether sighting in one city binds another, and the handling of daytime sightings. Understanding these rules ensures unity in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is confirming the crescent of Ramadan. The ruling: it is wajib to fast upon valid confirmation. What constitutes valid confirmation?
The passage distinguishes between clear skies (needs large crowd) and cloudy (two witnesses suffice).
2
Locate the dalil
The text does not quote a primary revelation but uses caution (ihtiyat) and analogy with other time-based rulings (e.g., sunset differences) to support binding sighting across cities.
The argument contrasts with the Shafi'i position that allows local sighting.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi reasoning: since the crescent's separation from the sun varies by region, but the ummah is one, caution dictates that once confirmed anywhere, it binds all.
This is a qiyas based on the unity of worship timings.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Disputes: (a) Testimony: Hanafi requires two men or one man two women; Shafi'i accepts one. (b) Binding across cities: some Hanafis say not binding if distance is great; fatwa says binding.
Intra-Hanafi differences are also noted (e.g., number of witnesses).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that sighting in one city binds all, and daytime sighting is disregarded. This is chosen for caution (ihtiyat).
The author notes that the alternative view (non-binding) is more logically consistent but not the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت شهر رمضان وإلا فجمع عظيم لهما، والأضحى كالفطر ، ولا عبرة لاختلاف المطالع. أي لرمضان والفطر أي هلاله وبقية الشهور حتى يتواتر الخبر من كل جانب، فلو جاء واحد من خارج المصر فظاهر الرواية أن لا يقبل، وكذا إذا كان على مكان مرتفع في المصر ، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه تقبل شهادة رجلين ورجل وامرأتين، وعن خلف ابن أيوب قال: خمس مائة ببلخ قليل، وعن أبي حفص الكبير أنه ألفاً، يعتبر وعن محمد أنه قال: القلة والكثرة على رأي الأمام وهو الأصح وقال الشافعي تقبل شهادة الواحد. (مسكين) وإلا : أي وإن لم يكن بالسماء علة كالفطر أي كهلال الفطر في الثبوت بشهادة حرين أو حر وحرتين وهو الأصح. (ط، ع) ولا عبرة إلخ: أي إذا رأى الهلال أهل بلدة يلزم ذلك أهل بلدة أخرى في ظاهر الرواية مطلقاً سواء كان بين البلدتين تفاوت أو لا، وعليه الفتوى وقال بعضهم: لا يلزم وهو الأشبه وإن كان الأول هو الأصح للاحتياط؛ لأن انفصال الهلال من شعاع الشمس يختلف باختلاف الأقطار كما في دخول الوقت وخروجه، حتى إذا زالت الشمس في الشرق لا يلزم منه أن تزول في الغرب، وكذا طلوع الفجر وغروب الشمس، بل كلما تحركت الشمس درجة فتلك طلوع فجر لقوم وطلوع شمس لآخرين وغروب لبعض ونصف ليل لآخرين، وهذا مثبت في علم الأفلاك والهيئة رمز الحقائق: (۱۳۷۱، ۱۳۸] وقال بعضهم: إذا لم يكن بين بلدتين تفاوت لا تختلف المطالع، وإن كان بينهما تفاوت أي مسيرة شهر فصاعداً تختلف المطالع، ولا يلزم حكم إحدى البلدتين بالبلدة الأخرى، ولا عبرة أيضاً برؤية الهلال نهاراً قبل الزوال وبعده وهو الليلة المستقبلة، وعند أبي يوسف: إذا كان قبل الزوال فهو الليلة الماضية، فيحكم بوجوب الفطر، وعن أبي حنيفة في رواية إن كان مجراه أمام الشمس تتلوه، فهو من الليلة الماضية، فيحكم بوجوب الفطر، وإن كان مجراه خلف الشمس فهو من الليلة المستقبلة. (مسكين، عيني) المطالع بل إذا ثبت في مصر لزم سائر الناس، فيلزم أهل المشرق برؤية أهل المغرب وعليه الفتوى، ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً مطلقاً. (ط،ع)
Establishing the month of Ramadan: Otherwise, a large gathering is needed for them (for Ramadan and Fitr), and the Adha is like Fitr. There is no consideration for differences in rising places. That is, for Ramadan and Fitr, meaning its crescent and the rest of the months, until the report becomes widespread from every side. If one person comes from outside the city, the apparent ruling is that it is not accepted. Likewise if he is on a high place in the city. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the testimony of two men or one man and two women is accepted. From Khalaf ibn Ayyub: five hundred in Balkh is few. From Abu Hafs al-Kabir: one thousand is considered. From Muhammad: the fewness and manyness are upon the opinion of the Imam, and that is the most correct. Al-Shafi'i said: the testimony of one is accepted. (Miskin) Otherwise: if there is no obstruction in the sky, it is like Fitr, i.e., like the crescent of Fitr in being established by the testimony of two free men or one free man and two free women, and that is the most correct. (T, A) 'There is no consideration etc.': That is, if the people of a town see the crescent, it is binding upon the people of another town in the apparent narration, absolutely, whether there is a difference between the two towns or not, and upon that is the fatwa. Some said: it is not binding, and that is more appropriate, although the first is more correct for caution. Because the separation of the crescent from the sun's rays differs according to different regions, as in the entry and exit of times. When the sun sets in the east, it does not necessarily follow that it sets in the west. Likewise the dawn and sunset. Rather every time the sun moves a degree, that is dawn for some, sunrise for others, sunset for some, and midnight for others. This is established in astronomy and cosmology. Some said: if there is no difference between two towns, the rising places do not differ. If there is a difference, i.e., a month's travel or more, then the rising places differ, and the ruling of one town is not binding on the other. Also there is no consideration for seeing the crescent during the day before noon or after, it is for the coming night. According to Abu Yusuf: if it was before noon, it is for the past night, so the obligation of breaking fast is judged. From Abu Hanifa in one narration: if its path is in front of the sun following it, it is from the past night, so breaking fast is obligatory; if its path is behind the sun, it is from the coming night. (Miskin, Ayni) 'The rising places': Rather if it is established in one city, it binds all people, so the people of the east are bound by the sighting of the people of the west, and upon that is the fatwa. There is no consideration for seeing the crescent during the day at all. (T, A)
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on the methods of confirming the crescent of Ramadan and the related issues of testimony and differing rising places. The text begins by stating that if the weather is clear, a large crowd is needed for testimony of both Ramadan and Fitr; for other months, widespread report is required. There is a discussion on accepting testimony from a single individual: in the apparent riwayah, one person is not accepted even if on a high place, but there are narrations from Hanafi imams allowing two men or one man and two women (as per Hasan from Abu Hanifa). Imam Muhammad says the standard is the judge's discretion. Shafi'i accepts one witness. Then the text explains that once the crescent is seen in one city, it is binding on all other cities absolutely, according to the fatwa. Some scholars argued it is not binding if there is a significant distance (a month's travel), but the relied-upon view is the former. Finally, it discusses seeing the crescent during the day: generally it is considered for the coming night, but Abu Yusuf and a narration from Abu Hanifa differentiate based on whether it appears before noon or its relation to the sun. However, the fatwa is that it is completely disregarded.

Key Terms
المطالع (al-matali'): The rising places of the crescent, i.e., the locations where the new moon is first sighted.
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony regarding sighting of the crescent, which must meet certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, if the crescent of Ramadan is sighted in one city, what is the ruling for another city with a different rising time?
A) It is not binding, as each city follows its own sighting.
B) It is binding, and the people of the other city must fast based on that sighting.
C) It depends on whether the other city is within a day's travel.
D) It is binding only if the sighting was by two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa holds that the crescent sighting in any city binds all Muslims, with no regard for differences in rising places or daytime sightings.

Forgetful Eating, Drinking, and Intercourse During Fasting
Ruling with proof on forgetful acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating/drinking/intercourse during fasting and explain the proof from hadith and the refutation of the Maliki qiyas.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating/drinking/intercourse do not break the fast; the explicit hadith overrides qiyas.

A common concern among fasting Muslims is what happens if they eat or drink forgetfully. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance: the fast remains valid and no make-up is required. This lesson examines the ruling, the primary evidence from the Sunnah, and the objection raised by Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is eating, drinking, or intercourse while the fasting person has forgotten that he is fasting. The ruling is that the fast is not invalidated and no qada is required.
Forgetfulness (nisyan) is defined as the absence of recollection of the fast at the time of the act.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Abu Hurairah: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.'
Narrated by Bukhari and also by al-Daraqutni with a sound chain.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'فليتم' (let him complete) indicates obligation to continue fasting, and the clause 'فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه' shows that the act is not attributed to the person but to Allah's provision, so it is not a violation.
This meaning is stronger than any possible interpretation of merely refraining out of imitation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds that forgetful eating/drinking invalidates the fast, using qiyas: just as speaking forgetfully invalidates prayer and intercourse forgetfully invalidates ihram, so does eating forgetfully invalidate fasting.
The qiyas is based on the similarity that all are prohibited acts that contradict the essence of the worship.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi response is that the state of a fasting person is not a constant reminder of the prohibition, unlike prayer (where speaking is obviously out of place) and ihram (where the pilgrim's attire and restrictions are always evident). Moreover, the explicit hadith takes precedence over qiyas.
Thus the Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) and no qada is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده فإن أكل الصائم أو شرب أو جامع ناسياً أو احتلم أو أنزل بنظر أو ادهن أو احتجم أي الصائم في نومه أي الصائم بزيت أو غيره باب ما يفسد الصوم إلخ [ أي في بيان مفسدات الصوم وغير المفسدات له.] لما فرغ من بيان الصوم وأنواعه شرع في العوارض الطارية عليه، وفساد الشيء إخراجه عما هو المطلوب وبينه وبين المبطلان في العبادات من النسب التساوي بخلافهما في المعاملات، ولهذا يثبت الملك بالقبض في البيع الفاسد لا في الباطل. (فتح) ناسياً [ أي حال كونه ناسياً وهو قيد للثلاثة قبل النية أو بعدها في الصحيح.] النسيان عدم استحضار الشيء وقت حاجته، وليس عذرا في حقوق العباد، وفي حقوقه تعالى عذر في سقوط الإثم والتقييد بالناسي يخرج المخطئ وهو الذاكر للصوم غير القاصد للفطر بأن لم يقصد الأكل ولا الشرب، بل قصد المضمضة أو اختبار طعم المأكول فسبق شيء إلى جوفه والمكره والنائم كالمخطئ، والحاصل أن هذه الأشياء الثلاثة ناسيا لا تفسد الصوم، وقال مالك: يفسد صومه وهو القياس؛ لوجود ما يضاد الصوم فصار كالكلام ناسيا في الصلاة وكالجماع في الإحرام أو الاعتكاف ولنا ما رواه أبو هريرة "من" نسي وهو صائم فأكل أو شرب فليتم صومه فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه"، رواه البخاري، وورد: "إذا أكل أو شرب ناسيا فإنما هو رزق ساقه الله إليه فلا قضاء ", وبه يندفع احتمال أن يكون المراد بإتمام الصوم الإمساك عن المفطرات تشبها بالصائمين، والحديث الثاني رواه الدار قطني وقال: إسناده صحيح، فإذا ثبت في الأكل والشرب ثبت في الجماع دلالة بخلاف الإحرام في الحج والصلاة والاعتكاف؛ لأن حالته مذكرة؛ لأن هيئته في هذه الأشياء تخالف هيئته العادية، وفي الصوم لا مذكر له. (عيني، فتح) أو احتلم: لقوله عليه: "ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم القيء والحجامة والاحتلام"، وقوله: "أو أنزل بنظر" أي لا يفسد أيضاً . سواء كان مرة أو مرتين، وقال مالك: إن نظر مرتين فأنزل فسد صومه ؛ لقوله عليه: "لا تتبع النظرة النظرة فإن الأولى لك، والأخرى عليك، ولنا أن النظر مقصور عليه غير متصل بها، فصار كالإنزال بالتفكر، والمراد بما روي في حق الإثم، ولأن ما يكون مفطراً لا يشترط التكرار فيه، وما لا يكون مفطراً لا يفطر بالتكرار، وإنما قيد بالنظر؛ لأنه إن أنزل بالتفخيذ ونحوه يفسد صومه، وقوله: "أو "احتجم فيه خلاف لأحمد؛ لقوله عليه: "أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم"، ولنا ما روي أنه عليه احتجم وهو محرم، واحتجم وهو صائم، وما رواه منسوخ؛ لأن احتجامه علي كان في السنة العاشرة، وما رواه كان في السنة الثامنة عام الفتح. (فتح) بنظر: ولو إلى فرجها مراراً أو ذكر وإن طال أو أصبح جنباً ولو استمر يوماً. (ط) أو احتجم لقوله علي: "لا يفطر من احتجم أو اغتاب"، رواه أبو داود. (ط،ع)
Chapter on what invalidates fasting and what does not invalidate it. If the fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, or has a wet dream, or ejaculates due to looking, or applies oil, or is cupped... i.e., the fasting person in his sleep, i.e., the fasting person with oil or something else. 'Chapter on what invalidates fasting etc.' [i.e., explaining the invalidators and non-invalidators.] When he finished explaining fasting and its types, he began on the incidental matters that occur to it. Corruption of a thing is taking it out from what is intended. Between corruption and nullification in acts of worship there is equality in relation, unlike in transactions. For this reason, ownership is established by possession in a corrupt sale, not in a void one. (Fath) 'Forgetfully': i.e., being forgetful, which is a condition for the three (eating, drinking, intercourse) whether before the intention or after, in the correct view. Forgetfulness is the absence of recollection of a thing at the time of need. It is not an excuse in the rights of people, but in the rights of Allah it is an excuse for the dropping of sin. The restriction with 'forgetful' excludes the mistaken person, who is mindful of fasting but does not intend to break it, e.g., he intended to rinse or taste food and something went into his throat, and the coerced person and the sleeper are like the mistaken. The conclusion is that these three things forgetfully do not invalidate fasting. Malik said: it invalidates his fast, and that is the analogy because there exists something that contradicts fasting, so it becomes like speaking forgetfully in prayer and like intercourse in ihram or itikaf. Our proof is what Abu Hurairah narrated: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' Narrated by Bukhari. Also narrated: 'If he eats or drinks forgetfully, it is only a provision Allah has provided him, so there is no make-up.' By this, the possibility that the meaning of completing the fast is refraining from invalidators in imitation of fasting people is repelled. The second hadith was narrated by al-Daraqutni and he said its chain is sound. So if it is established for eating and drinking, it is established for intercourse by indication, unlike ihram in Hajj, prayer, and itikaf, because his state is a reminder, since his form in these things differs from his normal form, while in fasting there is no reminder for him. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or had a wet dream': due to his statement: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: vomiting, cupping, and wet dream.' And his statement: 'Or ejaculated due to looking' means it also does not invalidate, whether once or twice. Malik said: if he looked twice and ejaculated, his fast is invalidated due to his statement: 'Do not follow one look with another, for the first is for you, and the second is against you.' Our proof is that looking is confined to him and not connected to it, so it is like ejaculation due to thinking. And what is narrated is regarding sin. And because whatever is an invalidator does not require repetition, and whatever is not an invalidator does not become invalid by repetition. It is restricted to looking because if he ejaculates due to thigh friction etc., it invalidates his fast. And his statement: 'or was cupped' regarding which there is a dispute with Ahmad due to his statement: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast.' Our proof is what was narrated that he (peace be upon him) was cupped while in ihram and while fasting, and what he narrated is abrogated because his cupping was in the tenth year, while what he narrated was in the eighth year during the conquest. (Fath) 'Due to looking': even if repeatedly at her private parts or the male organ even if prolonged, or if he became junub in the morning even if it continued all day. (T) 'Or was cupped' due to his statement: 'He does not break the fast who is cupped or backbites' narrated by Abu Dawud. (T, A)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on what invalidates and does not invalidate fasting. It begins by listing actions that do not break the fast: eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully; wet dream; ejaculation due to looking; applying oil; cupping. The commentary explains the definition of forgetfulness and distinguishes it from mistake, coercion, and sleep. It then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Malik and Ahmad, providing hadith proofs. For forgetful eating/drinking, the key hadith from Bukhari is cited. For wet dream, a hadith listing three things that do not break fast. For ejaculation due to looking, it distinguishes between looking and physical stimulation. For cupping, it addresses the apparent conflict with a hadith from Ahmad, noting abrogation based on the year of the events.

Key Terms
الناسي (al-nasi): One who forgets he is fasting while performing an act that would otherwise break the fast.
المخطئ (al-mukhti'): One who is mindful of fasting but does not intend to break it, e.g., accidental swallowing.
الجماع (al-jima'): Sexual intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a fasting person forgetfully eats and drinks, what is the ruling?
A) His fast is broken and he must make it up.
B) His fast is valid and no make-up is required.
C) He must make it up if it was before midday.
D) His fast is valid but he must refrain for the rest of the day.

Key Takeaway
Forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse does not break the fast, and no make-up is required, based on explicit prophetic hadith.

Sawm — Fasting

Wet Dream and Ejaculation Due to Looking During Fasting
Ruling with proof on involuntary emissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wet dream and ejaculation due to looking during fasting and explain the distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts.

Core Idea
Wet dream and ejaculation from looking do not invalidate the fast; they are involuntary or non-contact emissions.

Sometimes a fasting person experiences a wet dream or ejaculates while looking. Are these acts invalidating? The Hanafi school rules that they do not break the fast, provided there is no physical stimulation. This lesson examines the evidence and the Maliki objection on repeated looking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
Wet dream (ihtilam) during sleep and ejaculation due to looking (nazar) while awake are not invalidators of the fast. The fast remains valid.
Wet dream is completely involuntary; looking is voluntary but the emission is not directly caused by contact.
2
Locate the dalil for wet dream
The hadith: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: vomiting, cupping, and wet dream.'
This hadith is cited in the text as the proof for wet dream.
3
Locate the dalil for ejaculation due to looking
The text states that ejaculation from looking does not invalidate, based on the reasoning that looking is confined to the person and not connected to the act.
The text contrasts this with ejaculation due to thigh friction, which does invalidate because it involves physical contact.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on repeated looking
Imam Malik said that if a person looks twice and ejaculates, the fast is invalidated, citing the hadith 'Do not follow one look with another, for the first is for you and the second is against you.'
The Hanafi response: the hadith is about sin, not invalidation. Moreover, something that is not an invalidator does not become one by repetition.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position distinguishes between emission from looking (no physical contact) and emission from touch (invalidates). Repeated looking does not change the nature.
Thus, the fatwa is that ejaculation from looking does not break the fast, even if repeated.

Source Text

باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده فإن أكل الصائم أو شرب أو جامع ناسياً أو احتلم أو أنزل بنظر أو ادهن أو احتجم أي الصائم في نومه أي الصائم بزيت أو غيره باب ما يفسد الصوم إلخ [ أي في بيان مفسدات الصوم وغير المفسدات له.] لما فرغ من بيان الصوم وأنواعه شرع في العوارض الطارية عليه، وفساد الشيء إخراجه عما هو المطلوب وبينه وبين المبطلان في العبادات من النسب التساوي بخلافهما في المعاملات، ولهذا يثبت الملك بالقبض في البيع الفاسد لا في الباطل. (فتح) ناسياً [ أي حال كونه ناسياً وهو قيد للثلاثة قبل النية أو بعدها في الصحيح.] النسيان عدم استحضار الشيء وقت حاجته، وليس عذرا في حقوق العباد، وفي حقوقه تعالى عذر في سقوط الإثم والتقييد بالناسي يخرج المخطئ وهو الذاكر للصوم غير القاصد للفطر بأن لم يقصد الأكل ولا الشرب، بل قصد المضمضة أو اختبار طعم المأكول فسبق شيء إلى جوفه والمكره والنائم كالمخطئ، والحاصل أن هذه الأشياء الثلاثة ناسيا لا تفسد الصوم، وقال مالك: يفسد صومه وهو القياس؛ لوجود ما يضاد الصوم فصار كالكلام ناسيا في الصلاة وكالجماع في الإحرام أو الاعتكاف ولنا ما رواه أبو هريرة "من" نسي وهو صائم فأكل أو شرب فليتم صومه فإنما أطعمه الله وسقاه"، رواه البخاري، وورد: "إذا أكل أو شرب ناسيا فإنما هو رزق ساقه الله إليه فلا قضاء ", وبه يندفع احتمال أن يكون المراد بإتمام الصوم الإمساك عن المفطرات تشبها بالصائمين، والحديث الثاني رواه الدار قطني وقال: إسناده صحيح، فإذا ثبت في الأكل والشرب ثبت في الجماع دلالة بخلاف الإحرام في الحج والصلاة والاعتكاف؛ لأن حالته مذكرة؛ لأن هيئته في هذه الأشياء تخالف هيئته العادية، وفي الصوم لا مذكر له. (عيني، فتح) أو احتلم: لقوله عليه: "ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم القيء والحجامة والاحتلام"، وقوله: "أو أنزل بنظر" أي لا يفسد أيضاً . سواء كان مرة أو مرتين، وقال مالك: إن نظر مرتين فأنزل فسد صومه ؛ لقوله عليه: "لا تتبع النظرة النظرة فإن الأولى لك، والأخرى عليك، ولنا أن النظر مقصور عليه غير متصل بها، فصار كالإنزال بالتفكر، والمراد بما روي في حق الإثم، ولأن ما يكون مفطراً لا يشترط التكرار فيه، وما لا يكون مفطراً لا يفطر بالتكرار، وإنما قيد بالنظر؛ لأنه إن أنزل بالتفخيذ ونحوه يفسد صومه، وقوله: "أو "احتجم فيه خلاف لأحمد؛ لقوله عليه: "أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم"، ولنا ما روي أنه عليه احتجم وهو محرم، واحتجم وهو صائم، وما رواه منسوخ؛ لأن احتجامه علي كان في السنة العاشرة، وما رواه كان في السنة الثامنة عام الفتح. (فتح) بنظر: ولو إلى فرجها مراراً أو ذكر وإن طال أو أصبح جنباً ولو استمر يوماً. (ط) أو احتجم لقوله علي: "لا يفطر من احتجم أو اغتاب"، رواه أبو داود. (ط،ع)
Chapter on what invalidates fasting and what does not invalidate it. If the fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, or has a wet dream, or ejaculates due to looking, or applies oil, or is cupped... i.e., the fasting person in his sleep, i.e., the fasting person with oil or something else. 'Chapter on what invalidates fasting etc.' [i.e., explaining the invalidators and non-invalidators.] When he finished explaining fasting and its types, he began on the incidental matters that occur to it. Corruption of a thing is taking it out from what is intended. Between corruption and nullification in acts of worship there is equality in relation, unlike in transactions. For this reason, ownership is established by possession in a corrupt sale, not in a void one. (Fath) 'Forgetfully': i.e., being forgetful, which is a condition for the three (eating, drinking, intercourse) whether before the intention or after, in the correct view. Forgetfulness is the absence of recollection of a thing at the time of need. It is not an excuse in the rights of people, but in the rights of Allah it is an excuse for the dropping of sin. The restriction with 'forgetful' excludes the mistaken person, who is mindful of fasting but does not intend to break it, e.g., he intended to rinse or taste food and something went into his throat, and the coerced person and the sleeper are like the mistaken. The conclusion is that these three things forgetfully do not invalidate fasting. Our proof is what Abu Hurairah narrated: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' Narrated by Bukhari. Also narrated: 'If he eats or drinks forgetfully, it is only a provision Allah has provided him, so there is no make-up.' By this, the possibility that the meaning of completing the fast is refraining from invalidators in imitation of fasting people is repelled. The second hadith was narrated by al-Daraqutni and he said its chain is sound. So if it is established for eating and drinking, it is established for intercourse by indication, unlike ihram in Hajj, prayer, and itikaf, because his state is a reminder, since his form in these things differs from his normal form, while in fasting there is no reminder for him. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or had a wet dream': due to his statement: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: vomiting, cupping, and wet dream.' (T) 'Or was cupped' due to his statement: 'He does not break the fast who is cupped or backbites' narrated by Abu Dawud. (T, A)

Cupping, Applying Oil, and Other Non-Invalidating Acts During Fasting
Ruling with proof on cupping, oil, and janabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cupping, applying oil, and being in a state of junub during fasting, and explain the abrogation argument regarding the hadith on cupping.

Core Idea
Cupping, applying oil, and being junub in the morning do not break the fast; the hadith that suggests otherwise is abrogated.

Besides the common invalidators, there are actions that people often mistakenly think break the fast, such as cupping or applying oil. The Hanafi school clarifies that these do not invalidate, based on the Prophet's own practice and the clear distinction between abrogated and abiding narrations. This lesson explores these rulings and the dispute with Imam Ahmad on cupping.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The acts covered: cupping (hijamah), applying oil (idhān), and being in a state of janabah in the morning. All are ruled not to invalidate the fast.
Applying oil and being junub are also mentioned in the text as non-invalidators.
2
Locate the dalil for cupping
The Hanafi proof comes from the hadith that the Prophet was cupped while fasting, and from the statement 'He does not break the fast who is cupped or backbites' (Abu Dawud).
The text also cites a hadith that the Prophet was cupped while in ihram and while fasting.
3
Address the conflicting hadith
Imam Ahmad cites a hadith: 'The cupper and the cupped have broken their fast.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith is abrogated because the Prophet's cupping in the tenth year (later) overrides the earlier hadith from the year of conquest.
The principle: later actions of the Prophet abrogate earlier conflicting statements when the timing is known.
4
Note the other acts
Applying oil to the head or body, and being in a state of janabah in the morning (even if continuing all day) do not invalidate the fast, as they are not nutrients or acts of worship that require purity.
These are straightforward and not generally disputed among the schools.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position on cupping is relied upon due to the abrogation argument and the explicit hadith from Abu Dawud. The other acts are also non-controversial.
Thus, a fasting person can undergo cupping for medical reasons without breaking the fast.

Key Terms

الادهان (al-idhān): Applying oil externally to the body or hair.
الجنب (al-junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on cupping (hijamah) during fasting?
A) It invalidates the fast, and qada is required.
B) It does not invalidate the fast.
C) It invalidates the fast only if done by a Muslim cupper.
D) It invalidates the fast only if blood is drawn in large quantity.

Key Takeaway
Cupping, applying oil, and being in a state of janabah do not invalidate the fast; the conflicting hadith on cupping is abrogated.

Forgetful Eating/Drinking/Sexual Intercourse Does Not Break Fast
Establishes the ruling and proof for forgetful acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during fast, identify the key hadith evidence, and explain why the Hanafi position differs from the Maliki position.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating/drinking/intercourse does not invalidate the fast—Allah Himself provided the sustenance.

This passage clarifies an important exception to the rules of fasting: when a fasting person eats, drinks, or engages in marital intimacy forgetfully, the fast remains valid. The text defines forgetfulness and distinguishes it from other unintentional acts like mistakes or coercion. It then presents the proof from authentic hadith and addresses the objection raised by Imam Malik based on analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What does the passage rule on? It states that eating, drinking, or intercourse forgetfully does not invalidate the fast.
The act is done forgetfully.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith from Abu Hurairah: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' Also a hadith from al-Daraqutni.
Both hadith are authentic.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'let him complete his fast' (amr) indicates the fast remains valid. The rationale 'Allah has fed him' shows the act is not attributable to the fasting person, thus no invalidation.
The amr is for continuation, not just refraining from further acts.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds that the fast is invalidated, analogizing to forgetful speech in prayer or intercourse in ihram.
The analogy is based on the presence of the action that opposes the purpose.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the explicit hadith over qiyas. They argue that fasting lacks a distinctive form that reminds the person, unlike prayer or hajj, so the analogy fails.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

Source Text

(عيني، فتح)
Book of Fasting. Chapter of what invalidates the fast and what does not. If the fasting person eats, drinks, or engages in sexual intercourse forgetfully, or has a wet dream, or emits by looking, or applies oil, or undergoes cupping... [Then commentary:] The chapter of what invalidates the fast etc. [i.e., explaining the invalidators and non-invalidators of the fast.] When he finished explaining fasting and its types, he began on the accidental occurrences upon it. Invalidity of a thing is to remove it from what is required. Between invalidity (fasad) and nullification (butlan) in acts of worship there is equivalence, unlike in transactions, so ownership is established by possession in a fasid sale but not in a batil sale. (Fath) 'Forgetfully' [i.e., being in a state of forgetfulness, which is a condition for the three acts before or after the intention according to the sound view.] Forgetfulness is not remembering a thing at the time of need. It is not an excuse in the rights of people, but in the rights of Allah it is an excuse for the dropping of sin. The restriction to the forgetful excludes the mistaken one, who remembers the fast but does not intend to break it, such as intending rinsing or tasting food, and something enters his stomach; also the coerced and the sleeping are like the mistaken. The conclusion is that these three acts forgetfully do not invalidate the fast. Malik said: It invalidates his fast, and that is the analogy, because there is opposition to fasting, so it is like speaking forgetfully in prayer or like intercourse in ihram or i'tikaf. Our proof is what Abu Hurairah narrated: 'Whoever forgets while fasting and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink.' Narrated by al-Bukhari. And it is narrated: 'When he eats or drinks forgetfully, it is only provision that Allah has brought to him, so there is no make-up.' With this, the possibility that the meaning of completing the fast is abstaining from invalidators like fasting people is refuted. The second hadith was narrated by al-Daraqutni who said its chain is authentic. If it is established for eating and drinking, it is established for intercourse by implication, unlike ihram in Hajj, prayer, and i'tikaf, because their state is a reminder, for their form differs from the ordinary form, whereas in fasting there is no reminder. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling that if a fasting person eats, drinks, or has sexual intercourse forgetfully, the fast remains valid and is not broken. The passage begins by defining the chapter, then explains forgetfulness and its effect. Forgetfulness is defined as not remembering at the time of need, and it is an excuse in the rights of Allah such that sin is removed, but not an excuse in the rights of people. The restriction to the forgetful excludes the mistaken person who does something unintentionally but remembers the fast; such acts do break the fast. The passage then addresses the dispute with Imam Malik, who analogizes forgetful breaking of the fast to forgetful speech in prayer, both of which invalidate. The Hanafi response relies on authentic hadiths from the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly stating that the forgetful person should complete his fast, as Allah provided the sustenance. The second hadith confirms there is no make-up required. The analogy is extended from eating and drinking to intercourse. The difference from prayer, Hajj, and i'tikaf is that those acts have a distinct form that reminds one of the sacred state, whereas the form of a fasting person is ordinary, so there is no reminder.

Key Terms
النسيان (al-nisyan): Forgetfulness: lack of recollection at the time of need. It is a valid excuse in the rights of Allah, removing sin and the invalidation of the fast.
المخطئ (al-mukhti'): The mistaken one: one who remembers the fast but does not intend to break it, e.g., intending to rinse and water enters. This does break the fast according to Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is broken and must be made up with qada'.
B) The fast remains valid and no make-up is required.
C) The fast is broken but no make-up is required.
D) The fast becomes makruh.

Key Takeaway
Forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse during fast does not break it, thanks to the explicit hadith and the principle that Allah's provision overrides human forgetfulness.

Wet Dreams and Emission by Looking Do Not Break the Fast
States ruling for wet dream and emission by looking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wet dreams and emission by looking during fasting, and explain the proof and the Maliki dispute.

Core Idea
Wet dreams and emission by looking do not break the fast; only direct physical contact invalidates.

What happens to your fast if you have a wet dream during the day? Does looking at something arousing and emitting break it? The Hanafi school gives clear answers: both leave your fast intact. This ruling is based on a hadith and a careful distinction between direct physical contact and other forms of arousal. Here we explore the proofs and the Maliki disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their ruling
First, recognise that the text deals with two scenarios: wet dream (ihtilam) and emission by looking (inzal bi nazar). Both are ruled as not invalidating the fast.
The ruling is 'sahih' – the fast remains valid.
2
Locate the dalil for wet dream
The author cites the hadith: 'Three things do not break the fast: vomiting, cupping, and wet dream.' This is a direct textual proof that wet dreams are not mufsidat.
The hadith is a Sunnah ahad, but its indication is clear (dalala qatiya).
3
Understand the Hanafi proof for emission by looking
The Hanafi position is that looking is not connected to the body of the other person, so it resembles emission by thought, which does not break the fast. The text argues that what is not an invalidator does not become one by repetition.
The illa (effective cause) for invalidation is direct physical contact.
4
Analyze the Maliki objection
Imam Malik holds that if one looks twice and then emits, the fast is broken, citing the hadith: 'Do not follow one glance with another...' The Hanafi response distinguishes between sin and invalidation: the hadith refers to prohibition, not nullification.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi: fast valid; Maliki: fast broken if emission after repeated looks.
5
Apply the ruling to cases
The text adds that the ruling is specifically about looking. If emission occurs by direct contact (e.g., thigh rubbing), the fast is broken. Also, being in a state of janaba all day does not break the fast.
This sharpens the boundary of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage covers two related rulings: wet dreams (ihtilam) and emission due to looking (inzal bi nazar). Both do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school. The proof for wet dreams is the hadith listing three things that do not break the fast. For emission by looking, the Hanafis hold that it does not break the fast, whether once or multiple times. A dispute with Imam Malik arises: Malik says if the person looks twice and then emits, the fast is broken, citing the prohibition of following one glance with another. The Hanafi response is that looking is an act confined to the looker and not directly connected to the woman, so it resembles emission by thought, which does not break the fast. The hadith about glances is about sin, not about nullifying the fast. Furthermore, if something is not an invalidator, repetition does not make it so. The text also clarifies that the restriction to looking means that if emission occurs by direct physical contact like thigh rubbing, the fast is broken. An additional note mentions that looking repeatedly at private parts or being in a state of janaba does not break the fast.

Key Terms
الاحتلام (al-ihtilam): Wet dream: involuntary emission of semen during sleep. It does not break the fast.
الإنزال بالنظر (al-inzal bi al-nazar): Emission caused by looking at something arousing. It does not break the fast in the Hanafi school.

Cupping Does Not Invalidate the Fast
Ruling that cupping does not invalidate fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cupping while fasting and explain the evidence from the Prophet's practice and the hadith of 'Ali, distinguishing it from the Hanbali position.

Core Idea
Cupping does not break the fast in the Hanafi school because the Prophet's later practice abrogates earlier hadith.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common medical concern during fasting: does cupping (hijama) invalidate the fast? The Hanafi school holds firmly that it does not, and the commentator provides dual evidence from the Sunnah. Understanding this ruling requires engaging with the principle of naskh (abrogation) and the strength of different hadith transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested act and the two positions
Recognize that the issue is cupping (hijama) during a fast. One position (Imam Ahmad) says it breaks the fast; the other (Hanafi) says it does not.
2
Locate the opposing proof
Examine the hadith cited by Imam Ahmad: 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast.' Understand that this is a direct statement attributed to the Prophet (peace be upon him).
3
Trace the Hanafi counter-proof
Identify the two proofs: (1) The Prophet himself was cupped while fasting in the tenth year, and (2) the hadith of 'Ali that 'whoever undergoes cupping or backbites does not break his fast.'
4
Analyse the naskh argument
Understand why the Hanafis consider the Ahmad's hadith abrogated: the Prophet's cupping in the tenth year occurred after the hadith from the eighth year (year of Conquest), so the later action supersedes the earlier statement.
Naskh (abrogation) is a key usul principle: a later ruling can abrogate an earlier one if they conflict.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare practical outcomes: In the Hanafi school, a person who undergoes cupping while fasting has a valid fast and does not need to make up the day. In the Hanbali school, the fast is broken and must be made up later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: "أو "احتجم فيه خلاف لأحمد؛ لقوله عليه: "أفطر الحاجم والمحجوم"، ولنا ما روي أنه عليه احتجم وهو محرم، واحتجم وهو صائم، وما رواه منسوخ؛ لأن احتجامه علي كان في السنة العاشرة، وما رواه كان في السنة الثامنة عام الفتح. أو احتجم لقوله علي: "لا يفطر من احتجم أو اغتاب"، رواه أبو داود. (ط،ع)
And his statement: 'or underwent cupping' – there is a dispute regarding it with Ahmad, because of his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast.' Our proof is that it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) had cupping done while in ihram and while fasting, and what he (Ahmad) narrated is abrogated, because his cupping (peace be upon him) was in the tenth year, and what he narrated was in the eighth year, the year of the Conquest. Or cupping: because of the statement of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Whoever undergoes cupping or backbites does not break his fast.' Narrated by Abu Dawud. (T, A)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on cupping (hijama) during fasting. The matn mentions cupping, and the commentator notes a dispute with Imam Ahmad. Ahmad's evidence is the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast.' The Hanafi response is twofold: first, there is a hadith in Sahih that the Prophet (peace be upon him) himself was cupped while fasting and while in ihram. Second, the hadith that Ahmad relies upon is abrogated (mansukh) because the cupping of the Prophet in the eighth year (Conquest) was before the final ruling, and later, in the tenth year, the Prophet was cupped while fasting, indicating the permissibility. Additionally, the Hanafis cite the hadith from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that cupping does not break the fast. Thus, cupping is not a mufsid.

Key Terms
الحجامة (al-hijama): Cupping: a medical procedure where blood is drawn from the skin. In the Hanafi school, it does not break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It invalidates the fast but no make-up is needed.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, cupping does not invalidate the fast because the Prophet's later practice abrogates the earlier hadith, and 'Ali's statement confirms this.

Kohl (Eyeline) Does Not Break the Fast
Ruling that applying kohl does not invalidate fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on applying kohl while fasting and explain the basis for the ruling using the hadith of 'Aisha and the principle of entry through orifices.

Core Idea
Kohl does not break the fast because it does not enter through a natural orifice and the Prophet himself did it while fasting.

This lesson addresses a common question: does applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes invalidate the fast? The Hanafi school clearly states that it does not, even if one perceives its taste in the throat. We will examine the primary proof—the hadith of 'Aisha—and the school's response to opposing views from Malik and Ahmad. Understanding this ruling clarifies the broader principle that only substances entering through open orifices can break the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes while fasting. Hanafi ruling: it does not break the fast.
2
Locate the primary proof
The proof is the hadith of 'Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting, reported by al-Daraqutni.
This is a sunnah ahad hadith with zanni thubut.
3
Analyze the proof
The Prophet's explicit action indicates permissibility. Additionally, there is no direct passage from the eye to the brain; tears come out like sweat. Any trace found in the throat is only an effect, not the substance itself.
Analogous to grinding medicine and tasting it—does not break fast.
4
Examine the dispute
Malik and Ahmad hold that if the taste reaches the throat, the fast is broken, citing a hadith that the Prophet said to avoid kohl at night. The Hanafi response: that narration is weak (munkar) according to Yahya ibn Ma'in, and even if authentic, it was specific to that type of kohl out of compassion.
The dispute shows the importance of hadith criticism and contextual interpretation.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Hanafi, the fast remains valid in all cases. Under Maliki/Hanbali, if the taste is detected, the fast is broken. The mufta bihi position is the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو اكتحل أو قبل، بخلاف الإنزال به أو دخل حلقه غبارٌ أو ذباب وهو ذاكر لصومه
أو اكتحل: أي الاكتحال لا يفسد الصوم أيضاً؛ لما روي عن عائشة لها أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم اكتحل وهو صائم، رواه الدار قطني، ولا فرق بين أن يجد طعمه في حلقه أو لم يجد، وكذا لو بزق ووجد لونه في الأصح، وقال مالك وأحمد: وجد طعمه في حلقه يفسد؛ لما روي أنه عليه أمر بالإثمد المروح عند النوم، وقال: ليتقه الصائم"، ولنا: ما روينا من حديث عائشة لها ; ولأنه ليس بين العين والدماغ مسلك والدمع يخرج بالترشح كالعرق، وما يجده في حلقه أثر الكحل لا عينه فلا يضره كمن دق الدواء ووجد طعمه في حلقه؛ إذ لا يمكن الامتناع عنه وكذا من اغتسل فوجد برد الماء في باطنه لا يفطر؛ لأن المفطر إنما هو الداخل من المنافذ، وما روياه منكر قاله يحيى بن معين، فلا الاحتجاج به، ولئن صح فهو محمول على أنه عليه قال ذلك شفقة عليهم؛ لاحتمال أنه عليه عرف في الإمحمد صفة لا توافق الصائم كالحرارة ونحوه.
Or applied kohl or kissed, unlike emission from it, or if dust or a fly enters his throat while he is aware of his fast. Or applied kohl: meaning kohl does not break the fast either; because it is narrated from 'Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting, narrated by al-Daraqutni. There is no difference between finding its taste in the throat or not, likewise if he spits and sees its color in the correct view. Malik and Ahmad said: If he finds its taste in his throat, it breaks the fast, because it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) ordered using ithmid (antimony) that is perfumed at night and said: 'Let the fasting person be cautious of it.' Our proof: the hadith of 'Aisha; and because there is no passage between the eye and the brain, tears come out by percolation like sweat. What he finds in his throat is only the trace of kohl, not its substance, so it does not harm him, like the one who grinds medicine and finds its taste in his throat, as it is impossible to avoid it. Similarly, the one who bathes and finds the coldness of water in his interior does not break his fast, because the invalidator is only what enters through the orifices. What they narrated is munkar (rejected) according to Yahya ibn Ma'in, so it cannot be used as evidence. Even if it is authentic, it is interpreted as the Prophet (peace be upon him) saying that out of compassion for them, because perhaps he knew that the ithmid had a property that does not suit the fasting person, like heat etc.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling of applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes during fasting. The Hanafi position is that it does not break the fast, even if one finds its taste in the throat or spits its color. The proof is the hadith of 'Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting. The text also cites the dispute with Malik and Ahmad, who hold that if the taste reaches the throat, the fast is broken, based on a narration that the Prophet said to avoid it. The Hanafi response argues that there is no direct passage from the eye to the brain; any trace that reaches the throat is merely an effect, not the substance itself, so it is like tasting ground medicine. Furthermore, the contrary narration is considered weak by Yahya ibn Ma'in. If it were authentic, it would be due to the specific property of that kohl, not a general rule. The underlying principle is that only substances that enter through the orifices (mouth, nose, etc.) can potentially break the fast; kohl does not enter through such pathways.

Key Terms
الاكتحال (al-iktihal): Applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes. In fasting, it does not break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a fasting person applies kohl and finds its taste in his throat, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken and must be made up.
B) The fast remains valid.
C) The fast is broken but no make-up is needed.
D) It is makruh but the fast is valid.

Key Takeaway
Kohl does not break the fast, even if taste or color is detected, because only substances entering through open orifices can invalidate fasting.

Kissing While Fasting: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing while fasting, identify the key condition (emission), and apply the ruling to scenarios.

Core Idea
Kissing while fasting is permissible unless it leads to emission; only qada' is required, not kaffarah.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi ruling on an intimate act during fasting: kissing. The source passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that a kiss does not break the fast unless it results in emission. We will explore the textual proof from hadith, the logical distinction from other legal contexts, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that if a fasting person kisses and does not emit, the fast is not broken. Focus on the condition: emission.
The ruling is permissive, not prohibitive.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted kissing and cupping for the fasting person. This is an ahad hadith but accepted by the Hanafi school.
The hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni and deemed trustworthy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit permission (rukhsah) in the hadith indicates allowance. In Hanafi usul, permission implies the act is not prohibited and does not nullify the fast unless another factor intervenes.
The ruling is based on the absence of emission, which is the effective cause (illa) for breaking the fast.
4
Distinguish from musahara and ruj'a
The text contrasts fasting rules with those of establishing marriage or revoking divorce. In those cases, kissing with desire establishes legal effects even without emission, because the cause is intent. In fasting, the cause is actual gratification (qada' al-shahwa) via emission.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the different legal frameworks.
5
Derive the consequence when emission occurs
If emission occurs from kissing, the fast is broken, and only qada' (make-up) is required, not kaffarah (expiation), because there is no actual intercourse (insertion).
Kaffarah is reserved for intentional sexual intercourse during fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: "أو "قبل" يعني ولم ينزل لا يفطر؛ لما روى أبو سعيد الخدري أنه عليه رخص في القبلة للصائم والحجامة، رواه الدار قطني وقال رواته ثقات بخلاف المصاهرة والرجعة، فإنهما يثبتان بالقبلة بالشهوة وكذا بالمس وإن لم ينزل؛ لأن الحكم فيها أدير على السبب المفضي للوقاع وهنا على قضاء الشهوة، ولهذا لو أنزل بالقبلة لا يثبت به حكم المصاهرة ويفسد به الصوم ولو أنزل بقبلة فعليه القضاء؛ لوجود معنى الجماع وهو الإنزال بالمباشرة دون الكفارة؛ لعدم الإيلاج [رمز الحقائق: ۱۳۸/۱]
And his statement: 'or kissed' means that if he did not emit, it does not break the fast; because Abu Sa'id al-Khudri narrated that he (peace be upon him) permitted kissing for the fasting person and cupping, narrated by al-Daraqutni who said its narrators are trustworthy. This differs from the cases of establishing marriage (musahara) and revoking divorce (ruj'a), for these are established by kissing with desire, and likewise by touching even without emission, because the ruling there rests on the cause that leads to intercourse, whereas here it rests on gratifying desire. Therefore, if he emits by kissing, the ruling of marriage is not established by it, and the fast is broken. If he emits by kissing, he must make up (qada') without expiation (kaffarah) because there is no insertion. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/138]
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on kissing during fasting. The matn mentions kissing, and the commentator clarifies that if the fasting person kisses and does not emit semen, the fast does not break. This is based on the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted kissing and cupping for the fasting person. The passage distinguishes kissing during fasting from kissing that establishes marriage (musahara) or revocation of divorce (ruj'a), where the intent matters. In fasting, the ruling is based on whether the kiss leads to emission or not. If emission occurs, the fast is broken and only qada' is required, not kaffarah, because there is no actual intercourse. The absence of kaffarah is due to the lack of direct insertion.

Key Terms
القبلة (al-qubla): Kissing. During fasting, if it does not lead to emission, it does not break the fast.
المصاهرة (al-musahara): Establishing marital relationship through a kiss or touch with desire; distinct from fasting rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No make-up needed.
B) Qada' (make-up) only, without kaffarah.
C) Both qada' and kaffarah.
D) The fast is invalid and must be repeated with kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Kissing while fasting is allowed unless it leads to emission; if emission occurs, only qada' is required.

Things Entering Throat Involuntarily Do Not Break Fast
Ruling on dust, flies, tears, etc., entering the throat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between avoidable and unavoidable entry of substances into the throat while fasting and state the Hanafi ruling for each case.

Core Idea
Entry that can be avoided breaks the fast; entry that cannot be avoided does not.

Fasting requires abstaining from anything reaching the stomach. But what if something enters involuntarily—like dust, a fly, or rain? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on such cases, drawing a critical distinction between what a person could have prevented and what was beyond control.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key condition: awareness
The text emphasizes 'while he is aware of his fast'. If the person is forgetful, the fast does not break even if the entry could have been avoided. Awareness is a prerequisite for the fast to be broken by entry.
The phrase 'بالطريق الأولى' (a fortiori) indicates that forgetfulness protects the fast even more strongly.
2
Classify the type of substance
Substances are divided into those that are typically avoidable (tears, sweat, rain, snow) and those that are often unavoidable (dust, flies, smoke). The ruling hinges on this classification, not the substance itself.
The text explicitly distinguishes between internal fluids (tears, sweat) and external particles.
3
Apply the principle of avoidability
If the person could have reasonably prevented the entry (e.g., by closing the mouth, covering the face), then the fast is broken. If prevention was not possible, the fast remains valid.
The standard is practical possibility, not absolute prevention.
4
Note the qiyas to smoke
The passage analogizes unavoidable entry to smoke from a fumigator, which is also beyond control. This qiyas grounds the ruling in a recognized legal analogy.
Smoke inhaled passively does not break the fast, but active inhalation (like using a fumigator) does.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on rain and snow
The text states that rain and snow break the fast 'in the correct view' (في الأصح), indicating a difference of opinion within the school. The relied-upon position is that they break because they are generally avoidable.
This nuance affects fatwa: a mufti must follow the correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو دخل حلقه غبار أو ذباب وهو ذاكر لصومه
أما لو دخل حلقه دموعه أو عرقه أو دم رعافه أو مطر أو ثلج فسد صومه؛ لتيسر طبق فمه وفتحه أحياناً مع الاحتراز عن الدخول، وقيد بالدخول للاحتراز عن الإدخال ولهذا صرحوا بأن الاحتواء على المبخرة مفسد، وليس كشم الورد والمسك؛ لوضوح الفرق بين هواء تطيب بريح المسك وشبهه وبين جوهر دخان وصل إلى جوفه بفعله. وقوله : "وهو ذاكر "لصومه" جملة حالية يشير إلى أنه إن كان ناسيا لصومه لا يفسد بالطريق الأولى. (مسكين، فتح)
لصومه: لا يفسد لعدم استطاعته الامتناع عنه فأشبه الدخان بخلاف المطر والثلج في الأصح. (ع)
Or dust or a fly enters his throat while he is aware of his fast. As for if his tears, sweat, blood from nosebleed, rain, or snow enter his throat, his fast is broken, because it is easy to close his mouth and open it occasionally while avoiding entry. The restriction to 'enters' is to avoid 'making it enter', hence they explicitly stated that inhaling from a fumigator is an invalidator, unlike smelling roses or musk, due to the clear difference between air perfumed with the scent of musk and the like, and the essence of smoke that reaches his stomach by his action. His statement: 'while he is aware of his fast' is a circumstantial clause indicating that if he is forgetful of his fast, it does not break the fast a fortiori. (Miskin, Fath) 'For his fast': it does not break because he cannot avoid it, so it resembles smoke, unlike rain and snow in the correct view. (A)
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when something enters the throat of a fasting person, such as dust, a fly, tears, sweat, blood, rain, or snow. The key condition is whether the person is aware of his fast and whether he could have avoided the entry. If dust or a fly enters without control while one is aware, some say it breaks the fast, but the correct view is that it does not if he cannot avoid it. However, for tears, sweat, blood, rain, or snow, the fast is broken because one can normally close his mouth or avoid them. The text also emphasizes that active inhalation of smoke from a fumigator invalidates, unlike passive smelling of perfume. The condition 'while aware of his fast' is crucial; if one is forgetful, the fast does not break even by direct entry. The analogy here is to smoke: if one cannot avoid it, it does not break the fast.

Key Terms
الدخول (al-dukhul): Entry (of something into the throat). If it happens involuntarily, it may or may not break fast depending on ability to avoid.
الذاكر (al-dhakir): One who is aware and mindful of his fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is broken because he could have closed his mouth.
B) The fast remains valid because it is unavoidable.
C) The fast is broken only if the rain is deliberate.
D) The fast becomes makruh.

Key Takeaway
The validity of the fast depends on whether the entry of a substance could have been avoided; unavoidable entries do not break the fast.

Food Particles Between Teeth: Ruling for Small vs Large Amounts
Ruling on swallowing food stuck between teeth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that small unavoidable food particles (≤ chickpea size) do not break the fast, while large or intentionally swallowed particles do, and evaluate Zufar's contrary view.

Core Idea
A small unavoidable food particle does not break the fast; a large particle does – the dividing line is a chickpea.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq concludes the chapter on fasting with a practical ruling: what if food remains between your teeth and you swallow it? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on amount and intent, using a vivid analogy to saliva and a measurable benchmark – the chickpea. We will explore the ruling, its proof via qiyas (analogy), and an intra-madhhab disagreement with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swallowing food particles left between the teeth while fasting. The Hanafi position: if the amount is small (less than a chickpea), the fast remains valid; if large (chickpea or more), the fast is broken.
Small amount is considered unavoidable like saliva.
2
Follow the qiyas proof
The proof is qiyas: the small particle is analogized to saliva – both are unavoidable residues in the mouth and do not break the fast. The operative cause (illa) is unavoidability.
Large particles are avoidable and thus break the fast.
3
Note the intra-madhhab disagreement
Imam Zufar holds that any swallowing from the mouth breaks the fast because the mouth is part of the exterior (zahir), so everything entering from it is like external food. The majority Hanafi response is that small particles are unavoidable and thus exempt.
Zufar's view is not mufta bihi in the school.
4
Apply the benchmark
The distinguishing measure is a chickpea (himmisa). Anything less than a chickpea is small; chickpea-size or more is large. This provides a clear legal standard.
The text also discusses cases like taking out the particle and then eating it, which breaks the fast.
5
Consider related scenarios
The passage extends the rule: swallowing a sesame seed already between teeth does not break fast; swallowing from outside does. Chewing a seed that dissolves does not break. Collecting saliva and swallowing is makruh but valid; removing saliva then swallowing breaks the fast.
Intentional removal and re-swallowing changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أكل ما بين إلخ إذا كان قليلاً؛ لعدم إمكان الاحتراز عنه، فإنه يبقى القليل بين الأسنان عادة، وإن كان كثيراً يفطر، وقال زفر: يفطر في الوجهين؛ لأن الفم له حكم الظاهر فيكون داخلاً من الخارج، ولنا: أن القليل لا يمكن للامتناع عنه عادة فصار تبعاً لأسنانه بمنزلة ريقه والكثير يمكن الاحتراز عنه، والفاصل بينهما قدر الحمصة فما دونها قليل، وإن أخذه بيده وأخرجه ثم أكله ينبغي أن يفسد صومه كما روي عن محمد أن الصائم إذا ابتلع سمسمة بين أسنانه لا يفسد صومه، ولو ابتلعها من خارج ابتداء يفسد، ولو مضغها لا يفسد؛ لأنها تتلاشى، ولو جمع ريقه في فيه ثم ابتلعه لم يفطر ويكره، ولو أخرجه ثم ابتلعه يفطر كريق وغيره. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۳۹/۱]
Or ate what is between his teeth etc.: if it is a small amount, because it is impossible to avoid it, as a small amount usually remains between teeth; but if it is a large amount, it breaks the fast. Zufar said: It breaks the fast in both cases, because the mouth has the ruling of the exterior, so it is considered as entering from outside. Our proof: a small amount cannot normally be avoided, so it becomes a part of the teeth like saliva; a large amount can be avoided. The dividing line is the size of a chickpea; what is less than that is small. If he takes it out with his hand and then eats it, it should break his fast, as narrated from Muhammad: The fasting person, if he swallows a sesame seed between his teeth, does not break his fast; but if he swallows it from outside initially, it breaks. If he chews it, it does not break because it dissolves. If he collects his saliva in his mouth and then swallows it, it does not break fast but is disliked; if he takes it out and then swallows it, it breaks like other saliva. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/139]
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter with the ruling on swallowing food particles stuck between the teeth. The Hanafi position distinguishes between a small amount and a large amount. If it is less than the size of a chickpea, it does not break the fast because it is unavoidable; if it is that much or more, it breaks. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) holds that any amount breaks because the mouth is like the exterior. But the relied-upon position is the distinction based on amount. If one takes the food out and then swallows it, the fast is broken because it becomes intentional. The text also gives details about swallowing a sesame seed: if it is already between teeth, it does not break; if one swallows it from outside, it breaks. Chewing a small seed does not break as it dissolves. Spit collection is also discussed: if one collects saliva and swallows, it does not break but is disliked; if one removes saliva and then swallows, it breaks.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-himmisa): Chickpea; used as a measure to distinguish between small and large amounts of food particles between teeth.
السمسمة (al-simsima): Sesame seed; an example of a small particle that does not break the fast if already between teeth and swallowed.
الريق (al-reeq): Saliva; the analogy for unavoidable small particles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a fasting person swallows a food particle stuck between his teeth that is smaller than a chickpea, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken and must be made up.
B) The fast remains valid because it was unavoidable.
C) The fast is broken only if he was aware of the particle.
D) The fast becomes makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes small unavoidable food particles (≤ chickpea) from large ones, holding that only the latter break the fast.

Kohl Does Not Break the Fast: Hanafi View with Proof and Dispute
Ruling that kohl does not break fast with hadith and refutation of opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on kohl during fasting and evaluate the opposing proof from Malik and Ahmad using hadith criticism and rational argument.

Core Idea
Kohl does not break the fast, even if taste reaches the throat, because the Prophet applied kohl while fasting and there is no direct passage from eye to throat.

Many fasting Muslims use kohl (eyeliner) for cosmetic or medicinal purposes, but a common concern is whether it invalidates the fast if its taste reaches the throat. This lesson presents the clear Hanafi position that kohl does not break the fast, supported by a direct hadith of Aisha (ra) and rational reasoning, while also addressing and refuting the opposing view of Malik and Ahmad.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: applying kohl does not break the fast, regardless of taste or color appearing in saliva.
The text says 'لا يفسد الصوم' – it does not invalidate fasting.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Malik and Ahmad hold that finding the taste of kohl in the throat breaks the fast.
They rely on a hadith about perfumed kohl and a command for fasting persons to avoid it.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's dalil is a narration: the Prophet commanded using perfumed kohl and said 'the fasting person should avoid it.'
The text mentions this hadith as the basis for the opposing view.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) The opponent's hadith is munkar (denounced) by Yahya ibn Ma'in, so it cannot be used as evidence. (2) Even if authentic, it is interpreted as advice out of compassion, not a ruling of invalidation.
Also, rational arguments: no passage between eye and throat; only trace not substance enters; analogy with tasting medicine or feeling coolness after bathing.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi, the fast remains valid and no qada is required. Under Malik/Ahmad, the fast is invalidated and must be made up.
This difference affects millions of Muslims who use kohl while fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو اكتحل أو قبل، بخلاف الإنزال به أو دخل حلقه غبارٌ أو ذباب وهو ذاكر لصومه أو اكتحل: أي الاكتحال لا يفسد الصوم أيضاً؛ لما روي عن عائشة لها أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم اكتحل وهو صائم، رواه الدار قطني، ولا فرق بين أن يجد طعمه في حلقه أو لم يجد، وكذا لو بزق ووجد لونه في الأصح، وقال مالك وأحمد: وجد طعمه في حلقه يفسد؛ لما روي أنه عليه أمر بالإثمد المروح عند النوم، وقال: "ليتقِه الصائم"، ولنا: ما روينا من حديث عائشة لها ؛ ولأنه ليس بين العين والدماغ مسلك والدمع يخرج بالترشح كالعرق، وما يجده في حلقه أثر الكحل لا عينه فلا يضره كمن دق الدواء ووجد طعمه في حلقه؛ إذ لا يمكن الامتناع عنه وكذا من اغتسل فوجد برد الماء في باطنه لا يفطر؛ لأن المفطر إنما هو الداخل من المنافذ، وما روياه منكر قاله يحيى بن معين، فلا الاحتجاج به، ولئن صح فهو محمول على أنه عليه قال ذلك شفقة عليهم؛ لاحتمال أنه عليه عرف في الإمحمد [؟] صفة لا توافق الصائم كالحرارة ونحوه.
Or he applied kohl or kissed, unlike emission due to it, or dust or a fly entered his throat while he was mindful of his fast. As for applying kohl: that applying kohl does not break the fast also; because it was narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting, recorded by al-Dar Qutni, and there is no difference between finding its taste in his throat or not finding it. Similarly, if he spat and found its color, according to the most correct view. Malik and Ahmad said: If he finds its taste in his throat, it breaks the fast; because it was narrated that he (peace be upon him) commanded using perfumed kohl at sleep and said: 'The fasting person should avoid it.' And our proof: what we narrated from the hadith of Aisha, and because there is no passage between the eye and the brain, and tears emerge by exudation like sweat. What he finds in his throat is the trace of kohl, not its substance, so it does not harm him, like one who grinds medicine and finds its taste in his throat, as he cannot avoid it. Similarly, one who bathes and finds the coolness of water in his interior does not break his fast, because the invalidating thing is only what enters through orifices. What they narrated is munkar (denounced) according to Yahya ibn Ma'in, so it cannot be used as evidence. Even if it were authentic, it is interpreted that he said that out of compassion for them, because it is possible that he knew in al-Ithmid a quality incompatible with fasting, like heat and the like.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute concerning the use of kohl during fasting. The matn states the basic ruling: applying kohl does not break the fast, and kissing also does not break the fast unless accompanied by emission. The commentator then elaborates on kohl. He affirms that applying kohl does not invalidate the fast, providing the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet ﷺ applied kohl while fasting. The ruling applies equally whether the taste is felt or not, and even if the color appears in saliva. He then presents the opposing view of Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who hold that finding the taste of kohl in the throat breaks the fast, relying on a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ commanded using perfumed kohl and said the fasting person should avoid it. The Hanafi response is two-fold: first, the hadith they cite is munkar (weak/anomalous) according to Yahya ibn Ma'in, so it is not admissible as evidence. Second, even if it were authentic, it would be interpreted as a recommendation to avoid irritating substances, not as a ruling of invalidation. The Hanafis also provide rational arguments: there is no direct passage between the eye and the throat; tears exit by exudation like sweat, not through an open channel; and what reaches the throat is only the trace, not the substance, so it is analogous to tasting medicine or feeling coolness after bathing, which do not break the fast. The fast is only invalidated by substances entering through open orifices intentionally. Thus, the Hanafi position is that kohl does not break the fast under any circumstances.

Key Terms
الإمحمد [؟] (al-ithmid?): A type of kohl (antimony) used for the eyes.
اكتحل (iktahala): Applied kohl to the eyes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a fasting person who applies kohl and later finds its taste in his throat?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid, but kohl is disliked.
C) The fast remains valid with no effect.
D) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that kohl does not break the fast, regardless of taste reaching the throat, based on prophetic action, anatomical reasoning, and critical evaluation of opposing hadith.

Kissing Without Emission Does Not Break the Fast
Ruling that kissing the spouse without ejaculation does not invalidate the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kissing during fasting and explain the difference in consequence when ejaculation occurs.

Core Idea
Kissing without emission is permissible; ejaculation requires qada but not kaffarah.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a common question: does kissing one's spouse break the fast? The answer depends on whether ejaculation occurs. We will explore the textual proof and the nuanced reasoning behind the distinction between this ruling and other family law rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Here, it is a fasting person kissing his spouse without ejaculation. The ruling is that it does not break the fast.
The key condition is 'without ejaculation'.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith: the Prophet (PBUH) permitted kissing and cupping for the fasting person. This is recorded by al-Dar Qutni as authentic.
This is a zanni al-thubut hadith, but its dalala is clear permission.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit permission (rukhsa) indicates permissibility and non-invalidation. Since the proof is permission, the default ruling is that it does not break the fast.
The Hanafi rule: permission implies non-prohibition and non-invalidation.
4
Note the contrast with other rulings
The author contrasts this with the rulings of establishing affinity (musahara) and taking back a divorced wife (ruj'a), which are established even by kissing with desire without emission. Those rulings focus on the cause leading to intercourse, whereas fasting focuses on the satisfaction of desire.
Different legal objectives lead to different rulings for the same act.
5
Evaluate the consequence of ejaculation
If kissing leads to ejaculation, the fast is broken, but only qada (making up) is required, not kaffarah (expiation), because there is no actual penetration.
Kaffarah is attached to penetrative intercourse, not mere emission from direct contact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: "أو "قبل" يعني ولم ينزل لا يفطر؛ لما روى أبو سعيد الخدري أنه عليه رخص في القبلة للصائم والحجامة، رواه الدار قطني وقال رواته ثقات بخلاف المصاهرة والرجعة، فإنهما يثبتان بالقبلة بالشهوة وكذا بالمس وإن لم ينزل؛ لأن الحكم فيها أدير على السبب المفضي للوقاع وهنا على قضاء الشهوة، ولهذا لو أنزل بالقبلة لا يثبت به حكم المصاهرة ويفسد به الصوم ولو أنزل بقبلة فعليه القضاء؛ لوجود معنى الجماع وهو الإنزال بالمباشرة دون الكفارة؛ لعدم الإيلاج
And his (the author's) statement: 'or kissed' means he did not emit, and it does not break the fast; because Abu Sa'id al-Khudri narrated that he (peace be upon him) permitted kissing for the fasting person and cupping, recorded by al-Dar Qutni who said its narrators are trustworthy. This is unlike the establishment of affinity (musahara) and taking back a divorced wife (ruj'a), for these are established by kissing with desire and similarly by touching even without emission, because the ruling in those matters is based on the cause that leads to intercourse, whereas here it is based on the satisfaction of desire. For this reason, if he emits due to kissing, the ruling of affinity does not become established, but the fast is broken. And if he emits from kissing, upon him is qada (making up) because of the meaning of intercourse, which is emission through direct contact, but without kaffarah due to lack of penetration.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning kissing during fasting. The commentator explains the matn statement 'or kissed', specifying that it means kissing without ejaculation, and that it does not break the fast. The evidence is the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet ﷺ permitted kissing and cupping for the fasting person. This hadith is deemed authentic by al-Dar Qutni. The commentator then draws a contrast with the rulings of establishing affinity (musahara) and taking back a divorced wife (ruj'a), which are established by kissing with desire even without emission, because those rulings are based on the act that typically leads to intercourse. However, for the fast, the ruling is based on the satisfaction of desire, not just the act. Therefore, if kissing leads to ejaculation, the fast is broken, but even then only qada is required, not kaffarah, because there is no actual penetration. This shows the nuanced distinction the Hanafi school makes: the invalidation of the fast due to ejaculation from direct contact requires qada but not kaffarah, as the full rigors of intercourse (which require both qada and kaffarah) are attached to actual penetration.

Key Terms
قبلة (qubla): Kissing, specifically between spouses.
إنزال (inzal): Ejaculation of semen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person kisses his spouse and emits semen, what is the required action according to the Hanafi school?
A) Qada only, without kaffarah.
B) Qada and kaffarah.
C) Nothing; the fast is still valid.
D) Only kaffarah, without qada.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kissing without emission does not break the fast; only ejaculation from direct contact requires qada without kaffarah.
