Dust, Fly, and Unavoidable Substances in Fasting
Ruling on involuntary entry of substances during fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on involuntary entry of substances into the throat during fasting and distinguish between what breaks the fast and what does not based on avoidability.

Core Idea
Unavoidable substances like dust do not break the fast; avoidable ones like rain do.

A common concern for fasting individuals is what happens if something enters the throat unintentionally. The Hanafi school provides a clear distinction based on whether the substance could have been avoided. This lesson unpacks that distinction, examining the rulings for dust, flies, tears, sweat, blood, rain, and snow, and the critical condition of being mindful of the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognise that the ruling concerns involuntary entry of substances into the throat while fasting. The key is whether the fasting person is mindful (dhakir) of their fast.
Mindful means aware and intending to fast; forgetfulness (nisyan) changes the ruling.
2
Classify the substance by avoidability
Substances divide into two categories: those one can normally avoid (e.g., tears, sweat, rain, snow, blood) and those that are generally unavoidable (e.g., dust, flies, smoke).
The text explicitly lists tears, sweat, blood from nosebleed, rain, and snow as avoidable.
3
Apply the ruling to avoidable substances
If a mindful fasting person experiences entry of an avoidable substance, the fast is invalidated (fasad) because they could have taken precautions (e.g., closing the mouth).
The commentator emphasises 'taysir t…' (ease of closing the mouth) as the rationale.
4
Apply the ruling to unavoidable substances
If the substance is unavoidable (dust, fly, smoke) and the person is mindful, the fast remains valid because one cannot reasonably prevent it. This is analogous to the ruling on smoke.
The analogy (qiyas) to smoke is explicit in the commentary.
5
Note the effect of forgetfulness
If the fasting person is forgetful (nasi) at the time of entry, the fast is not broken regardless of the substance, as the state of forgetfulness removes the intent to break the fast.
The phrase 'while mindful' implies that forgetfulness takes priority in leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو دخل حلقه غبارٌ أو ذباب وهو ذاكر لصومه، أما لو دخل حلقه دموعه أو عرقه أو دم رعافه أو مطر أو ثلج فسد صومه؛ لتيسر طبق فمه وفتحه أحياناً مع الاحتراز عن الدخول، وقيد بالدخول للاحتراز عن الإدخال ولهذا صرحوا بأن الاحتواء على المبخرة مفسد، وليس كشم الورد والمسك؛ لوضوح الفرق بين هواء تطيب بريح المسك وشبهه وبين جوهر دخان وصل إلى جوفه بفعله. وقوله : "وهو ذاكر "لصومه" جملة حالية يشير إلى أنه إن كان ناسيا لصومه لا يفسد بالطريق الأولى. (مسكين، فتح) لصومه: لا يفسد لعدم استطاعته الامتناع عنه فأشبه الدخان بخلاف المطر والثلج في الأصح. (ع)
Or dust or a fly entered his throat while he was mindful of his fast. If his tears, sweat, blood from nosebleed, rain, or snow enter his throat, his fast is invalidated, because it is easy to close his mouth and open it sometimes while taking precautions against entry. And he qualified with 'entry' to distinguish from 'insertion', and for this reason they explicitly stated that inhaling smoke from a burner invalidates the fast, and it is not like smelling rose or musk, due to the clear difference between air perfumed with musk and the like and the essence of smoke that reaches his stomach by his action. And his statement: 'while he is mindful of his fast' is a circumstantial clause indicating that if he was forgetful of his fast, it does not break the fast by first priority. (From Miskeen, Fath) 'His fast' does not break because he cannot avoid it, so it resembles smoke, unlike rain and snow according to the most correct view. (From 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on substances that may enter the throat involuntarily. The matn states that if dust or a fly enters the throat while the fasting person is mindful of their fast, the fast remains valid. The commentator then explains that if tears, sweat, blood from a nosebleed, rain, or snow enter the throat, the fast is broken. The reason for the difference is that one can generally avoid these things by closing the mouth or taking precautions, whereas dust and flies are often unavoidable. The passage also clarifies that the qualification 'while mindful' implies that if a person is forgetful, the entry does not break the fast even more so. The commentary analogizes dust and fly to smoke, which is similarly unavoidable. The ruling distinguishes between the substance itself and the action of the fasting person: involuntary entry of unavoidable matter does not break the fast, but voluntary inhalation or insertion does. This is a key Hanafi distinction based on the ability to avoid.

Key Terms
غبار (ghubar): Dust particles in the air.
ذاكر لصومه (dhakir li-sawmihi): Mindful of one's fasting; knowing one is fasting and having intention.
دخول (dukhul): Involuntary entry of a substance into the throat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following would break a mindful fasting person's fast?
A) Involuntarily swallowing a fly.
B) Inhaling dust from a dusty road.
C) A few drops of rain entering the throat despite trying to close the mouth.
D) Smelling the fragrance of a rose.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, involuntary entry of unavoidable substances (dust, fly, smoke) does not break the fast, but avoidable substances (tears, sweat, blood, rain, snow) do; forgetfulness precludes nullification entirely.

Swallowing Small vs Large Food Residue Between Teeth: Hanafi Ruling and Dispute
Ruling on food residue between teeth with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing food residue between teeth, distinguish between small and large amounts by the chickpea measure, and explain Zufar's objection and the majority's response.

Core Idea
A small amount of food residue between teeth does not break the fast; a large amount does.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a common situation during fasting: inadvertently swallowing food left between teeth. The Hanafi school distinguishes by quantity, using the size of a chickpea as the dividing line. Imam Zufar disagreed, arguing that any amount breaks the fast. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swallowing food residue between teeth. The ruling: if little, fast valid; if much, fast broken.
2
Locate the evidence (ta'lil)
The small amount is unavoidable (la yumkin al-ihtiraz), so it is treated like saliva. The large amount can be avoided.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar says both cases break the fast because the mouth is considered external.
4
Understand the majority's response
The mouth is not like external; small residue is a follower of teeth.
5
Apply the distinction measure
The chickpea (hummusa) is the dividing line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أكل ما بين أسنانه إن كان قليلاً؛ لعدم إمكان الاحتراز عنه، فإنه يبقى القليل بين الأسنان عادة، وإن كان كثيراً يفطر، وقال زفر: يفطر في الوجهين؛ لأن الفم له حكم الظاهر فيكون داخلاً من الخارج، ولنا: أن القليل لا يمكن للامتناع عنه عادة فصار تبعاً لأسنانه بمنزلة ريقه والكثير يمكن الاحتراز عنه، والفاصل بينهما قدر الحمصة فما دونها قليل، وإن أخذه بيده وأخرجه ثم أكله ينبغي أن يفسد صومه كما روي عن محمد أن الصائم إذا ابتلع سمسمة بين أسنانه لا يفسد صومه، ولو ابتلعها من خارج ابتداء يفسد، ولو مضغها لا يفسد؛ لأنها تتلاشى، ولو جمع ريقه في فيه ثم ابتلعه لم يفطر ويكره، ولو أخرجه ثم ابتلعه يفطر كريق وغيره.
Or he ate what is between his teeth: if it is little, due to the impossibility of avoiding it, for a small amount usually remains between the teeth; if it is much, it breaks the fast. Zufar said: It breaks in both cases, because the mouth has the ruling of an external part, so it is an entry from outside. Our proof: the little cannot be avoided habitually, so it becomes a follower of the teeth, like his saliva, while the large amount can be avoided. The dividing line between them is the amount of a chickpea; what is less than that is little. And if he takes it out with his hand and then eats it, it should break his fast. Similarly, it is narrated from Muhammad that if a fasting person swallows a sesame seed from between his teeth, his fast is not broken, but if he swallows it from outside initially, it breaks. If he chews it, it does not break because it disintegrates. If he collects his saliva in his mouth and then swallows it, it does not break his fast but is disliked. If he takes it out and then swallows it, it breaks, like other than saliva.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on swallowing food particles left between teeth after eating. The matn states that if the amount is small, it does not break the fast, but if it is large, it does. The commentator explains the reason: a small amount is unavoidable because remnants normally stay between teeth, while a large amount can be avoided by rinsing. He then presents the view of Zufar, who holds that even a small amount breaks the fast because the mouth is considered an external opening, so any food that enters from outside (even between teeth) is an entry. The Hanafi response is that a small amount is treated like saliva; it is not considered an external intake because it is a normal residue. The dividing line is the size of a chickpea (hummusa): less than that is small, equal or more is large. The commentary also provides further rulings: if one deliberately takes out the residue with his hand and then eats it, the fast is broken. Swallowing a small seed from between teeth does not break the fast, but if taken from outside initially, it does. Chewing a small food particle does not break it because it dissolves. Collecting saliva and swallowing it does not break the fast but is disliked; if saliva is taken out of the mouth and then swallowed, it breaks the fast, as it is now treated as an external substance. This shows the nuanced approach of the Hanafi school in distinguishing between what can be avoided and what is habitual and unavoidable.

Key Terms
الحصة (al-himsah): A chickpea; used as a measure for the amount of food residue that distinguishes small from large.
تبعاً لأسنانه (taba'an li-asnanihi): As a follower of his teeth; meaning the food is considered attached to the teeth, like a part of the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the dividing line between a small and large amount of food residue between teeth?
A) A grain of rice
B) A chickpea
C) A sesame seed
D) A fingertip

Key Takeaway
A small amount (less than a chickpea) of food residue between teeth does not break the fast because it is unavoidable and considered a follower of the teeth.

Swallowing Food Residue After Removing It: Additional Rulings
Ruling on deliberate consumption of residue and saliva

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when swallowing food residue after intentionally removing it breaks the fast, and apply rulings for chewing, swallowing sesame seeds, and saliva management.

Core Idea
If you remove food residue and then swallow it, the fast is broken; chewing small particles that dissolve is fine.

This continuation from the same passage addresses scenarios beyond inadvertent swallowing. If a fasting person takes out food residue with his hand and then eats it, the fast is broken. Similarly, swallowing a sesame seed from between teeth is fine, but from outside the mouth it breaks the fast. Chewing small particles that dissolve is permitted. Even collecting and swallowing saliva is allowed but disliked, but if saliva is taken out of the mouth and then swallowed, it breaks the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case: taking out residue and eating it
The ruling: fast is broken because it becomes deliberate intake after removal from the mouth.
2
Understand the sesame seed example
Swallowing from between teeth: valid; from outside: invalid; chewing: valid because the seed disintegrates.
3
Analyze the saliva case
Collecting and swallowing within mouth: valid but makruh; taking out and swallowing: breaks fast.
4
Extract the general principle
Deliberate ingestion of something that was outside the mouth breaks the fast; unavoidable internal residues do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أكل ما بين أسنانه إن كان قليلاً؛ لعدم إمكان الاحتراز عنه، فإنه يبقى القليل بين الأسنان عادة، وإن كان كثيراً يفطر، وقال زفر: يفطر في الوجهين؛ لأن الفم له حكم الظاهر فيكون داخلاً من الخارج، ولنا: أن القليل لا يمكن للامتناع عنه عادة فصار تبعاً لأسنانه بمنزلة ريقه والكثير يمكن الاحتراز عنه، والفاصل بينهما قدر الحمصة فما دونها قليل، وإن أخذه بيده وأخرجه ثم أكله ينبغي أن يفسد صومه كما روي عن محمد أن الصائم إذا ابتلع سمسمة بين أسنانه لا يفسد صومه، ولو ابتلعها من خارج ابتداء يفسد، ولو مضغها لا يفسد؛ لأنها تتلاشى، ولو جمع ريقه في فيه ثم ابتلعه لم يفطر ويكره، ولو أخرجه ثم ابتلعه يفطر كريق وغيره.
Or he ate what is between his teeth: if it is little, due to the impossibility of avoiding it, for a small amount usually remains between the teeth; if it is much, it breaks the fast. Zufar said: It breaks in both cases, because the mouth has the ruling of an external part, so it is an entry from outside. Our proof: the little cannot be avoided habitually, so it becomes a follower of the teeth, like his saliva, while the large amount can be avoided. The dividing line between them is the amount of a chickpea; what is less than that is little. And if he takes it out with his hand and then eats it, it should break his fast. If he takes it out and then swallows it, it breaks, like other than saliva.

The commentator explains the reason: a small amount is unavoidable because remnants normally stay between teeth, while a large amount can be avoided by rinsing. What is the Hanafi ruling on a fasting person who collects saliva in his mouth and then swallows it?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast is broken but no qada.
C) The fast is valid but it is disliked.

Vomiting During Fasting: Passive vs Intentional and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on vomiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for vomiting during fasting and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the criterion for breaking the fast.

Core Idea
Passive vomiting does not break the fast; intentional vomiting does, but the return of vomit is disputed between quantity and action.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on vomiting while fasting. The primary rule is clear: if vomiting is involuntary (dhara'ahu), the fast remains valid; if it is induced (istaqa'a), it breaks. However, an intra-madhhab dispute arises over the return of vomit: does the quantity (filling the mouth) matter or the action of returning? We will explore the evidence and the agreed and disagreed cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and base ruling
The matn states: passive vomiting that returns on its own does not break the fast; intentional return or induced vomiting requires qada. Identify these two scenarios.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'Man dhar'ahu al-qay'...' distinguishes passive and intentional vomiting. It is recorded by Abu Dawood and deemed trustworthy.
The term 'dhara'ahu' means overcome by vomiting.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf considers quantity (ml' al-fam) as the criterion for breaking when vomit returns; Muhammad considers the action of returning (i'aada) as the criterion.
Both rely on the same hadith but interpret it differently.
4
Identify agreed and disagreed cases
Large amount returning or returned breaks by agreement; small amount returning does not break by agreement. Disagreement: returning a small amount (Abu Yusuf: no break; Muhammad: breaks) and natural return of a large amount (Abu Yusuf: breaks; Muhammad: no break).
This is the core of the dispute.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'al-sahih qawl al-tarafayn' meaning both views are valid in their contexts. The relied-upon view applies both criteria: quantity for natural return, action for intentional return.
In practice, later Hanafi fatwa often adopts Abu Yusuf's quantity criterion for natural return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو قاء وعاد لم يفطر، وإن أعاده أو استقاء أو ابتلع حصاةً أو حديداً قضى فقط. أو قاء وعاد: أي لا يفسد صومه أيضاً لو قاء وعاد القيء؛ لقوله عليه: "من ذرعه القيء فليس عليه القضاء، استقاء عمداً فليقض"، رواه أبو داود وقال الدار قطني رواته ثقات ويستوي فيه ملء الفم وما دونه، وقال أبو يوسف: إن عاد وكان ملء الفم يفسد، والصحيح قول الطرفين. (فتح) لم يفطر: جواب للمسائل المذكورة من عند قوله: "فإن أكل". (ع) وإن أعاده أو استقاء: يوضحه ما في "الهداية": "وقيء كثير عاد أو أعيد يفسد لا القليل في الحالين" أي إذا عاد القيء فالمعتبر عند أبي يوسف الكثرة يعني ملء الفم، وعند محمد: يعتبر الصنيع أي الإعادة، ففي إعادة الكثير يفسد اتفاقاً، وفي عود القليل لا يفسد اتفاقاً، وفي إعادة القليل لا يفسد عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، وفي عود الكثير يفسد عند أبي يوسف لا عند محمد. (فتح) أو استقاء: أي تكلف في القيء سواء كان ملء الفم أولا.
Or he vomited and it returned: it does not break the fast. But if he returns it (intentionally) or induces vomiting, or swallows a pebble or iron, he makes up only (qada). "Or he vomited and it returned" means his fast is not broken also if he vomited and the vomit returned; because of his saying (peace be upon him): "Whoever is overcome by vomiting has no qada upon him; whoever intentionally induces vomiting must make up." Recorded by Abu Dawood and al-Dar Qutni said its narrators are trustworthy. And it is equal whether it fills the mouth or less. Abu Yusuf said: If it returns and it fills the mouth, it breaks the fast. The correct view is the statement of both parties (i.e., the difference stands). (Fath) "It does not break" is the answer for the mentioned issues from his statement "For he ate". (Ayni) "And if he returns it or induces vomiting" is clarified in al-Hidayah: "Much vomit that returns or is returned breaks the fast, not little in both cases." That is, when vomit returns, the criterion according to Abu Yusuf is quantity (i.e., filling the mouth); according to Muhammad, the criterion is the action (i.e., the returning). So in returning much, it breaks by agreement; in the return of a little, it does not break by agreement; in returning a little, it does not break according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad; in returning much, it breaks according to Abu Yusuf but not according to Muhammad. (Fath) "Or induced vomiting" means he exerted himself in vomiting, whether it fills the mouth or not.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on vomiting during fasting. The matn states that if a person vomits and the vomit returns on its own (without action), the fast is not broken. However, if a person deliberately returns the vomit or induces vomiting, then the fast is broken and only qada is required. The commentator then elaborates with the hadith: "Whoever is overcome by vomiting has no qada; whoever intentionally induces vomiting must make up." This hadith is recorded by Abu Dawood and deemed trustworthy by al-Dar Qutni. The ruling applies whether the vomit fills the mouth or less. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Yusuf holds that if the vomit returns on its own and it is a large amount (filling the mouth), it breaks the fast; if it is a small amount, it does not. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the determining factor is the action of the person: if the person deliberately returns the vomit, it breaks; if it returns naturally, it does not. The commentator notes that the correct view (as per al-Hidayah) is that there is agreement in some cases: a large amount that returns or is returned breaks the fast; a small amount that returns does not break. The disagreement is only in the mixed cases: returning a small amount (Abu Yusuf: no break; Muhammad: breaks) and the return of a large amount (Abu Yusuf: breaks; Muhammad: no break). The commentary from 'Ayni clarifies that "or induced vomiting" means exerting oneself to vomit, regardless of quantity. This shows the detailed reasoning in Hanafi fiqh regarding intentionality and the ability to avoid.

Key Terms
ذرعه (dhara'ahu): Overcome by vomiting involuntarily.
استقاء (istaqa'a): Intentionally induced vomiting.
ملء الفم (ml' al-fam): Filling the mouth – the quantity criterion used by Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a fasting person vomits a small amount and it returns on its own?
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid.
C) The fast is broken only if the vomit filled the mouth.
D) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.

Key Takeaway
Passive vomiting does not break the fast; induced vomiting or intentional return of vomit does, but the return of vomit involves a valid intra-Hanafi dispute over quantity vs action.

Swallowing Inedible Objects: Qada Only, No Kaffarah (Hanafi vs Maliki)
Ruling on swallowing inedible objects with qada-only and Maliki dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on swallowing inedible objects while fasting and distinguish it from the Maliki position.

Core Idea
Swallowing a non-nourishing object breaks the fast but requires only qada, not kaffarah, because the meaning of al-fitr (nourishment) is absent.

When a fasting person intentionally swallows something that is not normally eaten, like a pebble or iron, does the fast break? And if so, what is required—just making up the day, or also an expiation (kaffarah)? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between the form and the meaning of breaking the fast. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains that while the act of swallowing invalidates the fast (so qada is needed), the absence of nutritional benefit means kaffarah is not required. We will trace the i'lla (cause) behind this ruling and explore the dispute with Imam Malik, who holds kaffarah necessary for any intentional swallowing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the matn: 'Or he swallowed a pebble or iron: he makes up only.' Note that the fast is invalidated (because something enters the stomach intentionally), but only qada is mandated—no kaffarah.
The text explicitly states 'qada faqat' (only make-up).
2
Understand the rationale (i'lla) for negating kaffarah
The commentator explains that the 'meaning of breaking the fast' (ma'na al-fitr) is the arrival of nourishment to the stomach for the benefit of the body. Since inedible objects provide no nutrition, the offense is 'incomplete' (qasurat al-jinaya), so kaffarah is lifted.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) based on the i'lla of nourishment.
3
Note the condition of repetition
The text adds that if someone repeatedly swallows such objects with the intent to break the fast, kaffarah may become obligatory according to the fatwa. This shows that the ruling can change if the act is done willfully to violate the fast.
The principle: repeated intentional sin may trigger the full penalty.
4
Compare with the Maliki position
Imam Malik holds that the obligation of kaffarah does not depend on the swallowed item being nourishing. For him, any intentional swallowing of a foreign object into the stomach breaks the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is clear: Hanafi= qada only; Maliki= qada+kaffarah.
5
Apply to similar cases (extension by qiyas)
The passage extends the ruling to smoke, flour, rice, dough, salt, leaves, and clay—each depending on whether the substance is normally eaten or nourishing. For example, salt requires kaffarah only if habitually eaten alone; Armenian clay requires kaffarah because it is used medicinally.
This shows how the i'lla of nourishment is applied consistently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ابتلع حصاةً أو حديداً قضى فقط. أو ابتلع حصاة إلخ: أي فعليه القضاء؛ لوجود صورة الفطر، ولا كفارة عليه في الكل؛ لعدم معنى الفطر وهو وصول الغذاء إلى جوفه لنفع البدن فقصرت الجناية فانتفت الكفارة، فعلى هذا لا تجب الكفارة في شرب الدخان واعلم أن كل ما انتفى فيه وجوب الكفارة محله إذا لم يقع منه مرة بعد أخرى لأجل قصد معصية إفساد الصوم فإن فعله وجبت على ما عليه الفتوى، وكذا لا تجب الكفارة في الدقيق والأرز والعجين إلا عند محمد وفي الملح لا تجب إلا إذا اعتاد أكله وحده، وعلى هذا أوراق الأشجار والنباتات كلها إن كانت تؤكل عادة تجب فيها وإلا لا، ولا تجب في الطين إلا الطين الأرمني؛ لأنه يتداوى به وقال مالك: لا يشترط كون المأكول غذاء في وجوب الكفارة. (فتح، عيني) أو حديداً أو حجراً أو تراباً أو شيئاً مما لا يتغذى به (ع) قضى فقط [ جواب للمسائل المذكورة بكلمة "وإن أعاده. (ع)] أي بلا وجوب الكفارة، وقال مالك: تجب الكفارة أيضاً في الابتلاع.
Or he swallowed a pebble or iron: he makes up only. "Or he swallowed a pebble etc.": meaning upon him is qada because the form of breaking the fast exists, but no kaffarah upon him in all such cases, because the meaning of breaking the fast (which is reaching of nourishment to the stomach for the benefit of the body) is absent, so the offense is incomplete and kaffarah is negated. Based on this, kaffarah is not obligatory for drinking smoke. And know that wherever the obligation of kaffarah is negated, its place is when it does not occur repeatedly for the purpose of intentionally breaking the fast. If he does that, it becomes obligatory according to the relied-upon fatwa. Similarly, kaffarah is not obligatory for flour, rice, or dough except according to Muhammad. For salt, it is not obligatory unless he has the habit of eating it alone. And accordingly for leaves of trees and plants: if they are normally eaten, kaffarah is obligatory; otherwise not. And kaffarah is not obligatory for clay except Armenian clay, because it is used medicinally. Malik said: It is not a condition that the eaten thing be nourishing for the obligation of kaffarah. (Fath, 'Ayni) "Or iron, or stone, or dust, or something that is not nourishing" (from 'Ayni) "He makes up only" (answer for the mentioned issues with the word 'and if he returns it') meaning without obligation of kaffarah. And Malik said: Kaffarah is also obligatory in swallowing such things.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for swallowing inedible objects like pebbles, iron, stones, dust, etc. The matn states that only qada (making up the fast) is required, not kaffarah (expiation). The commentator explains the rationale: although the action of swallowing (the form of breaking the fast) is present, the essential meaning of breaking the fast—reaching nourishment to the body for benefit—is absent. Since the object provides no nutrition, the offense is considered incomplete, and kaffarah is not obligatory. This ruling applies to all non-nourishing substances. The commentator then extends this to other cases: smoking, flour, rice, dough, salt, leaves, and clay. For salt, kaffarah is required only if the person habitually eats it alone. For leaves and plants, it depends on whether they are normally eaten. For clay, only Armenian clay (used medicinally) incurs the obligation. The commentary also notes a condition: if someone repeatedly swallows such things with the intent to break the fast, then according to the relied-upon fatwa, kaffarah may become obligatory because the action is done willfully to violate the fast. The passage then presents the opposing view of Imam Malik, who does not require the object to be nourishing for kaffarah to be obligatory; for him, any intentional swallowing of a foreign object into the stomach breaks the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on the underlying rationale ('illa) of nutritional benefit for the obligation of kaffarah.

Key Terms
قضى فقط (qada faqat): Only making up the fast (one day) is required; no additional expiation.
الغذاء (al-ghidha'): Nourishment; food that provides benefit to the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fasting person intentionally swallows a pebble. According to the Hanafi school, what is the required action?
A) Qada only.
B) Qada and kaffarah.
C) No action required because pebbles are not food.
D) Kaffarah only.

Key Takeaway
Swallowing a non-nourishing object invalidates the fast (so qada is required) but does not trigger kaffarah because the i'lla of nourishment is absent.

Sexual Intercourse During Fasting: Qada, Kaffarah, and the Dispute on Women's Liability
Ruling that sexual intercourse breaks the fast and requires qada and kaffarah with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which sexual intercourse breaks the fast in the Hanafi school and explain why both parties are equally liable for kaffarah, evaluating the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, both man and woman bear equal kaffarah for intercourse during Ramadan, based on the general wording of the hadith.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the critical issue of sexual intercourse during the fast of Ramadan. While the ruling that it breaks the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah is clear, the commentary explores important conditions and a major inter-madhhab dispute regarding the woman's liability. We will examine the conditions that make the act subject to kaffarah, the evidence from the hadith, and why the Hanafi school holds both parties equally responsible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed and the initial ruling: sexual intercourse (whether active or passive) invalidates the fast and requires qada and kaffarah.
2
Specify the conditions for full liability
List the conditions: (a) the partner is a living human, (b) the act is through one of the two passages, (c) the partner is a fully desired person (adult human). If conditions not met, only qada may be required if ejaculation occurs.
Note that ejaculation itself does not obligate kaffarah unless it occurs through full intercourse with a living human.
3
Locate the dalil for kaffarah
Identify the hadith of the Bedouin as the evidence for kaffarah, and note the key phrase 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan...' which uses the general word 'man' (whoever).
The Hanafi school relies on the general wording of this hadith to include both men and women.
4
State the Hanafi position on gender liability
The Hanafi school holds that both the man and woman are equally obligated for qada and kaffarah because the act is mutual and the hadith does not differentiate.
Consent is not a factor in the Hanafi view; the mere occurrence of the act triggers the obligation.
5
Present the opposing school's view
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools differentiate based on consent: if the woman is coerced, no kaffarah; if she consents, she is liable. Some Shafi'i views exempt her entirely.
This difference leads to different practical outcomes.
6
Analyze the Hanafi response and thamarah al-khilaf
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the fast is broken by the act itself, and the ruling is based on the meeting of circumcised parts, not on consent. The practical consequence is that in the Hanafi school, both parties always owe kaffarah; in other schools, the woman may be exempt if coerced.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن جامع أو جومع أو أكل أو شرب غذاءً أو دواءً. ومن جامع الخ: لابد أن يكون المحل مشتهى على الكمال، فلا تجب الكفارة لو جامع بهيمة أو ميتة ولو أنزل، أو صغيرة لا تشتهي وكذا إذا فخذ أو بطن أو لمس أو استمنى بكفه أو بمباشرة فاحشة ولو بين المرأتين وأنزل في الجميع، ولو لم ينزل لم يفطر، وأن يكون الجماع في أحد السبيلين من إنسان لاجني فيقضي ويكفر، أما القضاء فإنه مأمور بالصوم وقد فوّته فيقضيه، وأما الكفارة فلحديث الأعرابي كما يجيء، سواء أنزل أو لم ينزل؛ لأن أحكام الجماع كالحد والاغتسال تتعلق بالتقاء الختانين، وفساد الصوم ووجوب الكفارة منها، وهذا الحكم سواء في الذكر والأنثى؛ لقوله عليه : من أفطر في رمضان فعليه ما على المظاهر ، رواه الدار قطني، وكلمة "من" تطلق على الذكر والأنثى، وقال الشافعي ومالك وأحمد: إنما تجب على المرأة إن طاوعته، وفي قول الشافعي: لا تجب عليها، وفي قول: تجب عليها أيضاً ويتحمل عنها الزوج. (عيني، فتح) أو جومع: ذكرا كان أو أنثى في أحد السبيلين في محل مشتهى على الكمال بأن يكون إنساناً حياً.
And whoever has intercourse or is had intercourse with, or eats or drinks nourishment or medicine. "And whoever has intercourse etc.": The place must be fully desired (complete sexual desire). So kaffarah is not obligatory if he has intercourse with an animal or a corpse even if he emits, or with a young girl who is not desired. Similarly, if he rubs thighs or belly, or touches, or masturbates with his hand, or engages in lewd direct contact even between two women, and emits in all cases, it does not break the fast unless he emits (i.e., if he does not emit, it does not break the fast). And the intercourse must be through one of the two passages of a living human being; then he makes up and gives kaffarah. As for qada: he was commanded to fast and he lost it, so he makes it up. As for kaffarah: because of the hadith of the Bedouin as will come. It is the same whether he emits or not, because the rulings of intercourse like the prescribed punishment and ritual bath are attached to the meeting of the circumcised parts, and the invalidation of the fast and obligation of kaffarah are among them. This ruling is the same for male and female because of his saying (peace be upon him): "Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan has upon him what the one who practices zihar has upon him," recorded by al-Dar Qutni, and the word "man" (whoever) applies to male and female. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: It is only obligatory upon the woman if she consented; according to one view of al-Shafi'i, it is not obligatory upon her; according to another view, it is obligatory upon her as well and the husband bears it for her. (Ayni, Fath) "Or is had intercourse with": whether male or female, through one of the two passages, in a place that is fully desired, i.e., a living human being.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage provides the comprehensive ruling on sexual intercourse during fasting. The matn states that intercourse (whether one is the agent or recipient) breaks the fast, as does eating or drinking nourishing or medicinal substances. The commentary specifies the conditions: the partner must be a living human being, the intercourse must be through the front or back passage, and the person must be someone who is normally desired (i.e., an adult human who is not an animal, corpse, or a child not yet at the age of desire). If these conditions are not met, such as intercourse with an animal or a corpse, even with ejaculation, kaffarah is not obligatory; only qada is required if ejaculation occurs. If there is no ejaculation, the fast is not broken at all (since the act itself is not considered full intercourse). For full intercourse (penetration of the private parts), the ruling is that it breaks the fast whether or not ejaculation occurs, because the rulings of intercourse (like hadd and ghusl) are tied to the meeting of the circumcised parts. The consequence is both qada and kaffarah. The evidence for kaffarah is the hadith of the Bedouin (which appears later in the text). The ruling applies equally to men and women based on the general wording of the hadith. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that kaffarah is only obligatory on the woman if she consented; some Shafi'i views say it is not obligatory on her at all, while another view says it is obligatory on her but the husband bears it for her. The Hanafi position, as presented, is that both are equally liable for kaffarah when they mutually engage in intercourse.

Key Terms
التقاء الختانين (iltiqa' al-khitanayn): The meeting of the two circumcised parts; a metaphor for actual penetration.
المظاهر (al-muzahir): One who declares zihar (a pre-Islamic form of divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a married couple who both intentionally engage in intercourse during the day in Ramadan?
A) Neither needs to do anything because it is mutual.
B) Both must make up the fast and give kaffarah.
C) Only the husband must give kaffarah; the wife only makes up the fast.
D) Only qada is required for both, no kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, both men and women are equally obligated to perform qada and kaffarah when they engage in sexual intercourse during a Ramadan fast, regardless of who initiated or whether they consented.

Rulings on Vomiting and Swallowing Non-Food Items While Fasting
Ruling with proof on vomiting and swallowing non-food items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for intentional vomiting and swallowing non-food items, identify the hadith evidence, and explain why qada is required but not kaffarah.

Core Idea
Swallowing a pebble breaks the fast only for qada; nutrition is the key to kaffarah.

This lesson tackles two common scenarios that challenge fasting: vomiting and swallowing objects that are not food. The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between what breaks the fast and what requires expiation. The key is whether the substance nourishes the body. You will learn the proof from the hadith, the internal Hanafi disagreement on quantity vs. action, and the difference with the Maliki school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
From the matn, find the ruling for each act: involuntary vomiting (no break), intentional vomiting or returning vomit (qada only), swallowing non-food items like pebbles or iron (qada only). Note that kaffarah is not mentioned.
The matn says 'قضى فقط' (qada only) for all these.
2
Locate the dalil
The matn cites the hadith: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting has no qada; whoever induces vomiting must make up.' This distinguishes voluntary from involuntary. The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and is considered trustworthy by al-Daraqutni.
The hadith is the primary evidence; it implies qada is the only requirement.
3
Trace the Hanafi legal reasoning
The Hanafi school uses the criterion of 'nutrition reaching the stomach' to determine kaffarah. Since non-food items do not nourish, the 'meaning of breaking fast' is absent, so kaffarah is negated. For vomiting, the hadith directly commands qada only.
This is a talil (rationale) based on the maqasid of fasting.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on returning vomit
Abu Yusuf considers the quantity (mouthful) decisive; Muhammad considers the action (intentional return) decisive. The text in al-Hidayah reconciles by saying 'the correct is the statement of the two sides,' meaning each applies in different contexts.
The practical difference: returning a small amount intentionally invalidates according to Muhammad but not Abu Yusuf.
5
Compare with the Maliki position
Imam Malik requires kaffarah for swallowing any item, regardless of nutrition. The Hanafi response is that kaffarah is tied to nourishment, so non-food items do not trigger it.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a pebble swallowed in Ramadan – Hanafi: qada only; Maliki: qada and kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أو قاء وعاد لم يفطر، وإن أعاده أو استقاء أو ابتلع حصاةً أو حديداً قضى فقط.
أو قاء وعاد: أي لا يفسد صومه أيضاً لو قاء وعاد القيء؛ لقوله عليه: "من ذرعه القيء فليس عليه القضاء، استقاء عمداً فليقض"، رواه أبو داود وقال الدار قطني رواته ثقات ويستوي فيه ملء الفم وما دونه، وقال أبو يوسف: إن عاد وكان ملء الفم يفسد، والصحيح قول الطرفين. (فتح) لم يفطر: جواب للمسائل المذكورة من عند قوله: "فإن أكل". (فتح) أو استقاء: أي تكلف في القيء سواء كان ملء الفم أولا. (فتح، عيني)
أو حديداً أو حجراً أو تراباً أو شيئاً مما لا يتغذى به (ع) قضى فقط أي بلا وجوب الكفارة، وقال مالك: تجب الكفارة أيضاً في الابتلاع. (مسكين)
Book of Fasting. Chapter: What invalidates fasting and what does not. Or he vomits and it returns, he does not break his fast. And if he returns it (intentionally) or induces vomiting or swallows a pebble or iron, he only makes up (qada). 'Or he vomits and it returns': meaning his fasting is also not invalidated if he vomits and the vomit returns, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever is overcome by vomit, there is no qada upon him; whoever induces vomiting intentionally, let him make up.' Reported by Abu Dawud, and al-Daraqutni said its narrators are trustworthy. And it is equal whether it is mouthful or less. Abu Yusuf said: if it returns and it is a mouthful, it invalidates. The correct is the statement of the two sides. (Fath) 'Did not break fast': answer to the mentioned issues from his saying 'for he ate'. (Ayni) 'And if he returns it or induces vomiting': clarified by what is in al-Hidayah: 'Much vomit that returns or is returned invalidates; little does not in both cases.' That is, when vomit returns, the criterion according to Abu Yusuf is quantity, i.e., mouthful; according to Muhammad, the criterion is the action, i.e., returning. So in returning much, it invalidates by agreement; in returning little, it does not invalidate by agreement; in returning little (intentionally), it does not invalidate according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad; in returning much (unintentionally), it invalidates according to Abu Yusuf not according to Muhammad. (Miskin) 'Or swallows a pebble etc.': meaning qada is incumbent on him due to the existence of the form of breaking fast, and no kaffarah is upon him in all these, because the meaning of breaking fast (arrival of nourishment to his stomach for benefit of body) is absent, so the violation is minor and kaffarah is negated. Thus, no kaffarah is obligatory in drinking smoke. Know that every case where obligation of kaffarah is negated, its place is when it does not occur repeatedly with the intention of sin of breaking the fast; if he does so, it becomes obligatory, according to the fatwa. Similarly, no kaffarah in flour, rice, and dough except according to Muhammad. And in salt, it is not obligatory unless he is accustomed to eat it alone. On this basis, leaves of trees and plants: if they are usually eaten, kaffarah is obligatory; otherwise not. And no kaffarah in clay except Armenian clay because it is used medicinally. Malik said: it is not a condition that the eaten item be nutritious for obligation of kaffarah. (Fath, Ayni) 'Or iron or stone or dust or something not nourishing' – (Ayni) qada only, meaning without obligation of kaffarah. Malik said: kaffarah is also obligatory in swallowing. (Miskin)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on what invalidates fasting. It covers two main issues: vomiting and swallowing non-food items. The matn states that if someone vomits and the vomit returns by itself, the fast is not broken. If he intentionally returns the vomit or induces vomiting, he only has to make up that day (qada), no kaffarah. The proof is the hadith narrated by Abu Dawud: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting has no qada; whoever induces vomiting intentionally must make up.' The commentary details an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding returning vomit: Abu Yusuf looks at quantity (mouthful), Muhammad looks at the action (intentional return). The correct view according to the text is the last mentioned, though it says 'the correct is the statement of the two sides' which may mean the combination of views. For swallowing non-food items like pebbles, iron, etc., the ruling is qada only, no kaffarah, because the criterion for kaffarah is the arrival of nutritious food to the stomach, which is absent here. The commentary extends this to other substances like smoke, flour, rice, dough, salt, leaves, clay, etc., with details about when kaffarah becomes due. It also mentions the difference with Imam Malik, who requires kaffarah for swallowing anything, regardless of nutritional value.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed fast on another day, required as compensation for invalidating the fast.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a Ramadan fast through intercourse or by eating/drinking intentionally according to some schools; Hanafi requires kaffarah only for intercourse in Ramadan.
استقاء (istiqa'): Deliberately inducing vomiting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone swallows a pebble intentionally during a Ramadan fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) The fast is valid and no penalty.
B) The fast is broken and requires qada and kaffarah.
C) The fast is broken and requires qada only.
D) The fast is broken and requires kaffarah only.

Key Takeaway
Non-nutritive items break the fast but only require qada; kaffarah is reserved for nutritional breaking of the fast.

Sawm — Fasting

8 min
Intentional Invalidation of Fast and the Obligation of Kaffarah
Explains the general ruling and its proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which intentional acts during Ramadan invalidate the fast and require both qada and kaffarah according to the Hanafi school, and explain the hadith evidence and the conditions that trigger the kaffarah.

Core Idea
Intentional intercourse, eating, and drinking invalidate the fast and require the same kaffarah as zihar: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty.

This lesson introduces the foundational rule that any intentional act of breaking the fast—whether by intercourse, eating, or drinking—obligates both making up the day (qada) and a heavy expiation (kaffarah). The Hanafi school extends this to all such acts based on a general hadith and analogy, unlike other schools. We will study the proof and the precise conditions that make the kaffarah binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidating acts
From the matn: intercourse (active or passive), eating, or drinking intentionally. These are the three categories that trigger both qada and kaffarah.
2
Understand the kaffarah requirement
The kaffarah is identical to that of zihar: freeing a slave, or if unable, fasting two consecutive months, or if unable, feeding sixty poor persons. The order is fixed, not choice.
Contrary to Maliki and Shafi'i, who allow choice.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of the bedouin (Abu Hurayra) narrates the Prophet's verdict on a man who had intercourse in Ramadan. The Hanafi school uses this and the general statement 'whoever breaks fast in Ramadan' to include eating and drinking.
The hadith is the primary dalil for the kaffarah and its order.
4
Note the conditions for kaffarah
The act must be intentional (ʿamdan). For intercourse, it must be with a living human being, in one of the two passages (penetration), and the place must be completely desired (not with an animal, dead body, or minor not desired).
These conditions derive from the commentary.
5
Recognize gender equality
The ruling applies equally to male and female, as the hadith uses the general term 'man' which includes women according to Hanafi usul.

Source Text

ومن جامع أو جومع أو أكل أو شرب غذاءً أو دواءً.
عمداً قضى وكفّر ككفّارة الظهار، ولا كفّارة بالإنزال فيما دون الفرج، وبإفساد عندنا خلافاً للشافعي.
ومن جامع الخ: لابد أن يكون المحل مشتهى على الكمال، فلا تجب الكفارة لو جامع بهيمة أو ميتة ولو أنزل، أو صغيرة لا تشتهي وكذا إذا فخذ أو بطن أو لمس أو استمنى بكفه أو بمباشرة فاحشة ولو بين المرأتين وأنزل في الجميع، ولو لم ينزل لم يفطر، وأن يكون الجماع في أحد السبيلين من إنسان لاجني فيقضي ويكفر، أما القضاء فإنه مأمور بالصوم وقد فوّته فيقضيه، وأما الكفارة فلحديث الأعرابي كما يجيء، سواء أنزل أو لم ينزل؛ لأن أحكام الجماع كالحد والاغتسال تتعلق بالتقاء الختانين، وفساد الصوم ووجوب الكفارة منها، وهذا الحكم سواء في الذكر والأنثى؛ لقوله عليه : من أفطر في رمضان فعليه ما على المظاهر ، رواه الدار قطني، وكلمة "من" تطلق على الذكر والأنثى، وقال الشافعي ومالك وأحمد: إنما تجب على المرأة إن طاوعته، وفي قول الشافعي: لا تجب عليها، وفي قول: تجب عليها أيضاً ويتحمل عنها الزوج. (عيني، فتح)
أو جومع: ذكرا كان أو أنثى في أحد السبيلين في محل مشتهى على الكمال بأن يكون إنساناً حياً. (ط،ع)
عمداً: حال كونه عامداً، وهذا القيد يرجع إلى جميع الثلاثة. (ع) قضى: صوم ذلك اليوم بلا خلاف. قضى وكفر : في محل الرفع؛ لأنه خبر من "جامع" وقال الشافعي: لا كفارة فيها أي في الأكل والشرب؛ لأنها تثبت في الوقاع بالنص على خلاف القياس، فلا يقاس عليه غيره، ولنا ما روينا من أفطر في رمضان" الحديث، وقوله: "ككفارة الظهار" يعني في الترتيب؛ لحديث أبي هريرة ه: جاء رجل إلى النبي عليه فقال: هلكت يا رسول الله ، قال : "وما أهلكك؟ قال: وقعت على امرأتي في رمضان قال: هل تجد ما تعتق"؟ قال: لا، قال: "هل تستطيع أن تصوم شهرين متتابعين؟ قال: لا، قال: "هل تجد ما تطعم ستين مسكينا"؟ قال: لا، ثم جلس فأتي النبي بعرق فيه تمر، فقال: تصدق بهذا، فقال: أعلى أفقر منّا، فما بين لابتيها أهل بيت أحوج من أهل بيتي، فضحك عليه حتى بدت أنيابه، خلافاً لمالك حيث يقول بالتخيير قياساً على كفارة اليمين، وأيضاً يقول بنفي التتابع، وخلافاً للشافعي حيث يقول بالتخيير. (فتح) ككفارة الظهار: يعني إن كان يجد رقبة فعليه تحرير رقبة، فإن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين، فإن عجز أطعم ستين مسكيناً. (مسكين فيما دون الفرج [ أي القبل والدبر كالتبطين والتفخيذ والاستمناء بالكف، بل يلزمه القضاء فقط. (ط، ع)] أي يجب القضاء بلا كفارة في الإنزال فيما دون الفرج مطلقاً، سواء كان بالتفخيذ أو بالدبر، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وعنه أنه إن وطئ في الدبر فعليهما الكفارة، وهو قولهما، وهو الأصح، وكذا الكفارة في الاستمناء بالكف؛ لعدم الجماع صورة، وهو إدخال الفرج في الفرج، ويجب القضاء لوجوده معنى من حيث إنه قضاء الشهوة واختلف في الاستمناء بالكف فقيل: يحرم لما روي أن ناكح اليد ملعون، وعن عطاء: سمعت قوماً يحشرون وأيديهم حبالى، فأظن أنهم هؤلاء، وقال سعيد بن جبير: عذب الله أمة كانوا يعبثون بمذاكيرهم، وقيل: إن قصد به تسكين الشهوة يرجى أن لا يكون عليه وبال. (عيني، مستخلص)
And whoever has intercourse or is had intercourse with, or eats or drinks nourishment or medicine. Intentionally, he makes up and pays kaffarah like the kaffarah of zihar. And no kaffarah for orgasm below the private parts. And regarding invalidation, according to us contrary to al-Shafi'i. The commentary: 'Whoever has intercourse etc.': The place must be completely desired, so no kaffarah if he has intercourse with an animal or dead body even if he orgasms, or with a minor who is not desired. Similarly, if he rubs thighs, or belly, or touches, or masturbates with his hand, or engages in obscene acts even between two women and orgasms in all, and if he does not orgasm, he does not break his fast. And the intercourse must be in one of the two passages from a living human; then he makes up and pays kaffarah. As for qada, he was commanded to fast and he missed it, so he makes it up. As for kaffarah, it is due to the hadith of the bedouin as will come. Whether he orgasms or not, because the rulings of intercourse like hudud and ghusl are attached to the meeting of the circumcised parts, and the invalidation of the fast and obligation of kaffarah are among them. This ruling is the same for male and female due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever breaks fast in Ramadan is obligated as the one who does zihar is obligated.' Reported by al-Daraqutni. And the word 'whoever' applies to male and female. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: it is only obligatory on the woman if she consented. And according to one opinion of al-Shafi'i: it is not obligatory on her; and in another: it is obligatory on her also and the husband bears it. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or is had intercourse with': male or female in one of the two passages in a completely desired place, that it be a living human. (Tahawi, Ayni) 'Intentionally': as a state of being intentional, and this condition refers to all three. (Ayni) 'He makes up': the fast of that day without disagreement. 'He makes up and pays kaffarah': in the nominative because it is the predicate of 'whoever has intercourse'. Al-Shafi'i said: no kaffarah in it, i.e., in eating and drinking, because kaffarah is established for intercourse by text contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized to anything else. And our proof is what we narrated: 'Whoever breaks fast in Ramadan' hadith, and his saying 'like the kaffarah of zihar' means in order, due to the hadith of Abu Hurayra: A man came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said: I am ruined, O Messenger of Allah. He said: 'What ruined you?' He said: I had intercourse with my wife in Ramadan. He said: 'Do you find to free a slave?' He said: No. He said: 'Can you fast two consecutive months?' He said: No. He said: 'Do you find to feed sixty poor?' He said: No. Then he sat, and a basket of dates was brought to the Prophet, so he said: 'Give this as charity.' He said: To one poorer than us? For between its two lava plains there is no family more needy than my family. The Prophet laughed until his molars appeared. Contrary to Malik who says choice by analogy to kaffarah of oath, and also says no continuity. And contrary to al-Shafi'i who says choice. (Fath) 'Like the kaffarah of zihar': meaning if he finds a slave, he must free a slave; if not, then fasting two consecutive months; if unable, then feeding sixty poor. (Miskin) 'Below the private parts [i.e., front and back, like thighing and masturbation with the hand, but qada is only required. (Tahawi, Ayni)] Meaning qada without kaffarah is obligatory for orgasm below the private parts absolutely, whether by thigh or by back. And this is a narration from Abu Hanifa; and from him that if he has intercourse in the back, kaffarah is obligatory on both, and that is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is the more correct. Similarly, kaffarah for masturbation with the hand, because there is no intercourse in form, which is insertion of the private part into the private part, but qada is obligatory due to its existence in meaning, in that it fulfills desire. And there is disagreement regarding masturbation with the hand: some say it is forbidden because it is narrated that the one who marries his hand is cursed. And from Ata: I heard a people being gathered with their hands pregnant, and I think they are these. And Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: Allah tortured a nation who played with their private parts. And it is said: if he intends to calm desire, it is hoped that there is no sin. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning acts that invalidate the fast and require both qada and kaffarah. The matn states that whoever has intercourse (or is had intercourse with) or eats or drinks intentionally must make up the fast and pay kaffarah like that of zihar. The commentary elaborates that for intercourse to trigger kaffarah, it must be with a living human being in one of the two passages (penetration), and the place must be completely desired. Acts like masturbation or thighing that lead to orgasm do not require kaffarah according to the preferred view, but qada is still required. The hadith of the bedouin (Abu Hurayra) is cited to establish the kaffarah and its order (freeing slave, fasting two months, feeding sixty). There is a cross-madhhab dispute: Shafi'i does not require kaffarah for eating/drinking, and Malik and Shafi'i differ on the order and whether it is choice or sequence. Also, regarding anal intercourse, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa in one narration says no kaffarah, but the Sahibayn say kaffarah, and this is considered the more correct view. The passage also discusses masturbation, prohibiting it but noting that if done to calm desire, it may be excused.

Key Terms
كفارة الظهار (kaffarat al-zihar): The expiation for zihar, which is the same as for breaking fast in Ramadan according to Hanafi: freeing a slave, or fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor.
التقاء الختانين (iltiqa' al-khitanayn): The meeting of the two circumcised parts, i.e., the glans disappearing in the partner's private part, which triggers full rulings of intercourse including kaffarah.
عمداً ('amdan): Intentionally; the act must be deliberate for kaffarah to be obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intentionally drinking water during a Ramadan fast?
A) The fast is broken and requires only qada.
B) The fast is broken and requires both qada and kaffarah like zihar.
C) The fast is valid, no penalty.
D) The fast is broken and requires kaffarah but not qada.

Key Takeaway
Intentional invalidation of the Ramadan fast by intercourse, eating, or drinking obligates both qada and the kaffarah of zihar, as proven by the hadith of the bedouin and the general principle of 'whoever breaks fast in Ramadan'.

Non-Penetrative Acts, Anal Intercourse, and Masturbation: Qada vs Kaffarah
Clarifies the boundary between acts requiring kaffarah and those requiring only qada, and discusses disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between penetrative intercourse (requires kaffarah) and non-penetrative acts like thighing and masturbation (requires only qada), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on anal intercourse and the prohibition of masturbation.

Core Idea
Only full penetration triggers the heavy kaffarah; other intentional orgasms require only qada, with differences within the Hanafi school on anal intercourse.

The previous lesson established that intentional intercourse, eating, and drinking require kaffarah. But what about acts that lead to orgasm without penetration? This lesson examines the distinction between 'full intercourse' (penetration) and other acts like thighing and masturbation, which only require qada. We also look at the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether anal intercourse qualifies for kaffarah, and the general ruling on masturbation.

Guided Path
1
Identify acts with orgasm that do not involve penetration
Thighing (tafkheedh), belly rubbing, touching, masturbation by hand, and obscene acts between women. All these cause orgasm but not penetration.
2
Understand the criterion for kaffarah
Kaffarah only applies when there is full insertion (iltiqa' al-khitanayn) in one of the two passages of a living human. These non-penetrative acts are considered 'below private parts' (dūn al-farj).
The commentary says: for orgasm below private parts, only qada.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on anal intercourse
Abu Hanifa in one narration said no kaffarah for anal intercourse, but the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said yes, and this is the more correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ).
This dispute appears in the commentary as a divergence regarding the back passage.
4
Learn the ruling on masturbation (istimna')
Masturbation by hand leads to orgasm, invalidates the fast, and requires qada. It is generally prohibited, but if done to calm desire, may be excused. There is no kaffarah because there is no penetration.
The passage includes narrations against masturbation (cursed, etc.).
5
Compare with other schools on order and choice in kaffarah
Malik: kaffarah is by choice (takhyeer) among the three options, not sequential; Shafi'i also allows choice. Hanafi: sequential order (tartib) is wajib.
This is based on the hadith's sequential nature.

(Ayni, Fath) 'Or is had intercourse with': male or female in one of the two passages in a completely desired place, that it be a living human. (Tahawi, Ayni) 'Intentionally': as a state of being intentional, and this condition refers to all three. (Ayni) 'He makes up': the fast of that day without disagreement. 'He makes up and pays kaffarah': in the nominative because it is the predicate of 'whoever has intercourse'. Al-Shafi'i said: no kaffarah in it, i.e., in eating and drinking, because kaffarah is established for intercourse by text contrary to analogy, so it cannot be analogized to anything else. And our proof is what we narrated: 'Whoever breaks fast in Ramadan' hadith, and his saying 'like the kaffarah of zihar' means in order, due to the hadith of Abu Hurayra: A man came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said: I am ruined, O Messenger of Allah. He said: 'What ruined you?' He said: I had intercourse with my wife in Ramadan. He said: 'Do you find to free a slave?' He said: No. He said: 'Can you fast two consecutive months?' He said: No. He said: 'Do you find to feed sixty poor?' He said: No. Then he sat, and a basket of dates was brought to the Prophet, so he said: 'Give this as charity.' He said: To one poorer than us? For between its two lava plains there is no family more needy than my family. The Prophet laughed until his molars appeared. Contrary to Malik who says choice by analogy to kaffarah of oath, and also says no continuity. And contrary to al-Shafi'i who says choice. (Fath) 'Like the kaffarah of zihar': meaning if he finds a slave, he must free a slave; if not, then fasting two consecutive months; if unable, then feeding sixty poor. (Miskin) 'Below the private parts [i.e., front and back, like thighing and masturbation with the hand, but qada is only required. (Tahawi, Ayni)] Meaning qada without kaffarah is obligatory for orgasm below the private parts absolutely, whether by thigh or by back. And this is a narration from Abu Hanifa; and from him that if he has intercourse in the back, kaffarah is obligatory on both, and that is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is the more correct. Similarly, kaffarah for masturbation with the hand, because there is no intercourse in form, which is insertion of the private part into the private part, but qada is obligatory due to its existence in meaning, in that it fulfills desire. And there is disagreement regarding masturbation with the hand: some say it is forbidden because it is narrated that the one who marries his hand is cursed. And from Ata: I heard a people being gathered with their hands pregnant, and I think they are these. And Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: Allah tortured a nation who played with their private parts. And it is said: if he intends to calm desire, it is hoped that there is no sin. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning acts that invalidate the fast and require both qada and kaffarah. The matn states that whoever has intercourse (or is had intercourse with) or eats or drinks intentionally must make up the fast and pay kaffarah like that of zihar. The commentary elaborates that for intercourse to trigger kaffarah, it must be with a living human being in one of the two passages (penetration), and the place must be completely desired. Acts like masturbation or thighing that lead to orgasm do not require kaffarah according to the preferred view, but qada is still required. The hadith of the bedouin (Abu Hurayra) is cited to establish the kaffarah and its order (freeing slave, fasting two months, feeding sixty). There is a cross-madhhab dispute: Shafi'i does not require kaffarah for eating/drinking, and Malik and Shafi'i differ on the order and whether it is choice or sequence. Also, regarding anal intercourse, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa in one narration says no kaffarah, but the Sahibayn say kaffarah, and this is considered the more correct view. The passage also discusses masturbation, prohibiting it but noting that if done to calm desire, it may be excused.

Key Terms
استمناء (istimna'): Masturbation; the passage discusses that it requires qada if it leads to orgasm, but no kaffarah, and is generally prohibited unless for calming desire.
التقاء الختانين (iltiqa' al-khitanayn): The meeting of the two circumcised parts, i.e., the glans disappearing in the partner's private part, which triggers full rulings of intercourse including kaffarah.
دون الفرج (dūn al-farj): Below the private parts; refers to acts that do not involve penetration but still lead to orgasm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who masturbates to orgasm during a Ramadan fast?
A) The fast is valid.
B) The fast is broken and requires both qada and kaffarah.
C) The fast is broken and requires only qada.
D) The fast is broken and requires no penalty.

Key Takeaway
Non-penetrative acts that cause orgasm invalidate the fast but only require qada; kaffarah is reserved for full intercourse, including anal intercourse according to the mufta bihi view.

Kaffarah: Only for Ramadan, Not for Other Fasts
States that kaffarah is not required for non-Ramadan fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intentionally breaking a non-Ramadan fast requires only qada (make-up) and not kaffarah, and explain the rationale based on the special sanctity of Ramadan.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is specific to Ramadan; breaking any other fast only requires qada.

This passage clarifies an important distinction: the severe expiation (kaffarah) is only for intentionally breaking the Ramadan fast due to its unique sanctity. For all other fasts—whether voluntary, make-up, or expiatory—only make-up (qada) is required. The reason given is that Ramadan cannot be left empty of fasting; any other time can be compensated by another day.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that there is no kaffarah for non-Ramadan fasts, even if one is making up a missed Ramadan day. Qada is required, not kaffarah.
2
Understand the rationale
Kaffarah was prescribed only for violating the sanctity of Ramadan. Since other fasts do not have that level of sanctity, kaffarah is not applicable.
Ramadan is unique because it is a month that must be filled with fasting; any intentional break is a violation of that obligation.
3
Apply the ruling
If someone breaks a voluntary fast or a make-up fast, only qada (one day) is required, not the heavy expiation of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا كفارة أيضاً صوم غير رمضان ولو في قضاء رمضان؛ لأن الكفارة وردت في هتك رمضان؛ إذ لا يجوز إخلاؤه من الصوم، بخلاف غيره من الأزمنة رمز الحقائق: ١٤١/١] وإن احتقن إلخ وإن احتقن أو استعط هما للبناء للفاعل، وبناؤها للمفعول غير جائز ، وقوله : أو أقطر" الصواب قطر؛ لأن "أقطر" لم يأت متعديا، يقال: أقطر الشيء: حان له أن يقطر، بخلاف "قطر" فإنه جاء متعديا ولازما، وبالتضعيف متعديا لا غير وأما الإقطار بمعنى التقطير فلم يأت، وأطلق الإقطار فعم الدهن والماء، ولا خلاف في الأول، واختلف في الثاني فجزم في "النهاية" بأن الماء لا يقطر مطلقا، ولو استنشق ووصل الماء إلى دماغه يفطر عيني، فتح أو استعط أي صب في أنفه الدواء، والسعوط بفتح السين ما يجعل في الأنف من الأدوية ومنه يقال: سعطته وأسعطته. (ع) أذنه: دهناً؛ لأن الماء إذا أقطر فيه لا يفطر. (ع) أو :آمة وهي الشجة التي تبلغ أم الرأس. (ع)
No kaffarah is also for fasting other than Ramadan, even if it is for making up Ramadan, because kaffarah has been reported for violating the sanctity of Ramadan, since it is not permissible to leave it empty of fasting, unlike other times. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/141) And if he gives himself an enema (ihtaqan) or takes snuff (ista'ta) – both are built for the active voice, and building them for the passive is not permissible. And his saying: 'or puts drops (aqtara)' – the correct is 'qattara' because 'aqtara' is not used transitively; it is said 'aqtara al-shay' means its time for dripping arrived, unlike 'qattara' which is used transitively and intransitively. And with tashdid it is transitive only. As for 'iqtar' meaning to put drops, it is not transmitted. And he used 'iqtar' generally covering oil and water. There is no disagreement in the first (oil), and there is disagreement in the second (water). Al-Nihaya decided that water does not drip at all. And if he sniffs and water reaches his brain, he breaks his fast. (Ayni, Fath) 'Or takes snuff': meaning he pours medicine into his nose. And su'ut is what is placed in the nose of medicines. From it is said: sa'atuhu and as'atuhu. (Ayni) 'In his ear': oil; because water if put in the ear does not break the fast. (Ayni) 'Or amma': a head wound that reaches the membrane of the brain. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that kaffarah does not apply to breaking fasts other than Ramadan, even if one is making up Ramadan. The reason given is that kaffarah was legislated specifically for violating the sanctity of Ramadan, which is a month that must be filled with fasting. The commentary then discusses various actions that invalidate the fast but require only qada: giving an enema, taking snuff (dropping medicine into nose), putting drops in the ear (oil breaks fast, water does not), and treating a deep head wound (ja'ifah or amma). The passage also corrects a linguistic point: the verb 'aqtara' is incorrect for 'to drip'; the correct verb is 'qattara'.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast; includes freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.
قضاء (qada): Make-up; fasting one day to compensate for a broken fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on making up a Ramadan fast later and breaking it intentionally?
A) Kaffarah is required because it is a make-up of Ramadan.
B) Only qada is required because it is a non-Ramadan fast.
C) Both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) No expiation but must feed a poor person.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is exclusive to Ramadan; all other fasts only require qada when intentionally broken.

Actions That Invalidate a Fast: Enema, Snuff, Ear Drops, Head Wound
Lists actions requiring qada only: enema, snuff, ear drops, head wound treatment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the actions listed in the passage that invalidate a fast and require only qada, and apply the distinction between water and oil when drops enter the ear.

Core Idea
Enema, snuff, oil in ear, and medicine reaching the brain from a head wound break the fast; water in ear does not.

The passage enumerates several ways a fast can be invalidated without direct ingestion. The Hanafi school holds that if any substance enters the body through the nose, ear, or a wound, the fast is broken but only qada is required. The author also clarifies a linguistic point: the correct verb for 'putting drops' is qattara, not aqtara. Additionally, there is a disagreement about whether water in the ear breaks the fast—the relied-upon position is that it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the enema (ihtiqan)
The passage states that administering an enema (ihtaqan) invalidates the fast because fluid enters the body through the lower passage.
The verb is active voice: ihtaqana (he gave himself an enema).
2
Identify snuff (isti'tat)
Taking snuff (isti'tat) means pouring medicine into the nose. If it reaches the brain, it breaks the fast; if only to the nose, it does not.
Su'ut is the medicine placed in the nose.
3
Distinguish oil vs. water for ear drops
The passage says 'atara' (putting drops) in the ear: if it is oil, it breaks the fast; if water, it does not. Imam al-Nihaya is cited declaring that water does not drip at all.
The linguistic correction: the correct verb is qattara (with tashdid), not aqtara.
4
Identify the head wound (amma)
A deep head wound (amma) that reaches the brain membrane: if medicine is applied and reaches the brain, it breaks the fast.
