Excuses for Not Fasting: Illness and Travel (Hanafi Perspective)
Lists excuses permitting fast-breaking, focusing on illness and travel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a sick person and a traveler to break the fast, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast is allowed only when there is strong probability of harm; fasting remains better unless harm is certain.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the excuses that permit breaking the fast, focusing on illness and travel. The Hanafi school provides a moderate concession: a sick person may break if there is reasonable fear of increased illness, while a traveler may break if the journey meets the shari'i distance. However, the default recommendation is to fast if no harm is expected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
The passage begins by stating that breaking fast without excuse is sinful, with excuse it is not. The eight excuses are listed; the text expands on illness and travel.
The author mentions five, leaving three for later.
2
Locate the conditions for illness
Find the conditions: strong probability of increased illness, based on experience, signs, or a trustworthy Muslim physician. Compare with Shafi'i requirement of fear of death or limb loss.
Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn have a minor difference on inability to stand in prayer.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof for illness
The Hanafi argument is that increased illness may lead to death, so it must be avoided. The Shafi'i qiyas on tayammum is rejected because illness is not like water harm.
The Quranic permission (2:185) is absolute for 'sick'.
4
Determine the ruling for travel
Travel must be shari'i length and commenced before dawn to break that day. Fasting is recommended unless harmful. The dispute with Shafi'i on whether breaking is better is resolved by citing Companion practice.
The hadith 'Not from piety to fast while traveling' is interpreted as addressing harm.
5
Synthesize the practical outcome
In both cases, the default is to fast; breaking is a concession when needed. The mufta bihi position is that reasonable fear suffices for illness, and fasting is better for traveler if not harmful.
If harm is certain, breaking becomes better (afdal) and may be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض: [ أي في بيان أحكامها، وهي جمع عارضة من عرض الأمر إذا حدث أي في العوارض المبيحة لعدم الصوم. (ط، ع)] لما كان إفساد الصوم بغير عذر يوجب إثماً وبعذر لا يوجبه، احتيج إلى بيان الأعذار المبيحة لعدم الصوم، وهي ثمانية ذكر المصنف منها خمسة يعني المرض والسفر والحمل والإرضاع والشيخ الفاني، وبقي منها ثلاثة الإكراه والعطش والجوع الشديد إذا خيف منها الهلاك أو نقصان العقل، ونظمها العلامة المقدسي في بيت واحد فقال: سقم وإكراه وحمل وسفر رضع وجوع وعطش وكبر (فتح، طائي) زيادة المرض: بالصوم أو بطوء البر أو فساد العضو بغلبة الظن عن تجربة أو أمارة أو إخبار طبيب مسلم غير ظاهر الفسق. (ط) الفطر : يعني الفطر عندنا؛ لخوف زيادة المرض، وقال الشافعي لا يفطر إلا إذا خاف خوف الهلاك أو فوات العضو كما في التيمم ونحن نقول: إن زيادة المرض أو امتداده قد يفضي إلى الهلاك فيجب الاحتراز عنه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما إذا عجز عن القيام في الصلاة له الفطر. (عيني، فتح) وللمسافر أي الفطر له بالسفر الشرعي إذا أصبح مسافرا، أما إذا أصبح مقيما صائما ثم سافر فلا يحل له الإفطار في ذلك اليوم. (مسكين) وصومه أحب إن لم يضره، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ ﴾ (البقرة: ١٨٥) فعم الكل، وأجيز له التأخير رخصة، فإذا أخذ بالعزيمة يكون أفضل، وقال الشافعي: الفطر أفضل؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس من البر الصوم في السفر"، ولنا ما رواه أنس: "كنا نسافر مع النبي الله فمنا الصائم ومنا المفطر فلم يعب الصائم على المفطر، ولا المفطر على الصائم، متفق عليه وما رواه خرج في مسافر ضره الصوم على ما روي في القصة أو غشي عليه، ونحن نقول به. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٢] لم يضره : لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَنْ تَصُومُوا خَيْرٌ لَكُمْ ﴾ (البقرة : ١٨٤) فإن ضره فإفطاره أفضل. (ط،ع)
Section on excuses: i.e., explaining their rulings, and they are the plural of 'arid, from 'he presented' when something occurs, i.e., the excuses that permit not fasting. Since breaking the fast without excuse incurs sin, and with excuse does not incur it, it is necessary to explain the excuses that permit not fasting. They are eight: the author mentioned five: illness, travel, pregnancy, breastfeeding, and the decrepit old person. Three remain: coercion, thirst, and extreme hunger if death or loss of intellect is feared. Al-Maqdisi versified them in one couplet: 'Sickness, coercion, pregnancy, travel, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age.' Increase of illness: by fasting, or slowness of recovery, or corruption of a limb, based on strong probability from experience, signs, or the report of a Muslim physician not obviously sinful. Breaking fast: means breaking fast in our school out of fear of increased illness. Al-Shafi'i said: he only breaks if he fears death or loss of a limb, as in tayammum. We say: increase or prolongation of illness may lead to death, so it must be guarded against. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [Sahibayn], if he is unable to stand in prayer, he may break. For the traveler: break fast is permissible for him if he undertakes a legal journey while at dawn; if he was resident and fasting then traveled, it is not permissible for him to break fast that day. Fasting is more beloved for him if it does not harm him, because of His saying: 'So whoever witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185) which includes everyone. Delay is permitted as a concession; if he takes the resolve it is better. Al-Shafi'i said: breaking fast is better because of the Prophet's saying: 'It is not piety to fast while traveling.' Our evidence: Anas reported: 'We traveled with the Prophet; among us were fasting and not fasting, and the fasting did not criticize the not-fasting, nor the not-fasting criticize the fasting' (agreed upon). And what was reported about a traveler who was harmed by fasting, as in the story or he became unconscious, we also accept that. [If it] does not harm him: because of His saying: 'And that you fast is better for you' (2:184). If it harms him, then breaking is better.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This is a section (fasl) on the excuses (al-awarid) that permit a Muslim not to fast. The passage begins by stating the principle: breaking the fast without an excuse is sinful, but with an excuse it is not. Eight excuses are identified: illness, travel, pregnancy, breastfeeding, decrepit old age, coercion, extreme thirst, and extreme hunger. The author elaborates on two: illness and travel. For illness, the key condition is a reasonable fear that fasting will increase the illness, slow recovery, or damage a limb, based on experience, signs, or a trustworthy Muslim doctor's report. The Hanafi position permits breaking fast to prevent increased illness, even if not fatal, in contrast to Shafi'i who requires fear of death or limb loss. Abu Hanifa's view is that even moderate increase of illness justifies breaking, while the Sahibayn add inability to stand in prayer as another criterion. For travel, the travel must be a shari'i journey (long enough to permit qasr), and one must have intended travel before dawn to break fast that day. The text then discusses whether fasting or breaking is better for the traveler: the default recommendation is to fast if not harmful, based on the general command to fast the month. However, if harm is likely, breaking is better. A dispute with Shafi'i arises: Shafi'i considers breaking better based on a hadith, but the Hanafi response cites the practice of Companions who differed without criticism and the principle that fasting is better unless harmful.

Key Terms
عارضة (arida): An excuse that arises that permits not fasting, such as illness or travel.
زيادة المرض (ziyadat al-marad): Increase of illness caused by fasting, based on strong probability.
السفر الشرعي (al-safar al-shar'i): A journey long enough to permit qasr (shortening) of prayer, typically three days' travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for a sick person to break the fast due to illness?
A) Actual death must be feared
B) Strong probability that fasting will increase illness or delay recovery
C) Inability to stand in prayer
D) Doctor's recommendation even if no fear of harm

Key Takeaway
Fasting is obligatory, but the Shari'ah provides concessions for illness and travel when harm is likely; the default is to fast unless a valid excuse exists.

Qada and Fidya for Deceased Sick/Traveler
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on fidya for missed fasts after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada and fidya for a sick person or traveler who dies, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
If a sick or traveler dies while excused, no qada or fidya; if they recover then die, qada and a will for fidya are required.

What happens to the missed fasts of a sick person or traveler who never recovers, or who recovers but dies before making them up? The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction based on whether the person ever had an opportunity to fast after the excuse ended. In this passage, we explore the rulings on qada (making up fasts) and fidya (feeding the poor) for such cases, along with a key dispute with the Shafi'i school on whether fidya is due from the estate without a will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between dying while still excused (illness/travel) and dying after the excuse ended but before making up the fasts.
2
State the Hanafi hukm for each
If die while excused: no qada, no fidya. If recover/settle and then die: qada is required to the extent of the days of health/residence, and the deceased must bequeath (wasiyya) to feed for each missed day.
3
Locate the dalil for the ruling on continuity
The narration from Ibn Umar reported by al-Daraqutni: 'If he wishes he may separate, and if he wishes he may follow' – showing continuity is not obligatory.
This counters the claim that consecutive fasting is required.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Shafi'i analogizes fidya to debts, making it obligatory from the entire estate without a will. The Hanafi response: fasting is an act of worship requiring the deceased's choice, hence the need for a will.
The practical difference: without a will, Hanafi says no fidya; Shafi'i says it must be paid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is relied upon: no qada/fidya if die while excused; if excuse ends, qada and will for fidya are required.

(فتح مسكين يوم من الأيام التي أفطرا فيها. (ط) كالفطرة: أي نصف صاع من بر أو صاعاً من غيره. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٣/١] بوصية: منهما، فلو لم يوصيا بذلك لم يلزم الولي أي الوصي يطعم عنهما، ولو تبرع به الولي جاز. (ط،ع) بلا شرط ولاء [ أي متابعة فله الخيار إن شاء فرق، وإن شاء تابع (ط)] الولاء بكسر الواو الموالاة بمعنى المتابعة وهو الترتيب؛ لأن النص لم يشترط ذلك، وعند البعض لابد من المتابعة؛ لقوله علي: "من كان عليه قضاء رمضان فليسرده ولا يقطعه، قلنا: هذا غير ثابت وروى ابن عمر لما أنه عليها قال: "قضاء رمضان إن شاء فرق، وإن شاء تابع"، "، رواه الدار قطني فإن قلت: قراءة أبي فعدة من أيام أخر متتابعة" فيجب العمل بها كما في قراءة ابن مسعود لله في كفارة اليمين: "ثلاثة أيام متتابعات"، قلت هذه الرواية مشهورة، وتلك غير مشهورة، فلا يجوز التخصيص بها، ولكن المستحب التتابع مسارعة إلى إسقاط الواجب. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٣/١] جاء رمضان آخر ولم يقض الرمضان الذي قبله. (ع)] رمضان ههنا نكرة فاقتضى تنوينه؛ لأنه منصرف أي رمضان آخر يعني إن جاء رمضان الثاني على المكلف الذي لم يصم رمضان الأول أدى الثاني أي ينبغي له ذلك،
And no qada if they both die while in that state, and their guardian feeds for each day – i.e., the traveler. Meaning: qada is not obligatory for the sick and traveler. For them [the sick and traveler], [the feeding is] like fitra by will. And they make up what they were able [to make up] without condition of continuity. Then if Ramadan comes, they should perform the current [fast]. Commentary: 'And no qada if they both die': meaning if they die in their state of illness and travel and did not reach days of health – for the imam, qada is not obligatory upon them; because they were excused in performance, so it is more appropriate that they are excused in qada. Nor is payment of fidya obligatory. This is if the sick person does not have confirmed despair of recovery. If confirmed, he should feed for each day of illness. If the sick person recovers or the traveler settles and they do not make up [fasts] until death, qada is required to the extent of the days of health and residence – i.e., they must bequeath it. 'Upon them': i.e., upon illness and travel, because they did not reach 'a number of other days.' 'And their guardian feeds etc.': i.e., if the sick person recovers and the traveler settles and they do not fast, then die, it is required for their guardian – i.e., whoever has authority over their wealth – to feed [for each day] by their will. If they did not will, feeding is not required upon the heir. Al-Shafi'i said: it is required without a will from the entire estate, by analogy with debts owed to people. Our evidence: it is an act of worship, so it requires their choice, which is by will, and it is from one-third of the estate. If they die without a will, the condition is lost and falls due to impossibility. And his statement: 'they make up what they were able' means qada is required for them to the extent of health and residence; the benefit of the obligation of qada is the obligation of will for feeding. [The feeding is] like fitra: half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of other grain. By will from them; if they did not will that, the guardian (executor) is not required to feed for them; if the guardian donates it, it is permissible. 'Without condition of continuity': [meaning following] – he has the option: if he wishes he may separate, and if he wishes he may follow. The word 'wala'' with kasr of waw means muwalat: following, i.e., sequential; because the text did not stipulate that. Some hold that continuity is required due to the saying of Ali: 'Whoever has qada of Ramadan should do it consecutively and not interrupt it.' We say: this is not established. Ibn Umar narrated that he said about qada of Ramadan: 'If he wishes he may separate, and if he wishes he may follow.' Reported by al-Daraqutni. If you say: the recitation of Ubayy [is] 'a number of other days consecutively' so it must be acted upon, like the recitation of Ibn Mas'ud in the expiation of an oath: 'three days consecutive' – I say: this [Ubayy's] narration is well-known, but that [Ibn Mas'ud's] is not well-known, so it is not permissible to specify with it. However, it is recommended to fast consecutively to hasten the discharge of obligation. 'Then if another Ramadan comes and he did not make up the previous Ramadan' – 'Ramadan' here is indefinite, so its tanwin is required because it is declinable, meaning another Ramadan: i.e., if the second Ramadan comes upon the obligated person who did not fast the first Ramadan, he should perform the second, i.e., it is necessary for him to do so.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of a sick person or traveler who dies before making up missed fasts. According to the Hanafi school, if a person remains in their excuse (illness or travel) until death, neither qada (making up fasts) nor fidya (feeding the poor) is required. This is because they were excused from performing the fasts during their lifetime, so they are also excused from making them up after death, by priority. However, if the sick person recovers or the traveler settles and they have the opportunity to fast but die before doing so, then qada becomes obligatory upon them to the extent of the days of health/residence, and they must bequeath (wasiyya) for fidya from one-third of their estate. The fidya is like fitra: half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of other food per missed day. A dispute with Shafi'i arises: Shafi'i holds that fidya is obligatory from the entire estate without a will, by analogy with financial debts. The Hanafi response is that fasting is a physical act of worship, so it requires the person's own choice (the will) to convert it into a financial obligation; without a will, the obligation falls. The passage also discusses the issue of continuity (wala) in making up missed fasts: it is not obligatory to fast consecutively; one may separate them. Evidence from Ibn Umar's narration is cited. The recommended practice is to fast consecutively to fulfill the obligation quickly. Finally, if a second Ramadan comes without having made up the previous one, the current Ramadan must be fasted first, and the missed fasts are postponed further. The indefinite 'Ramadan' indicates any following Ramadan.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up missed fasts after the excuse ends.
فدية (fidya): Feeding a poor person for each missed fast, often required when fasting is not possible (e.g., elderly, chronic illness). Here after death, from will.
وصية (wasiyya): Will or bequest made by the deceased to feed for missed fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a sick person dies while still ill, with no hope of recovery, what is required?
A) Nothing - no qada and no fidya
B) Qada must be made by heirs
C) Fidya must be paid from the estate even without a will
D) Only qada, no fidya

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between dying while excused (no obligation) and dying after the excuse ends (qada wajib, fidya only via will), rejecting the Shafi'i analogy with debts.

Making Up Missed Fasts: Consecutiveness Not Required
Ruling that making up Ramadan fasts does not require consecutive days.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consecutiveness for qada fasts, identify the dalil, and explain the response to the opposing view.

Core Idea
The Quran and authentic hadith do not require consecutiveness for qada of Ramadan; it is recommended but not obligatory.

This lesson examines a key difference between Hanafis and others on how to make up missed Ramadan fasts. The passage presents the Hanafi position that consecutiveness is not a condition, supported by textual evidence and a critical evaluation of opposing proofs. Understanding this clarifies a common practical issue for Muslims who owe fasts.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the ruling
Read the passage to find the Hanafi position on consecutive fasts for qada. Note the phrase 'without condition of consecutiveness'.
2
2. Find the primary proof
The Quranic verse (2:184) does not mention consecutiveness, and the hadith from Ibn 'Umar explicitly allows separation.
3
3. Examine the opposing argument
Some scholars cite a hadith commanding consecutiveness and the qira'ah of Ubayy. Understand their evidence.
4
4. Evaluate the strength of opposing evidence
Hanafis argue the hadith is not authentic ('ghayr thabit') and the qira'ah is not widely transmitted ('mashhurah') to restrict the general text.
This shows the usul principle: only a mashhur or mutawatir can specify (takhsis) the general command.
5
5. Distinguish obligation from recommendation
Though not required, consecutiveness is mustahabb to hasten discharging the obligation. Note the practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بلا شرط ولاء [أي متابعة فله الخيار إن شاء فرق، وإن شاء تابع]؛ لأن النص لم يشترط ذلك، وعند البعض لابد من المتابعة لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "من كان عليه قضاء رمضان فليُسرده ولا يقطعه"، قلنا: هذا غير ثابت، وروي عن ابن عمر أنه قال: "قضاء رمضان إن شاء فرق وإن شاء تابع" رواه الدارقطني، فإن قلت: قراءة أبيّ "فعدة من أيام أخر متتابعة" فيجب العمل بها كما في قراءة ابن مسعود في كفارة اليمين: "ثلاثة أيام متتابعات"، قلت: هذه الرواية مشهورة، وتلك غير مشهورة، فلا يجوز التخصيص بها، ولكن المستحب التتابع مسارعة إلى إسقاط الواجب.
Without the condition of consecutiveness [i.e., following one after another, so he has the choice: if he wishes, he may separate them, and if he wishes, he may follow consecutively]; because the text did not stipulate that. And according to some, consecutiveness is necessary due to his statement: 'Whoever owes qada of Ramadan, let him fast it consecutively and not break it.' We say: this is not established. And it is narrated from Ibn 'Umar that he said: 'Qada of Ramadan: if he wishes, he may separate, and if he wishes, he may consecutively' – narrated by al-Daraqutni. If you say: the reading of Ubayy 'a number of other days consecutive' requires acting upon it, just as in the reading of Ibn Mas'ud regarding kaffarat al-yamin: 'three consecutive days'; we say: this narration (of Ubayy) is not well-known, and that one (of Ibn Mas'ud) is well-known. So it is not permissible to make takhsis with it. However, it is recommended to be consecutive to hasten the discharge of the obligation.

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of making up missed Ramadan fasts. The Hanafi position is that there is no requirement of consecutiveness (muwalat). The author cites that the Quranic text (2:184) does not stipulate consecutiveness. Some scholars (likely Shafi'is) argue for consecutiveness using a hadith that is not authentic according to Hanafis. The author also uses the statement of Ibn 'Umar as a proof for permissibility of separating. The discussion includes a counter-argument from the variant reading of Ubayy ibn Ka'b which uses 'mutatabi'at' (consecutive). The response is that this reading is not widely transmitted (ghayr mashhurah) and therefore does not have the weight to restrict the general text. However, while not obligatory, it is recommended to fast consecutively as it is quicker in fulfilling the obligation. This shows the Hanafi approach: permissibility with recommendation for the better practice.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-walā’): Consecutiveness; fasting missed days one after another without break.
مشهورة (mashhūrah): Well-known, widely transmitted (here referring to a qira'ah or hadith).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hanafis permit separating make-up fasts; consecutiveness is recommended but not obligatory, based on the absence of textual stipulation and weak opposing proofs.

Death of a Sick Person or Traveler: No Qada Required
Ruling on qada when a sick person or traveler dies without recovering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada and fidyah for missed fasts when a sick person or traveler dies, and distinguish the three scenarios covered in the passage.

Core Idea
Death while excused absolves qada; chronic illness requires fidyah; neglect after recovery obligates qada on the estate.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a common scenario in fasting obligations: what happens if the person who missed fasts due to illness or travel dies before making them up? The ruling distinguishes between dying while the excuse persists (no qada, no fidyah), dying with a chronic illness (fidyah due), and dying after recovery without making up (qada becomes a debt on the estate). Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of missed fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two excused categories
Note that the passage discusses two types of persons who are excused from fasting: the sick and the traveler.
These are explicitly mentioned in Quran 2:184.
2
State the default ruling if death occurs while the excuse persists
If a sick person or traveler dies while still in that state, there is no obligation of qada (making up) or fidyah.
The reasoning: since they were excused from fasting, they are even more excused from making up.
3
Distinguish the case of chronic illness with no hope of recovery
If the sick person has a chronic condition with no hope of recovery (ya'is min al-bur'), then for each missed day of illness they must pay fidyah (feed a poor person).
This is because the condition for qada (health) will never arise.
4
State the ruling if the person recovers but dies without making up
If the sick person recovers or the traveler returns home, but then dies before making up the missed fasts, qada becomes obligatory on the estate. The deceased should have bequeathed it.
Here the obligation is for the missed days during the period of health/residence only.
5
Explain the Hanafi reasoning
The Quranic verse postpones fasting to 'other days'; if death occurs before those days arrive, the condition for qada never materializes. Similarly, the excuse for performance extends to qada by priority.
This is a core Hanafi principle: absence of the condition removes the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قضاء إن ماتا عليهما، أي المريض والمسافر، أي لا يجب القضاء عنهما، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَىٰ سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾ أي لم يدركوا عدة من أيام أخر. وللإمام لا يلزمهما القضاء؛ لأنهما عذرا في الأداء فلأن يعذرا في القضاء أولى، ولا يلزمهما دفع الفدية، وهذا إذا لم يتحقق للمريض اليأس من البرء، فإن تحقق فدى لكل يوم من المرض، وإن صح المريض أو أقام المسافر ولم يقض حتى مات لزمه القضاء بقدر الصحة والإقامة أي لزمه الإيصاء به.
And there is no [obligation of] making up [fasts] if both die in their state, i.e., the sick person and the traveler, meaning making up is not obligatory for them, due to His saying: "But whoever among you is ill or on a journey [then an equal number of other days]" i.e., they did not reach other days. And for the Imam: making up is not binding on them because they were excused in performance, so they are even more excused in making up. Nor does payment of fidyah become binding on them. This is if the sick person has no certainty of recovery. If he is certain [of chronic illness], then he pays fidyah for each day of illness. If the sick person recovers or the traveler returns home and does not make up [the missed fasts] until death, making up becomes binding on him to the extent of health and residency, i.e., he must bequeath it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling when a sick person or traveler dies while still in their state of illness or travel. The legal consequence is that no qada (make-up fasts) is required from them or their estate, because the Quranic verse (2:184) explicitly postpones the obligation to 'other days,' and if death occurs before those days arrive, the condition for qada never materializes. The author notes that since they were excused from fasting in the first place, they are even more excused from making up. No fidyah is required either, as long as recovery was not hopeless. However, if the sick person had no hope of recovery (i.e., chronic illness), then for each day missed, a fidyah must be given. Also, if the person recovers health or ends travel but neglects to make up until death, then qada becomes binding on the estate, and the deceased should have made a will regarding feeding. This ruling emphasizes the distinction between excused absence of obligation and neglect after the excuse ends.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍā’): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship, here fasting.
فدية (fidyah): Compensatory payment for missed fasts when one is permanently unable to fast, such as feeding a poor person per day.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When they die while still sick.
B) When they recover but do not make up fasts.
C) When they have a chronic illness with no hope of recovery.
D) They never need to pay fidyah.

Key Takeaway
Death in a state of excuse removes both qada and fidyah; chronic illness requires fidyah; neglect after recovery makes qada a debt on the estate.

Fasting for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Women: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling that pregnant and breastfeeding women may break fast, owe only qada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women regarding fasting, identify the supporting proofs (hadith and qiyas), and contrast the Hanafi position with the Shafi'i position on fidyah.

Core Idea
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break fast but owe only qada, no fidyah.

This lesson examines the fiqh of fasting for pregnant and breastfeeding women. The Hanafi school permits them to break the fast if they fear harm for themselves or the child, requiring only makeup days (qada). This ruling is based on a hadith and qiyas with the traveler, and it differs from the Shafi'i view that adds a fidyah payment. Understanding this ruling clarifies important principles about exceptions, obligation, and the scope of analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women who break their fast? Distinguish between the permissibility of breaking and the obligation of qada.
The act of breaking is mubah (permissible) when there is genuine fear; the qada afterward is wajib.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited and note its classification (ahad, zanni). Identify the textual proof that the women share the traveler's concession.
Hadith: 'Verily Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding the fast.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit mention of pregnant and breastfeeding alongside the traveler indicates they are granted the same concession without additional penalty.
The text treats them as a single category with the traveler for lifting the obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the Shafi'i position: they require fidyah when fear is for the child, by analogy with the old man (shaykh fani) who pays fidyah for missed fasts.
Shafi'i qiyas: the benefit of breaking goes to the child, who is not obligated to fast, similar to the old man.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand the Hanafi response: the fidyah for the old man is an exception (kharaj an al-qiyas), so it cannot be extended. Moreover, the child is not obligated to fast, so the mother's qada suffices.
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللحامل والمرضع إن خافتا على الولد والنفس، وللحامل [أي الفطر جائز للحامل أيضاً للحرج كالمسافر] لحديث: "إن الله وضع عن المسافر الصوم، وشطر الصلاة، وعن الحامل والمرضع الصوم"؛ ولأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة، لاسيما عند إعسار الزوج، أي يجب عليهما القضاء بلا كفارة وفدية، وقال الشافعي: تجب الفدية فيما إذا خافت على الولد؛ لأنه إفطار انتفع به من لم يلزمه الصوم، وهو الولد، فتجب الفدية كإفطار الشيخ الفاني، ولنا أن الفدية وجبت على الشيخ الفاني بخلاف القياس فلا يلحق به خلافه؛ لأن الفدية على الشيخ لعجزه عن الصوم الواجب والطفل لا يجب عليه الصوم، وإنما يجب على أمه وهي تصوم القضاء، فلا يجب عليها غيره، وقيل: المراد من المرضع الظئر؛ لأنها لا تتمكن من الامتناع عن الإرضاع؛ لوجوبه عليها بعقد الإجارة، وأما الأم فليس عليها الإرضاع، فإن امتنعت على الأب استجار مرضعة أخرى.
And for the pregnant and breastfeeding [woman] if they fear for the child and themselves. And for the pregnant: [i.e., breaking the fast is also permissible for the pregnant due to hardship like the traveler] due to the hadith: 'Verily Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding the fast.' And because breastfeeding is obligatory upon the mother religiously, especially when the husband is poor. That is, making up is obligatory upon them without atonement or fidyah. Al-Shafi'i said: Fidyah is obligatory when she fears for the child, because it is a break from which someone who is not obligated to fast benefits, namely the child, so fidyah is obligatory like the break of the senile old man. Our proof is that fidyah was imposed on the senile old man contrary to analogy, so it should not be extended to other cases. Because fidyah on the old man is due to his inability to perform the obligatory fast, whereas the child is not obligated to fast; rather, it is obligatory upon his mother, and she will make up the fast, so nothing else is required of her. And it is said: the intended meaning of the breastfeeding woman is the wet nurse, because she cannot refrain from breastfeeding due to its obligation upon her by contract of hire. As for the mother, breastfeeding is not obligatory upon her; if she refuses, the father may hire another wet nurse.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings for pregnant and breastfeeding women. They are permitted to break the fast if they fear harm for themselves or the child, by analogy with the traveler and based on a specific hadith. The Hanafi position is that they owe only qada (make-up fasts) without any kaffarah or fidyah. The Shafi'i opinion is also presented: they require fidyah in addition to qada if the fear is for the child, drawing an analogy with the elderly person who cannot fast at all. The Hanafi response points out that the elderly person's fidyah is an exception to normal qiyas, so it cannot be extended. Moreover, the child is not obligated to fast, so the mother's qada suffices. The passage also mentions a nuance: the ruling applies more clearly to a wet nurse (dha'r) who is contractually obligated to breastfeed, whereas a mother's breastfeeding is religiously recommended but not legally obligatory in the same way. Thus, the Hanafis confirm only qada for both.

Key Terms
الظئر (al-dha’r): A wet nurse who breastfeeds a child for hire.
فدية (fidyah): Compensation payment for missed fasts when permanent inability exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required of a pregnant woman who breaks her fast due to fear for her child?
A) Only qada of missed days.
B) Qada plus fidyah for each day.
C) No qada, only fidyah.
D) Nothing, as the fear is a valid excuse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that pregnant and breastfeeding women who break fast due to fear owe only qada, rejecting fidyah because the child is not obligated to fast.

Fasting for the Elderly and Voluntary Faster
Rulings: elderly must pay fidyah; voluntary faster may break fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an elderly person who cannot fast and explain the proof, and also state the ruling for a voluntary faster.

Core Idea
The elderly unable to fast must pay fidyah; the voluntary faster may break at any time — both based on textual proof.

This lesson covers two distinct rulings related to fasting: the obligation of fidyah for the elderly who cannot fast, and the permissibility of breaking a voluntary fast. The Hanafi school relies on a Quranic verse and the consensus of the Companions, while addressing a disagreement with other schools. Understanding these rulings clarifies when fasting is excused and when compensation is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The first ruling: an elderly person who is permanently unable to fast may break and must pay fidyah. The second: a person fasting voluntarily may break at any time.
2
Understand the condition for the elderly
The criterion is permanent inability to fast, not age. The person must be in constant decline with no hope of recovery. The ruling applies only to obligatory Ramadan fasts originally due; not to expiatory fasts.
The author clarifies that the criterion is inability, not age (e.g., passing 50 years).
3
Locate the proof
The Hanafi proof is Quran 2:184, interpreted by Ibn 'Abbas as referring to the elderly, and the reported consensus of the Companions.
The proof is textual (nass), so analogy (qiyas) to the sick person is rejected.
4
Note the disagreement
Malik and al-Shafi'i (in his old view) say no fidyah, analogizing the elderly to a sick person who dies before recovery.
The Hanafi response is that the text overrides analogy.
5
Apply to the voluntary faster
A person who starts a voluntary (nafl) fast may break it without any obligation to make it up or pay compensation.
This is stated concisely at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشيخ الفاني [أي الهرم وهو الذي فنيت قوته ولم يقدر على الصيام، أي الفطر للشيخ الفاني أيضاً] قيل: هو من جاوز الخمسين والمدار على عدم قدرة الصوم، أن يكون كل يوم في نقص إلى أن يموت والعجوز الكبيرة التي لا ترجى قدرتها على الصوم كالشيخ الفاني، ويلحق به من في معناه وأيس من حياته، وقوله: "وهو يفدي" بفتح الياء التحتية أي وجوباً لو موسراً، وإلا فيستغفر الله، وهذا إذا كان الصوم أصلا بنفسه وخوطب بأدائه حتى لو لزمه الصوم ككفارة يمين ثم عجز لم تجز له الفدية، وقال مالك: لا فدية عليه، وبه قال الشافعي في القديم؛ لأنه عاجز عن الصوم، فأشبه المريض إذا مات قبل البرء وصار كالصغير والمجنون، ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ ﴾ قال ابن عباس: هي للشيخ الكبير والمرأة الكبيرة لا يستطيعان الصوم فيطعمان، ولم يرو عن أحد من الصحابة خلاف ذلك فكان إجماعا، ولا يجوز المصير إلى القياس مع وجود النص. يفدي: أي يطعم لكل يوم مسكينا. فقط : أي يفدي هو فقط دون المسافر والمريض والحامل والمرضع. وللمتطوع أي للمتطوع بالصوم الفطر أيضاً.
And for the senile old man [i.e., decrepit one whose strength has perished and is not able to fast, i.e., breaking the fast is also for the senile old man.] It is said: he is one who has passed fifty, but the criterion is inability to fast, that he is daily in decline until death. The old woman who cannot hope to have ability to fast is like the senile old man. And one in his meaning who has no hope of life is annexed to him. And his statement: 'and he pays fidyah' – with fatha on the under-yā’ – i.e., obligatory if wealthy, otherwise he seeks forgiveness from Allah. This is if the fast is originally an obligation upon him; if he owes a fast like an expiatory fast (kaffarat yamin) and then becomes unable, fidyah is not sufficient for him. Malik said: No fidyah upon him, and al-Shafi'i said the same in his old opinion, because he is unable to fast, so he resembles the sick person who dies before recovery, and becomes like the child and insane. Our proof is His saying: 'And upon those who are able [to fast, but with hardship] – a fidyah: feeding a poor person.' Ibn 'Abbas said: It is for the old man and old woman who cannot fast, so they feed. And no one from the Companions is known to have opposed that, so it is consensus. And it is not permissible to resort to qiyas while the text exists. Fidyah: i.e., he feeds for each day a poor person. 'Only' – i.e., he pays fidyah only, not the traveler, sick, pregnant, or breastfeeding. And for the voluntary faster: i.e., for the one who volunteers to fast, breaking [is also permissible].
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This final passage covers two separate rulings. First, the elderly person (shaykh fani) who is permanently unable to fast: it is allowed to break, but he must pay fidyah (feeding a poor person for each missed day) if he has means; if not, he seeks forgiveness. The author clarifies that the criterion is inability, not age. This applies only to original obligation of Ramadan; if the fast is expiatory, fidyah does not substitute. A disagreement with Malik and al-Shafi'i (in his old view) is presented; they analogize to the sick person, but the Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse (2:184) and the consensus (ijma) of the Companions as reported by Ibn 'Abbas. The second ruling: a person who is fasting a voluntary (nafl) fast may break it at any time without penalty. This is stated concisely.

Key Terms
الشيخ الفاني (al-shaykh al-fānī): An elderly person whose physical strength has declined to the point of being unable to fast, with no hope of recovery.
المتطوع (al-mutaṭawwi‘): One who performs a voluntary act of worship, here a voluntary fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They must fast as much as possible.
B) They may break and must pay fidyah for each day.
C) They may break with no obligation.
D) They must make up fasts later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires fidyah for the elderly who cannot fast and permits breaking a voluntary fast, both supported by textual evidence.

Rulings on Deferred Qada and Pregnant/Nursing Women
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on qada delay and pregnant/nursing women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that delaying qada without excuse does not require fidya, and that pregnant and nursing women who fear harm must only make up fasts (qada), and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i analogy to the decrepit old man.

Core Idea
For pregnant and nursing women, the Hanafi school requires only qada, not fidya, because fidya is a special exception for the elderly and cannot be analogized.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text addresses two related rulings: first, that delaying qada (makeup fasts) from a previous Ramadan does not trigger fidya; second, that pregnant and nursing women are permitted to break their fast if they fear harm, but must later make up the days. The text also engages with the Shafi'i view that fidya is due for the benefit to the child. By understanding the Hanafi proofs—especially the hadith and the principle that exceptions (like fidya for the elderly) are not extended by analogy—you will grasp a core Hanafi methodology in fasting laws.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rulings
The passage covers: (1) a person who delays making up missed Ramadan fasts without an excuse, and (2) pregnant and nursing women who fear harm. Note the ruling for each regarding fidya.
2
Extract the hadith dalil
The hadith: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and nursing woman the fast.' Write it down and note its transmission (ahad, zanni al-thubut).
The hadith is classified as ahad, but still operative in fiqh rulings.
3
Understand the Hanafi deduction from the hadith
The Hanafi school deduces from the hadith that the fast is lifted, but no mention of fidya, so only qada is required. Also, fidya is a substitute for fasting; combining the original (qada) with a substitute (fidya) is impermissible.
4
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues by qiyas to the decrepit old man: the child benefits from the break, so fidya is due. Identify the analogical reasoning.
Shafi'i uses the illah of benefiting a non-obligated person.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: fidya for the old man is khilaf al-qiyas (an exception to analogy) and cannot be extended. The child is not obligated to fast; the mother is, and she will make up the fasts. Thus no fidya.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that exceptions are not extended by qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض على القضاء، وللحامل والمرضع إن خافتا على الولد والنفس، وللشيخ الفاني، وهو يفدي فقط، وللمتطوع. = أي الشيخ الفاني أي الحامل والمرضع راجع إلى المرضع وإلا فلو قدم القضاء وقع عن الأداء، ثم قضى الأول؛ لأن وقته العمر، ولا فدية عليه خلافاً للشافعي فيما إذا كان التأخير لغير عذر، فيطعم عن كل يوم مسكيناً، ولنا أن تأخير الأداء عن وقته لا يوجب الفدية، فتأخير القضاء وهو مطلق عن الوقت أولى أن لا يوجبها، ولأن الفدية خلف عن الصوم فلا يجوز الجمع بين الأصل والخلف. (مستخلص، فتح) القضاء: أي الرمضان الذي قبله ثم صام القضاء ولا فدية عليه (ع) وللحامل [ أي الفطر جائز للحامل أيضاً للحرج كالمسافر. (ع)] لحديث: "إن الله وضع عن المسافر الصوم، وشطر الصلاة، وعن الحامل والمرضع الصوم"؛ ولأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة، لاسيما عند إعسار الزوج، أي يجب عليهما القضاء بلا كفارة وفدية، وقال الشافعي: تجب الفدية فيما إذا خافت على الولد؛ لأنه إفطار انتفع به من لم يلزمه الصوم، وهو الولد، فتجب الفدية كإفطار الشيخ الفاني، ولنا أن الفدية وجبت على الشيخ الفاني بخلاف القياس فلا يلحق به خلافه؛ لأن الفدية على الشيخ لعجزه عن الصوم الواجب والطفل لا يجب عليه الصوم، وإنما يجب على أمه وهي تصوم القضاء، فلا يجب عليها غيره، وقيل: المراد من المرضع الظئر؛ لأنها لا تتمكن من الامتناع عن الإرضاع؛ لوجوبه عليها بعقد الإجارة، وأما الأم فليس عليها الإرضاع، فإن امتنعت على الأب استجار مرضعة أخرى. (عيني، فتح) وهو والمرضع: بالجر عطف على الحامل، أي الفطر للمرضع أيضاً ولو ظئراً والقضاء بلا كفارة ولا فدية. (ط) والنفس: راجع إلى الحامل وعليه القضاء بلا فدية. (ع)
Chapter on Excuses: concerning the qada (makeup fasts), and for the pregnant and nursing woman if they fear for the child and themselves, and for the decrepit old man, and he only pays fidya, and for the voluntary faster. = Meaning the decrepit old man, meaning the pregnant and nursing woman refers to the nursing woman. Otherwise, if he presented the qada, it would fall as performance (ada), then he makes up the first; because its time is life (lifetime), and no fidya is upon him, contrary to al-Shafi'i when the delay is without excuse, so he feeds a poor person for each day. And for us: delaying the performance from its time does not necessitate fidya, so delaying the qada, which is unrestricted from time, is more deserving of not necessitating it. And because fidya is a substitute for fasting, so combining the original and substitute is not permissible. (Mukhtasar, Fath) Qada means the previous Ramadan, then he fasted the qada and no fidya is upon him. (Ayni) And for the pregnant woman: breaking the fast is permissible for the pregnant woman also due to hardship like the traveler. (Ayni) Due to the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and nursing woman the fast.' And because breastfeeding is obligatory upon the mother religiously, especially when the husband is unable, so they must perform qada without kaffarah or fidya. Al-Shafi'i said: Fidya is obligatory when she fears for the child, because it is a breaking of fast from which one who is not obliged to fast (the child) benefited, so fidya is obligatory like the breaking of the decrepit old man. And for us: Fidya was obligated on the decrepit old man contrary to analogy, so it should not be analogized upon others. Because fidya for the old man is due to his inability to perform the obligatory fast, and the child is not obliged to fast; rather, it is obliged upon his mother, and she fasts the qada, so nothing else is obligatory upon her. And it was said: The intended meaning of 'al-murdi' (the nursing woman) is the wet nurse, because she cannot refrain from breastfeeding due to its obligation by contract of hire. As for the mother, breastfeeding is not obligatory upon her; if she refuses, the father may hire another wet nurse. (Ayni, Fath) And 'al-murdi' is in the genitive, conjoined to 'al-hamil', meaning breaking the fast is allowed for the nursing woman also, even if a wet nurse, and qada without kaffarah or fidya. (T) And 'an-nafs' (the soul) refers to the pregnant woman, and upon her is qada without fidya. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. It opens the chapter on excuses (al-‘awāriḍ) in the Book of Fasting, listing four categories of people: (1) one who owes makeup fasts (qada) from a previous Ramadan, (2) pregnant and nursing women, (3) the decrepit old man, and (4) the voluntary faster. The commentary first discusses the ruling on a person who delays making up missed Ramadan fasts without a valid excuse. According to the Hanafi school, no fidya (expiation) is required for such delay, contrary to Imam al-Shafi‘i who holds that feeding a poor person for each day is necessary. The Hanafi proof is that delaying an obligatory act from its time does not necessitate fidya; delaying qada, which is unbounded in time, is even less likely to. Furthermore, fidya is a substitute for fasting, and one cannot combine the original duty (qada) with a substitute. The text then turns to pregnant and nursing women. The ruling is that they are permitted to break the fast if they fear harm to themselves or their child/offspring, based on the hadith that Allah has lifted fasting from the traveler, pregnant, and nursing women. They must later make up the missed days (qada) but no kaffarah (expiation) or fidya is required. Imam al-Shafi‘i argues that if she fears for the child, fidya is due because the benefit goes to someone (the child) who is not obliged to fast, analogous to the decrepit old man. The Hanafi response explains that the fidya on the old man is an exception to analogy (khilāf al-qiyās), so it cannot be extended. The child is not obliged to fast, so no fidya on his behalf. The mother is obliged to make up the fasts, so only qada is required. The text also clarifies that the term 'al-murḍi‘' (nursing woman) in the primary text is interpreted by some as referring to a wet nurse (ẓi'r) who is hired to breastfeed, as she cannot refuse due to contractual obligation. For the mother, breastfeeding is not legally obligatory, so the father may hire a wet nurse if the mother refuses. Thus, the ruling applies to both. The phrase 'على النفس' (fearing for themselves) is specifically for the pregnant woman, and she must make up the fasts with no fidya. This passage is from the Hanafi legal tradition and highlights the school’s nuanced approach to exceptions and its reliance on hadith and qiyas.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qadā'): The obligation to make up missed fasts after Ramadan.
الحامل (al-ḥāmil): A pregnant woman who fears harm to herself or her fetus.
المرضع (al-murḍi‘): A nursing woman, either the mother or a wet nurse hired to breastfeed.
الفدية (al-fidyah): A compensatory payment of food to the poor for each day of missed fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a pregnant woman who breaks her fast due to fear for her child?
A) She must make up the fast only (qada).
B) She must pay fidya only.
C) She must make up the fast and pay fidya.
D) She is excused and need not make up or pay fidya.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, pregnant and nursing women who break their fast out of fear need only make up the missed days (qada), with no fidya, in contrast to the Shafi'i school.

The Ruling on the Decrepit Old Man: Fidya for Permanent Incapacity
Ruling on fidya for permanent incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the elderly who cannot fast and apply the distinction between fidya and qada.

Core Idea
The elderly who cannot fast are not excused — they must pay fidya, based on Quran and consensus.

This lesson addresses the ruling for individuals permanently unable to fast due to old age or terminal illness. The Hanafi school differentiates between temporary excuses (qada) and permanent incapacity (fidya). We explore the textual evidence and the scholarly disagreement with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is fasting Ramadan. The ruling for the decrepit old man (and similar) is that he is allowed to break the fast and must pay fidya for each missed day, if financially able.
The fidya is wajib (obligatory), not merely recommended.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 2:184, interpreted by Ibn Abbas and the Companions to refer specifically to the elderly who cannot fast.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala, so the Hanafi interpretation uses tafsir and ijma'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase 'yutīqūnahu' is interpreted as 'can barely fast' or 'cannot fast' based on the explanation of Ibn Abbas. This yields the ruling of fidya.
The Hanafi school relies on this tafsir over a literal reading.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik and the old view of Imam al-Shafi'i hold that no fidya is required, drawing an analogy to the sick, child, and insane who have no compensation.
The opposing school uses qiyas; the Hanafis respond that qiyas cannot override nass and ijma'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because it is supported by explicit text and consensus of the Companions, and no valid counter-proof exists.
This is a clear example of tark al-qiyas li wujūd al-nass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشيخ الفاني [ أي الهرم وهو الذي فنيت قوته ولم يقدر على الصيام، أي الفطر للشيخ الفاني أيضاً. (ط)] قيل: هو من جاوز الخمسين والمدار على عدم قدرة الصوم، أن يكون كل يوم في نقص إلى أن يموت والعجوز الكبيرة التي لا ترجى قدرتها على الصوم كالشيخ الفاني؛ كان ويلحق به من في معناه وأيس من حياته، وقوله: "وهو يفدي" بفتح الياء التحتية أي وجوباً لو موسراً، وإلا فيستغفر الله، وهذا إذا كان الصوم أصلا بنفسه وخوطب بأدائه حتى لو لزمه الصوم ككفارة يمين ثم عجز لم تجز له الفدية، وقال مالك: لا فديه عليه، وبه قال الشافعي في القديم؛ لأنه عاجز عن الصوم، فأشبه المريض إذا مات قبل البرء وصار كالصغير والمجنون، ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ ﴾ (البقرة: 184) قال ابن عباس: هي للشيخ الكبير والمرأة الكبيرة لا يستطيعان الصوم فيطعمان، ولم يرو عن أحد من الصحابة خلاف ذلك فكان إجماعا، ولا يجوز المصير إلى القياس مع وجود النص. (فتح المعين) يفدي: أي يطعم لكل يوم مسكينا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ ﴾ (البقرة:184) أي لا يطيقونه. (ط، ع) فقط : أي يفدي هو فقط دون المسافر والمريض والحامل والمرضع. (ط)
And for the decrepit old man [meaning the aged one whose strength has perished and who is unable to fast, i.e., breaking the fast is also for the decrepit old man. (T)] It was said: He is one who has passed fifty, and the criterion is inability to fast, that he decreases every day until death, and the old woman who cannot be hoped to have ability to fast is like the decrepit old man; so it is, and those in a similar state and hopeless of recovery are joined to him. And His statement: 'and he pays fiduciary' with fatha on the under-dot ya, meaning obligation if he is wealthy, otherwise he seeks forgiveness from Allah. And this applies when the fast is originally obligatory upon him and he is addressed to perform it; if a fast becomes obligatory upon him, such as expiation for an oath, and then he becomes unable, fiduciary is not permissible for him. Malik said: No fiduciary is upon him, and al-Shafi'i held the same in the old view; because he is unable to fast, so he resembles the sick person who dies before recovery and becomes like the child and the insane. And for us is the saying of the Exalted: 'And upon those who are able [to fast]' (2:184). Ibn Abbas said: It is for the old man and the old woman who cannot fast, so they feed. And no contrary report is known from any of the Companions, so it is consensus. And it is not permissible to resort to analogy in the presence of the text. (Fath al-Mu'in) He pays fiduciary: meaning he feeds a poor person for each day, due to the saying of the Exalted: 'And upon those who are able [to fast], a fiduciary of feeding a poor person' (2:184), meaning those who cannot bear it. (T, A) 'Only': meaning he alone pays fiduciary, not the traveler, the sick, the pregnant, or the nursing woman. (T)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, focusing on the decrepit old man (al-shaykh al-fānī) who is physically unable to fast. The commentary defines this person as one whose strength has depleted and continues to decline until death, including the old woman in a similar state. The key ruling is that such a person is allowed to break the fast and must only pay fidya (feeding a poor person for each missed day) if financially able; otherwise, he seeks forgiveness. This is in contrast to the views of Imam Malik and the earlier opinion of Imam al-Shafi‘i, who held that no fiduciary is required, analogizing to the sick person or the insane. The Hanafi school relies on the Quranic verse 2:184, interpreted by Ibn Abbas to refer specifically to the elderly who cannot fast, and on the consensus (ijma‘) of the Companions, as no dissent is recorded. The Hanafi critique states that analogy cannot override the clear textual evidence. The text further specifies that fiduciary is only for the elderly; it does not apply to the traveler, the sick, the pregnant, or the nursing woman. This highlights the Hanafi principle that fidya is an exceptional rule for those with permanent incapacity, not for temporary excuses. The passage is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary, firmly establishing the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الشيخ الفاني (al-shaykh al-fānī): An old man whose strength has perished and who cannot fast, often past fifty (but criterion is inability).
الفدية (al-fidyah): Feeding a poor person for each missed day, obligatory on the elderly who cannot fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the obligation of an elderly person who cannot fast due to old age?
A) He must make up the fasts later (qada).
B) He must pay fidya (feed a poor person for each day).
C) He is completely excused with no obligation.
D) He must combine qada and fidya.

Key Takeaway
The elderly permanently unable to fast must pay fidya (feed a poor person) each day, based on Quran and consensus.

Breaking a Voluntary Fast and the Obligation of Qada
Ruling with proof on breaking voluntary fast and qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast, explain the obligation of qada, and evaluate the evidence from hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
A voluntary faster who breaks his fast must make it up (qada), even if breaking was allowed with an excuse.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common practical question: can a person break a voluntary fast once they have started? The Hanafi school provides a detailed answer, distinguishing between cases with and without excuse, and crucially, requiring that the missed fast be made up on another day. The ruling is supported by the Prophet's own practice and the principle of not invalidating good deeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and note the two Hanafi positions.
The act is breaking a voluntary fast. According to one narration (Abu Yusuf), breaking is allowed even without excuse, but qada is wajib. The correct position (sahih) restricts breaking to valid excuses.
Focus on the sahih position as mufta bihi.
2
Examine the dalil for qada.
The primary proof is the hadith from Aisha where the Prophet broke his voluntary fast and said 'But I will fast a day in its place' (al-Nasa'i), and the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33).
The hadith is ahad but considered strong enough for wajib.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response.
Imam al-Shafi'i cites the hadith 'The voluntary faster is his own master' to argue no qada is required. The Hanafi response is that this hadith is weak in its chain, as noted by al-Tirmidhi and al-Qurtubi.
This shows the importance of hadith criticism in fiqh.
4
Evaluate the tarjih and the conditions for breaking.
The correct view is that breaking is allowed only with an excuse (e.g., hospitality) and qada is wajib. Breaking without excuse is not permissible. Additional conditions: before zawal if the host would be offended; after zawal only for parental obedience.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi requires qada, Shafi'i does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتطوع أي للمتطوع بالصوم الفطر أيضاً. (ط) بغير عذر في رواية، ويقضي، ولو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر أمسك بقية يومه ولم يقض شيئا، ولو نوى المسافر الإفطار ثم قدم ونوى الصوم في وقته وجوبا تشبيها للصائمين بغير عذر في رواية عن أبي يوسف، وذكر الكرخي وأبو بكر عن أصحابنا أنه لا يفطر إلا من عذر؛ لما روي أنه عليه قال: "إذا دعي أحدكم إلى الطعام فليجب، فإن كان مفطراً فليأكل، وإن كان صائماً فليصل" أي فليدع، ولو كان الفطر جائزاً كان الأفضل الفطر لإجابة الدعوة التي هي سنة، ووجه رواية أبي يوسف ماروت عائشة لما أنها قالت: دخل النبي الله ذات يوم فقال: "هل عندكم شيء؟" فقلنا: لا، قال: "إني إذا صائم"، ثم أتى يوماً آخر، فقلنا: يا رسول الله، أهدي لنا حيس، فقال: "أرنيه فلقد أصبحت صائماً" فأكل، رواه مسلم، وزاد النسائي "ولكن أصوم يوماً مكانه" ولا خلاف أنه يجوز للعذر ، والضيافة عذر، والصحيح من المذهب أنه إن كان صاحب الدعوة لا يتأذى بترك الإفطار لا يفطر وإلا يفطر إذا كان قبل الزوال، وبعده لا يفطر إلا إذا كان عقوقا بالوالدين أو بأحدهما. (عيني، مسكين) في رواية بشرط أن يكون من نيته القضاء، وفي أخرى لا يحل إلا بعذر وهي الصحيحة. (ط) ويقضي: أي المتطوع إذا أفطر بغير عذر أو غيره يقضي الصوم، وقال الشافعي: ليس عليه قضاء؛ لأن المتطوع أمير نفسه ومتبرع بما أدى فلا يلزم ما يتبرع به، ولنا ما روينا من رواية النسائي: "ولكن أصوم يوما مكانه" وهو قول أبي بكر وعمر وعلي وابن عباس وغيرهم، ولأن المؤدى صارت قربة فتجب صيانتها عن البطلان؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تُبْطِلُوا أَعْمَالَكُمْ ﴾ (محمد:33) فإذا وجب المضى وجب القضاء بتركه، وحديث: "الصائم المتطوع أمير نفسه" في إسناده مقال قاله الترمذي وقال القرطبي: غير صحيح. (عيني، مستخلص)
And for the voluntary faster: meaning the voluntary faster may also break the fast. (T) Without an excuse in one narration, and he makes it up, and if a child reaches puberty or an unbeliever accepts Islam, he refrains for the rest of his day and does not make up anything, and if a traveler intends to break his fast then returns and intends fasting in its time, his fasting is valid, as an obligation for the sake of resembling the fasting ones ... without an excuse in a narration from Abu Yusuf, and al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr mentioned from our companions that he does not break except with an excuse; due to what was narrated that he said: 'When one of you is invited to food, let him respond; if he is not fasting, let him eat; if he is fasting, let him pray' meaning let him supplicate. And if breaking was permissible, the better would be to break to accept the invitation, which is sunnah. And the basis of the narration of Abu Yusuf is what Aisha narrated that the Prophet entered one day and said: 'Do you have anything?' We said: No. He said: 'Then I am fasting.' Then another day, we said: O Messenger of Allah, some hais (a mixture) was gifted to us. He said: 'Show it to me; indeed, I am fasting in the morning.' So he ate. Muslim narrated it, and al-Nasa'i added: 'But I will fast a day in its place.' And there is no disagreement that it is permissible with an excuse, and hospitality is an excuse. The correct view in the school is that if the host is not offended by not breaking, he does not break; otherwise, he breaks if before midday; after midday, he does not break except if it causes disobedience to parents or one of them. (Ayni, Miskin) In one narration, it is conditioned that he intend to make it up; in another, it is not permissible except with an excuse, and that is the correct one. (T) And he makes it up: meaning the voluntary faster, if he breaks without an excuse or otherwise, makes up the fast. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no qada upon him, because the voluntary faster is his own master and is donating what he performs, so he is not obligated for what he donates. And for us is what we narrated from the narration of al-Nasa'i: 'But I will fast a day in its place,' and it is the saying of Abu Bakr, Umar, Ali, Ibn Abbas, and others, and because the performed act becomes a supererogatory worship, so its protection from nullification is obligatory, due to the saying of the Exalted: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' (Muhammad:33). So when its continuation becomes obligatory, then its abandonment necessitates qada. And the hadith 'The voluntary faster is his own master' has a weakness in its chain, as al-Tirmidhi said, and al-Qurtubi said it is not authentic. (Ayni, Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute, dealing with the voluntary faster (al-mutatawwi‘). The core issue: may a person who voluntarily started a fast break it without an excuse? According to one narration from Abu Yusuf, it is permissible (even without a valid excuse), and the person must then make up (qada) that day. This is supported by the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet broke his voluntary fast after having intended it. Al-Karkhi and others quote a hadith about accepting invitations, which suggests the better choice is to break when invited. The school’s correct position (ṣaḥīḥ al-madhhab) is that breaking is permissible only with an excuse; hospitality (ḍiyāfah) counts as an excuse. But there is nuance: if the host would not be offended, it is better not to break; otherwise, one should break before midday (before zawāl). After midday, one should not break unless it involves disobedience to parents. The passage then addresses whether qada is required: the Hanafi school says yes, because of the Prophet’s action and the verse forbidding invalidation of deeds. The voluntary faster is not his own master in the sense that once he starts a good deed, he should complete it; if he breaks, he must compensate. This is in contrast to Imam al-Shafi‘i, who holds no qada is required, based on the weak hadith 'al-mutatawwi‘ amīr nafsihi'. The Hanafi response demonstrates that the hadith is weak, and the stronger evidence supports qada. The passage also includes a brief mention of the traveler who changes his intention, but that is not elaborated. Overall, the Hanafi ruling is that a voluntary faster who breaks his fast must make it up, regardless of the reason.

Key Terms
المتطوع (al-mutatawwi‘): One who voluntarily performs an act of worship, here specifically a voluntary fast (nafl).
القضاء (al-qadā'): Making up a broken voluntary fast on another day.
الضيافة (al-ḍiyāfah): Hospitality; a valid excuse for breaking a voluntary fast, provided it does not cause offense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who breaks a voluntary fast without an excuse?
A) He must make it up (qada) on another day.
B) He is excused and need not make it up.
C) He must pay fidya only.
D) He is sinful but no qada required.

Key Takeaway
A voluntary faster who breaks his fast, even with an excuse, must make it up (qada) on another day according to the Hanafi school.

Imsak without Qada for Child or Convert During Ramadan
Explanation of Hanafi ruling on child reaching puberty or convert during Ramadan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a child reaching puberty or an unbeliever converting during Ramadan and explain the reasoning distinguishing fasting from prayer.

Core Idea
Partial eligibility during Ramadan day does not impose qada; only imsak is wajib.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a child who becomes baligh or a non-Muslim who accepts Islam during the day in Ramadan. The Hanafi school draws a careful distinction between the obligation of the entire fast and mere respect for the remaining time. Understanding this ruling helps clarify when an obligation arises and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a child reaching puberty or an unbeliever converting during the day. The ruling is that they must refrain (imsaak) for the rest of the day, but do not need to make up (qada) that day.
2
Locate the dalil
The opposing view (Malik/Zufar) uses qiyas to prayer: catching part of the time is like catching all of it, thus the whole day must be made up.
The text refutes this analogy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi response distinguishes fasting from prayer: in prayer, one can complete the missed parts after entering the time; in fasting, the first part of the day cannot be retrieved, so partial time does not create full obligation.
The obligation of fasting is for the continuous period from dawn to dusk.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik and the Hanafi scholar Zufar hold that qada is required. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold no qada.
Zufar's view is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority Hanafi view: no qada. The text explicitly sides with this view and notes that even if the person broke the fast, no qada is needed because the day was not obligatory.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر أمسك بقية يومه ولم يقض شيئا خلافاً لمالك وزفر، فهما يوجبان عليه قضاء ذلك اليوم؛ لأن إدراك جزء من الوقت كإدراك كله كما في الصلاة قلنا لا يتمكن من أداء الصوم بإدراك جزء من النهار بخلاف الصلاة. (فتح عيني) ولم يقض شيئاً: أي لم يقض هذا اليوم وإن أفطر فيه؛ لأن الصوم غير واجب فيه. (مسكين)
And if a child reaches puberty or an unbeliever accepts Islam, he refrains from the remainder of his day and does not make up anything, contrary to Malik and Zufar, for they both obligate him to make up that day because grasping part of the time is like grasping all of it, as in prayer. We said: He cannot perform the fast by grasping part of the day, unlike prayer. (Fath 'Ayni) And he does not make up anything: meaning he does not make up this day even if he broke the fast in it, because fasting is not obligatory in it. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-madhhab dispute (though here the opponents are Malik and Zufar), addressing what happens when a child attains puberty (bulūgh) or an unbeliever embraces Islam during the day in Ramadan. The ruling of the Hanafi school is clear: such a person must refrain from eating and drinking for the remainder of that day (imṣāk) out of respect for the sacred time, but he is not required to make up (qada) that day, nor any previous days missed before eligibility. This is contrary to the opinions of Imam Malik and Imam Zufar (a Hanafi but with a differing view), who hold that since such a person was alive for part of the day, that part triggers the obligation for the whole day, analogous to prayer where catching a part of the time makes the whole prayer obligatory. The Hanafi response distinguishes between prayer and fasting: in prayer, if one catches the time, he can complete the prayer; in fasting, merely catching the beginning of the day does not enable one to complete the full fast, as the obligation to fast is for the entire day. Therefore, no qada is required. The commentary further clarifies that even if this person inadvertently ate during the rest of the day (thinking he is not obligated), he still does not owe qada. The basis is that the entire day was not obligatory upon him. This ruling highlights the Hanafi principle that fasting is a continuous act from dawn to dusk, and partial eligibility does not create a full obligation.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imṣāk): Refraining from eating, drinking, and other invalidators for the rest of the day out of respect.
القضاء (al-qadā'): Making up the missed day on another day; not required in this case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a boy reaches puberty at noon during Ramadan, what must he do?
A) He must refrain from eating for the rest of the day and later make up that day.
B) He must refrain for the rest of the day, but no make-up is required.
C) He may continue eating as the day was not obligatory.
D) He must only refrain if he had the intention to fast from dawn.

Key Takeaway
A child who reaches puberty or an unbeliever who converts during Ramadan must only refrain for the remainder of the day, with no obligation to make it up.
