Rulings on Breaking a Voluntary Fast and Its Qada
Rulings on excuses for breaking a voluntary fast and related qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast, cite the primary proofs (hadith and Quran), and explain the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast broken without excuse requires qada — the Prophet's command to replace it is binding.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario: what happens if someone breaks a voluntary fast without a valid excuse? The Hanafi school requires making up the fast (qada) based on strong textual evidence, while al-Shafi'i disagrees. In this lesson, we will dissect the ruling, its proofs, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its legal category
First, note that the passage rules that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse necessitates qada. Establish that this is a 'wajib' ruling, not fard, due to the zanni thubut of the dalil. The relied-upon position is that breaking is permissible with qada.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf from Abu Yusuf, but the mufta bihi is the majority view.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalil naqli)
The passage cites the hadith 'But I will fast a day in its place' (narrated by Nasa'i) and the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (Muhammad 47:33). Identify these as the main evidence.
The hadith is ahad and zanni al-thubut, but its meaning (dalala) is qati for the Hanafis because the imperative indicates obligation.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof proves the ruling)
In the hadith, the Prophet (peace be upon him) uses the command 'أصوم' (I will fast) which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation. The verse forbids invalidating deeds, implying that a validly started voluntary act must be completed or replaced.
The Shafi'i counter-proof (voluntary faster is master of himself) is deemed weak due to chain issues.
4
Understand the inter-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i argues that no qada is required because the volunteer is not bound. The Hanafis respond that the weakness of the Shafi'i hadith and the strength of their own proofs settle the matter.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis require qada and consider breaking sinful without excuse; Shafi'is do not.
5
Apply the ruling to real scenarios
Consider a case: A person starts a voluntary fast, then receives an invitation to lunch before zawal. If the host would be offended by refusal, the guest may break the fast but must make it up later. If after zawal, breaking is only allowed for parent-obedience.
The mufta bihi position from al-Marghinani and later Hanafi authorities is followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض بغير عذر في رواية، ويقضي، ولو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر أمسك بقيّة للمتطوع بالصوم الفطر أيضا بعد الفجر كل واحد منهما يومه ولم يقض شيئا، ولو نوى المسافر الإفطار ثم قدم ونوى الصوم في وقته وجوبا تشبيها للصائمين ك و ف ك كل منهما شح، ويقضي بإغماء سوى يوم ، أي ما فاته من الصوم أي لا يقضى يوما مصره من السفر بغير عذر في رواية عن أبي يوسف، وذكر الكرخي وأبو بكر عن أصحابنا أنه لا يفطر إلا من عذر؛ لما روي أنه عليه قال: "إذا دعي أحدكم إلى الطعام فليجب، فإن كان مفطراً فليأكل، وإن كان صائماً فليصل" أي فليدع، ولو كان الفطر جائزاً كان الأفضل الفطر لإجابة الدعوة التي هي سنة، ووجه رواية أبي يوسف ماروت عائشة لما أنها قالت: دخل النبي الله ذات يوم فقال: "هل عندكم شيء؟" فقلنا: لا، قال: "إني إذا صائم"، ثم أتى يوماً آخر، فقلنا: يا رسول الله، أهدي لنا حيس، فقال: "أرنيه فلقد أصبحت صائماً" فأكل، رواه مسلم، وزاد النسائي "ولكن أصوم يوماً مكانه" ولا خلاف أنه يجوز للعذر ، والضيافة عذر، والصحيح من المذهب أنه إن كان صاحب الدعوة لا يتأذى بترك الإفطار لا يفطر وإلا يفطر إذا كان قبل الزوال، وبعده لا يفطر إلا إذا كان عقوقا بالوالدين أو بأحدهما. (عيني، مسكين) في رواية بشرط أن يكون من نيته القضاء، وفي أخرى لا يحل إلا بعذر وهي الصحيحة. (ط) ويقضي: أي المتطوع إذا أفطر بغير عذر أو غيره يقضي الصوم، وقال الشافعي: ليس عليه قضاء؛ لأن المتطوع أمير نفسه ومتبرع بما أدى فلا يلزم ما يتبرع ،به ولنا ما روينا من رواية النسائي: "ولكن أصوم يوماً مكانه" وهو قول أبي بكر وعمر وعلي وابن عباس وغيرهم، ولأن المؤدى صارت قربة فتجب صيانتها عن البطلان؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تُبْطِلُوا أَعْمَالَكُمْ ﴾ (محمد:۳۳) فإذا وجب المضى وجب القضاء بتركه، وحديث: "الصائم المتطوع أمير نفسه" في إسناده مقال قاله الترمذي وقال القرطبي: غير صحيح. (عيني، مستخلص) صبي: أو صبية بالسن أو بغيره في بعض نهار رمضان. (ط، ع) أمسك بقية يومه: كما أنه إذا أكل في رمضان ناسياً فظن أن ذلك يفطره، فأكل بعده عمداً يجب القضاء، وفي رواية استحباباً، ثم اختلفوا في هذا الإمساك، فقيل: مستحب، وقيل: واجب وهو الصحيح، وكذا يجب الإمساك على كل من صار أهلا في آخر النهار كحائض أو نفساء طهرت بعد الفجر أو معه وكمجنون أفاق ومريض برئ ومسافر أقام قبل الزوال أو بعده، وقوله: "ولم يقض شيئا" أي لم يقض هذا اليوم ولا ما مضى خلافاً لمالك ،وزفر، فهما يوجبان عليه قضاء ذلك اليوم؛ لأن إدراك جزء من الوقت كإدراك كله كما في الصلاة قلنا لا يتمكن من أداء الصوم بإدراك جزء من النهار بخلاف الصلاة. (فتح عيني) ولم يقض شيئاً: أي لم يقض هذا اليوم وإن أفطر فيه؛ لأن الصوم غير واجب فيه. (مسكين) وقته أي في وقت النية، فكان الأولى تأنيث الضمير وهو قبل الضحوة الكبرى. (فتح) صح صومه سواء كان فرضاً أو نفلاً . (ع)
Section on impediments. Without excuse, according to one narration, he must make up. And if a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam, he restrains for the remainder of his day and does not make up anything. And if a traveler intends to break the fast then returns and intends fasting in its time, his fast is valid. The discussion of invitation: it is not permissible to break except with an excuse. The correct position: if the host is not offended by leaving the fast, he does not break; otherwise he breaks if before zawal. He makes up any missed fast. The voluntary faster if he breaks without excuse or otherwise must make up. Al-Shafi'i said: no qada because the volunteer is master of himself. Our proof: the hadith 'But I will fast a day in its place' and the verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds.' The hadith 'The voluntary faster is master of himself' is weak. As for the child: he restrains for the rest of his day; dispute on whether it is mustahab or wajib, correct is wajib. He does not make up this day nor the past, contrary to Malik and Zufar. For the traveler: his fast is valid if he intended before dhuha al-kubra.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a section on impediments (العوارض) in the book of fasting. It first discusses the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast without an excuse: according to one narration, it is permitted but requires qada. Then it addresses the case of a child reaching puberty or a disbeliever accepting Islam during the day in Ramadan: they must abstain for the rest of the day but do not owe qada for that day or prior days. The traveler who intends to break his fast, then returns to his city and intends fasting within the time of intention (before dhuha al-kubra) has a valid fast. The passage then extensively discusses the issue of being invited to break a voluntary fast. Two narrations are presented: one from Abu Yusuf that breaking is permissible only with an excuse, and another that it is generally permissible. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that if the host will not be hurt by the guest's refusal, it is better not to break; otherwise, breaking is permissible before zawal. After zawal, breaking is only allowed if it involves obedience to parents. The obligation of qada for a broken voluntary fast is established by the hadith 'But I will fast a day in its place' (narrated by Nasa'i) and the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (Muhammad 33). The Shafi'i position that no qada is required is rejected because the hadith they rely on is weak. The passage concludes with the ruling on the child, affirming that qada is not required for that day or the past, contrary to Malik and Zufar, because fasting requires the entire day and is not analogous to prayer.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs a voluntary (nafl) act of worship, here a voluntary fast.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed religious obligation that was validly started but broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who breaks a voluntary fast without a valid excuse?
A) No sin, no qada needed.
B) Sinful and must make up the fast (qada).
C) Only sinful but no qada.
D) The fast becomes invalid and only atonement (kaffarah) is required.

Key Takeaway
A voluntary fast once started must be either completed or compensated with qada; breaking without a valid excuse is sinful, and compensation is always wajib according to the Hanafi school.

Qada for Fainting (Ighma') During Ramadan
Ruling on qada for fainting with Maliki dissent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for fainting, distinguish between fainting at night and during the day, and explain the disagreement with Imam Malik.

Core Idea
Fainting during the day requires qada unless fainting at night with presumed intention.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on fasting obligations when a person faints. The key distinction is whether the fainting occurred at night, allowing a presumption of intention, or during the day. We will explore the conditions that trigger qada and the rare case of Malik's dissent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fainting (ighma) during a Ramadan fast. The ruling is that qada is required unless the fainting occurred at night and the person had intended to fast.
2
Locate the basis
The Hanafi position is based on the assumption that a person intending to fast at night is presumed upright. If evidence shows no intention, qada is required.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds that qada is required even for fainting at night, contrary to the Hanafi view.
Malik's position implies he requires intention for each day individually.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
For Malik, every day of fainting, even at night, requires qada; for Hanafis, night fainting with known intention does not.

كتاب الصوم
٢٣٦
فصل في العوارض
حدث في ليلته، وبجنون غير ممتد، وبإمساك بلا نية صوم وفطر، ولو قدم مسافر
الإغماء
أي ويقضي مافاته
أي يقضي مافات عنه
في رمضان
مصره في بعض النهار
حدث في ليلته [ أي فيه أم في ليلته فلا يقضيه لوجود الصوم إلا إذا علم أنه لم ينو . (ط، ع)] أي يقضي الصوم الفائت بالإغماء سوى يوم حدث الإغماء في ليلته، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحكم لا يختلف بحدوث الإغماء في اليوم؛ لأنه إذا لم يجب القضاء بحدوثه في الليلة مع أنها غير محل للصوم فلأن لا يجب بحدوثه في اليوم أولى، ووجه عدم القضاء: أنه نوى من الليل حملاً لحاله على الصلاح حتى لو كان منهتكا يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو مسافراً، قضى الكل؛ لعدم ما يدل على وجود النية، فالحاصل أنه إذا علم أنه نوى فلا شك في عدم القضاء، وإن علم أنه لم ينو فلا شك في القضاء خلافاً لمالك، فإنه يوجب قضاء اليوم الذي حدث فيه الإغماء أيضاً، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط النية عنده لصوم كل يوم وهو خلاف ما سبق عنه. (عيني، فتح) ويجنون غير ممتد: اعلم أن الجنون ينافي النية التي هي شرط العبادات، فلا يجب القضاء الممتد منه مطلقا، مع أن يكون مستوعبا لشهر رمضان كله للحرج وغير الممتد جعل كالنوم؛ لأن الجنون لا ينفي أصل الوجوب، والمراد باستيعابه أن لا يفيق مقدار ما يمكنه إنشاء الصوم فيه حتى لو أفاق في ليلة أو في آخر يوم منه فقط لا قضاء عليه، وعليه الفتوى سواء كان أصليا أو عارضياً أي يقضي ما فات بجنون غير مستغرق للشهر، وقيل: هذا إذا بلغ مفيقا ثم جن، أما إذا بلغ مجنونا وهو الجنون الأصلي ثم أفاق في بعض الشهر فعن محمد أنه ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى إلحاقا له بالصبي، واختاره بعض المتأخرين، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجب عليه قضاء ما مضى من الشهر كالجنون العارض ولا رواية فيه عن الإمام، والأصح أنه ليس على المجنون الأصلي قضاء ما مضى، وقال زفر والشافعي: يسقط القضاء في جنون غير ممتد أيضاً؛ لأن القضاء فرع وجوب الأداء وهو منتف لعدم الأهلية فكذا ما يبتنى عليه، ونحن لا نسلم أن القضاء يترتب على وجوب الأداء بل يجب في الذمة لوجود السبب وهو ألا تری أن النائم يجب عليه القضاء، وهو مرفوع عنه في حق الأداء، وكذا المسافر يجب عليه القضاء وهو الشهر، دون الأداء، وإذا تحقق الوجوب بلا مانع يتعين القضاء، وقال مالك: يقضي في الحالين. (عيني، فتح) غير ممتد أي مستغرق للشهر ولو استغرقه لا يقضي. (ط) وبإمساك : يعني من لم ينو بالإمساك صوما ولا فطرا رمضان يقضي ولا يكفر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية؛ لأن المستحق عليه الإمساك، فعلى أي وجه وجد يقع عنه كما إذا وهب كل النصاب من الفقير ولنا أن المستحق عليه هو الإمساك بجهة العبادة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ ﴾ (البيئة:٥) والإخلاص لا يكون بدون النية، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في لزوم القضاء ووجوب الكفارة، فإن لم يأكل لا يلزم القضاء عنده، وإن أكل تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأنه صائم عنده، وعند أبي حنيفة : الحكم على عكسه؛ لأنه غير صائم وعندهما إن أكل بعد الزوال فكما قال أبو حنيفة: لا كفارة عليه، وإن أكل قبله تجب عليه الكفارة؛ لأنه فوت إمكان التحصيل. (مستخلص، فتح) وفطر: أي بلا نية فطر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية من الصحيح المقيم. (مسكين) ولو قدم مسافر: فإن قلت: في إيجاب القضاء عليه مخالفة لما سبق من أن المسافر إذا نوى الإفطار ثم قدم مصره فنوى الصوم في وقته صح، قلت: لا تخالف؛ لأن ما ههنا يحمل على ما إذا قدم بعد استعمال المفطر أو بعد مضي وقت النية فيمسك وعليه القضاء. (فتح)
By fainting: meaning due to it, because it is a type of illness. He makes up what he missed of Ramadan from some of the day... He makes up the fast missed due to fainting except a day in which fainting occurred at night. Indication: the ruling does not differ if fainting occurs during the day. Reason for no qada: he intended at night assuming he is in a state of uprightness. But if he is dissolute or a traveler, he makes up everything because no evidence of intention. So if it is known he intended, no qada; if known he did not, qada. Contrary to Malik. And insanity non-extended: insanity negates intention which is a condition of worship. Qada is not required for extended insanity (covering whole month) due to hardship. Non-extended is treated like sleep. Qada is required for non-extended insanity. The fatwa is that one makes up what was missed due to non-extensive insanity. For original insanity (since birth), according to Muhammad, no qada for the past; Abu Yusuf says qada; the soundest is no qada. Zufar and Shafi'i say no qada even for non-extended. Our argument: qada is not dependent on viability of performance but on cause, as with sleeper and traveler. And by abstaining without intention: one who does not intend fasting nor breaking fast in Ramadan must make up and no kaffarah. Zufar says it counts as fast because abstinence is what is required. Our proof: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion' (98:5) and sincerity requires intention. The difference: if he does not eat, no qada according to Zufar; if he eats, kaffarah because he is fasting according to him. According to Abu Hanifa, opposite: no kaffarah if after zawal; according to them, kaffarah if before zawal. And if a traveler returns: this is not contradictory to earlier because it refers to returning after using a mufattir or after the time of intention.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage continues the section on impediments, covering three additional rulings. First, on fainting: if a person faints during the day, he must make up the fast (qada) unless the fainting occurred at night, because it is presumed he had intended to fast. If it is known he did not intend, qada is required. Malik holds qada even for night fainting, which is contrary to the Hanafi position. Second, on insanity: insanity destroys the validity of intention, which is a condition for fasting. If insanity covers the entire month, no qada is required due to hardship. If it is non-extended (i.e., not covering the whole month), qada is required, analogous to sleep. For original insanity (congenital), there is a difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf; the soundest position is that no qada is required for the past. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that qada is never required because it depends on the obligation to perform, which is absent. The Hanafi response is that qada is based on the cause (e.g., being Muslim and capable of intention) and not on actual performance, as evidenced by the sleeper and traveler who must make up fasts they never performed. Third, on fasting without intention: if a person in Ramadan refrains from eating and drinking without intending to fast, he must make up the day and no kaffarah (expiation) is due. Zufar holds that this counts as a valid fast because the obligation is merely abstinence. The Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse requiring sincerity (98:5), which necessitates intention. The practical difference: according to Zufar, if he later eats, kaffarah becomes obligatory; according to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah if he eats after zawal, but kaffarah if before zawal (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage ends by clarifying the ruling on a traveler returning to his city: if he returns after having used something that breaks his fast or after the time of intention, he must make up the day; this does not contradict the earlier ruling that a returning traveler may validly intend fasting if he returns in time.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighma'): Fainting, a temporary loss of consciousness due to illness or similar cause.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast after its time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man faints at night before Fajr during Ramadan and wakes up after Maghrib. According to the Hanafi school:
A) He must make up the day because fainting invalidates the fast.
B) He does not need to make up because the fainting was at night.
C) He must make up only if he was a traveler.
D) He must make up and also pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Qada for fainting is only required if the fainting occurs during the day or if intention is proven absent; night fainting with presumption of intention does not require qada.

Qada for Insanity (Junun) During Ramadan
Ruling on qada for insanity with internal and external disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between extended and non-extended insanity, state the Hanafi ruling on qada for each, and evaluate the objections of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Non-extended insanity requires qada like sleep; extended insanity does not due to hardship.

This lesson explores the Hanafi treatment of insanity (junun) in fasting obligations. The critical distinction is whether the insanity covers the entire month or only part of it. We will analyze the analogy to sleep and the dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who reject qada entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is insanity during Ramadan. The Hanafi ruling is: if insanity is non-extended (i.e., not covering the whole month), qada is required; if it is extended (covers entire month), no qada is required.
Extended insanity means the person never becomes conscious during the month.
2
Understand the basis: analogy to sleep
Non-extended insanity is analogized to sleep: both negate intention but do not remove the underlying obligation. The sleeper must make up fasts; similarly, the insane person must make up fasts during lucid intervals.
The qiyas is that the dhimmah remains liable even if performance is temporarily impossible.
3
Note the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on original insanity
For congenital insanity (junun asli), Imam Muhammad holds no qada for the past (like a child), while Abu Yusuf holds qada is required. The soundest view is no qada.
4
Analyze the opposing view of Zufar and Shafi'i
They argue that qada depends on the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada), which is absent due to lack of legal capacity. The Hanafi response: qada is based on the cause (being a Muslim of sound mind at the onset of Ramadan) and is evidenced by the traveller who must make up even though he is exempted from performance.

Source Text

أي يقضي مافات عنه

حدث في ليلته [ أي فيه أم في ليلته فلا يقضيه لوجود الصوم إلا إذا علم أنه لم ينو . (ط، ع)] أي يقضي الصوم الفائت بالإغماء سوى يوم حدث الإغماء في ليلته، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحكم لا يختلف بحدوث الإغماء في اليوم؛ لأنه إذا لم يجب القضاء بحدوثه في الليلة مع أنها غير محل للصوم فلأن لا يجب بحدوثه في اليوم أولى، ووجه عدم القضاء: أنه نوى من الليل حملاً لحاله على الصلاح حتى لو كان منهتكا يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو مسافراً، قضى الكل؛ لعدم ما يدل على وجود النية، فالحاصل أنه إذا علم أنه نوى فلا شك في عدم القضاء، وإن علم أنه لم ينو فلا شك في القضاء خلافاً لمالك، فإنه يوجب قضاء اليوم الذي حدث فيه الإغماء أيضاً، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط النية عنده لصوم كل يوم وهو خلاف ما سبق عنه. (عيني، فتح) ويجنون غير ممتد: اعلم أن الجنون ينافي النية التي هي شرط العبادات، فلا يجب القضاء الممتد منه مطلقا، مع أن يكون مستوعبا لشهر رمضان كله للحرج وغير الممتد جعل كالنوم؛ لأن الجنون لا ينفي أصل الوجوب، والمراد باستيعابه أن لا يفيق مقدار ما يمكنه إنشاء الصوم فيه حتى لو أفاق في ليلة أو في آخر يوم منه فقط لا قضاء عليه، وعليه الفتوى سواء كان أصليا أو عارضياً أي يقضي ما فات بجنون غير مستغرق للشهر، وقيل: هذا إذا بلغ مفيقا ثم جن، أما إذا بلغ مجنونا وهو الجنون الأصلي ثم أفاق في بعض الشهر فعن محمد أنه ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى إلحاقا له بالصبي، واختاره بعض المتأخرين، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجب عليه قضاء ما مضى من الشهر كالجنون العارض ولا رواية فيه عن الإمام، والأصح أنه ليس على المجنون الأصلي قضاء ما مضى، وقال زفر والشافعي: يسقط القضاء في جنون غير ممتد أيضاً؛ لأن القضاء فرع وجوب الأداء وهو منتف لعدم الأهلية فكذا ما يبتنى عليه، ونحن لا نسلم أن القضاء يترتب على وجوب الأداء بل يجب في الذمة لوجود السبب وهو ألا تری أن النائم يجب عليه القضاء، وهو مرفوع عنه في حق الأداء، وكذا المسافر يجب عليه القضاء وهو الشهر، دون الأداء، وإذا تحقق الوجوب بلا مانع يتعين القضاء، وقال مالك: يقضي في الحالين. (عيني، فتح) غير ممتد أي مستغرق للشهر ولو استغرقه لا يقضي. (ط) وبإمساك : يعني من لم ينو بالإمساك صوما ولا فطرا رمضان يقضي ولا يكفر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية؛ لأن المستحق عليه الإمساك، فعلى أي وجه وجد يقع عنه كما إذا وهب كل النصاب من الفقير ولنا أن المستحق عليه هو الإمساك بجهة العبادة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ ﴾ (البيئة:٥) والإخلاص لا يكون بدون النية، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في لزوم القضاء ووجوب الكفارة، فإن لم يأكل لا يلزم القضاء عنده، وإن أكل تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأنه صائم عنده، وعند أبي حنيفة : الحكم على عكسه؛ لأنه غير صائم وعندهما إن أكل بعد الزوال فكما قال أبو حنيفة: لا كفارة عليه، وإن أكل قبله تجب عليه الكفارة؛ لأنه فوت إمكان التحصيل. (مستخلص، فتح) وفطر: أي بلا نية فطر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية من الصحيح المقيم. (مسكين) ولو قدم مسافر: فإن قلت: في إيجاب القضاء عليه مخالفة لما سبق من أن المسافر إذا نوى الإفطار ثم قدم مصره فنوى الصوم في وقته صح، قلت: لا تخالف؛ لأن ما ههنا يحمل على ما إذا قدم بعد استعمال المفطر أو بعد مضي وقت النية فيمسك وعليه القضاء. (فتح)
By fainting: meaning due to it, because it is a type of illness. He makes up what he missed of Ramadan from some of the day... He makes up the fast missed due to fainting except a day in which fainting occurred at night. Indication: the ruling does not differ if fainting occurs during the day. Reason for no qada: he intended at night assuming he is in a state of uprightness. But if he is dissolute or a traveler, he makes up everything because no evidence of intention. So if it is known he intended, no qada; if known he did not, qada. Contrary to Malik. And insanity non-extended: insanity negates intention which is a condition of worship. Qada is not required for extended insanity (covering whole month) due to hardship. Non-extended is treated like sleep. Qada is required for non-extended insanity. The fatwa is that one makes up what was missed due to non-extensive insanity. For original insanity (since birth), according to Muhammad, no qada for the past; Abu Yusuf says qada; the soundest is no qada. Zufar and Shafi'i say no qada even for non-extended. Our argument: qada is not dependent on viability of performance but on cause, as with sleeper and traveler. And by abstaining without intention: one who does not intend fasting nor breaking fast in Ramadan must make up and no kaffarah. Zufar says it counts as fast because abstinence is what is required. Our proof: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion' (98:5) and sincerity requires intention. The difference: if he does not eat, no qada according to Zufar; if he eats, kaffarah because he is fasting according to him. According to Abu Hanifa, opposite: no kaffarah if after zawal; according to them, kaffarah if before zawal. And if a traveler returns: this is not contradictory to earlier because it refers to returning after using a mufattir or after the time of intention.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage continues the section on impediments, covering three additional rulings. First, on fainting: if a person faints during the day, he must make up the fast (qada) unless the fainting occurred at night, because it is presumed he had intended to fast. If it is known he did not intend, qada is required. Malik holds qada even for night fainting, which is contrary to the Hanafi position. Second, on insanity: insanity destroys the validity of intention, which is a condition for fasting. If insanity covers the entire month, no qada is required due to hardship. If it is non-extended (i.e., not covering the whole month), qada is required, analogous to sleep. For original insanity (congenital), there is a difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf; the soundest position is that no qada is required for the past. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that qada is never required because it depends on the obligation to perform, which is absent. The Hanafi response is that qada is based on the cause (e.g., being Muslim and capable of intention) and not on actual performance, as evidenced by the sleeper and traveler who must make up fasts they never performed. Third, on fasting without intention: if a person in Ramadan refrains from eating and drinking without intending to fast, he must make up the day and no kaffarah (expiation) is due. Zufar holds that this counts as a valid fast because the obligation is merely abstinence. The Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse requiring sincerity (98:5), which necessitates intention. The practical difference: according to Zufar, if he later eats, kaffarah becomes obligatory; according to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah if he eats after zawal, but kaffarah if before zawal (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The passage ends by clarifying the ruling on a traveler returning to his city: if he returns after having used something that breaks his fast or after the time of intention, he must make up the day; this does not contradict the earlier ruling that a returning traveler may validly intend fasting if he returns in time.

Key Terms
جنون غير ممتد (junun ghayr mumtadd): Insanity that does not extend for the entire month, i.e., the person has intermittent lucid periods.
جنون ممتد (junun mumtadd): Insanity that covers the entire month, with no lucid intervals.
جنون أصلي (junun asli): Insanity present from before puberty, i.e., congenital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a person whose insanity covers the entire month of Ramadan?
A) He must make up all missed fasts.
B) He does not need to make up any fasts.
C) He must make up only if the insanity was original.
D) He must make up half of the fasts.

Key Takeaway
Non-extended insanity requires qada (like sleep); extended insanity covering the whole month does not; original insanity is exempted according to the soundest view.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
9 min
Fasting Without Intention (Imsak Bila Niyyah)
Ruling on abstinence without intention with Quranic evidence and internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that fasting without intention is invalid and requires qada, explain the Quranic proof, and describe the intra-Hanafi dispute on kaffarah.

Core Idea
Without intention, fasting is invalid; qada is wajib, and kaffarah only if eating occurs before noon.

This lesson covers the scenario where a person in Ramadan refrains from food and drink without forming the intention to fast. The Hanafi school requires qada (make-up) and no expiation unless he eats before noon, according to the two disciples. We will examine the Quranic evidence and the disagreement with Zufar, who considers such abstinence a valid fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is imsak (abstaining) during Ramadan without intention to fast. The Hanafi ruling: the fast is invalid, qada is wajib, and no kaffarah is due (except as per Sahibayn if eating before zawal).
2
Understand the opposing view of Zufar
Zufar holds that the mere act of abstinence fulfills the fast, because the obligation is simply to refrain from eating. Therefore, no qada is required, and if he eats, kaffarah becomes wajib.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof: Quran 98:5
The verse commands sincerity (ikhlas) in worship, which requires intention. Abstinence without intention lacks sincerity, so the act is not a valid worship.
The dalil is dalalatan qati'ah (definitive in meaning) for the necessity of intention.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on kaffarah
Abu Hanifa: no kaffarah at all. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: kaffarah wajib if he eats before noon, but not after noon. The relied-upon fatwa follows the two disciples.

Source Text

حدث في ليلته [ أي فيه أم في ليلته فلا يقضيه لوجود الصوم إلا إذا علم أنه لم ينو . (ط، ع)] أي يقضي الصوم الفائت بالإغماء سوى يوم حدث الإغماء في ليلته، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحكم لا يختلف بحدوث الإغماء في اليوم؛ لأنه إذا لم يجب القضاء بحدوثه في الليلة مع أنها غير محل للصوم فلأن لا يجب بحدوثه في اليوم أولى، ووجه عدم القضاء: أنه نوى من الليل حملاً لحاله على الصلاح حتى لو كان منهتكا يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو مسافراً، قضى الكل؛ لعدم ما يدل على وجود النية، فالحاصل أنه إذا علم أنه نوى فلا شك في عدم القضاء، وإن علم أنه لم ينو فلا شك في القضاء خلافاً لمالك، فإنه يوجب قضاء اليوم الذي حدث فيه الإغماء أيضاً، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط النية عنده لصوم كل يوم وهو خلاف ما سبق عنه. Commentary

Key Terms
إمساك بلا نية (imsak bila niyyah): Refraining from eating, drinking, etc. during Ramadan without the intention to fast.
إخلاص (ikhlas): Sincerity in worship, directing the act solely to Allah.
زوال (zawal): Noon, the moment when the sun passes its zenith.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the ruling for a person who refrains from eating without intention but then eats before zawal?
A) Qada only, no kaffarah.
B) Qada and kaffarah.
C) No qada, kaffarah only.
D) Nothing is required because he had no intention.

Key Takeaway
Fasting without intention is invalid and requires qada; kaffarah is added if the person eats before noon, according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner

Ruling on Fainting (Ighma') During Fasting
Explains ruling on make-up for a fasting person who faints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on make-up (qada) for fainting during fasting and distinguish when intention eliminates the obligation to make up.

Core Idea
A fast is not invalidated by fainting if intention was made at night; no make-up needed.

Fainting (ighma') during Ramadan raises the question of whether the missed fast requires make-up. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on the presence of intention. This lesson explores the ruling and its rational basis, as well as a differing view from the Maliki school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that a person who faints does not need to make up the day if he had valid intention from the night. First, note that the ruling is conditional on intention.
2
Understand the rational evidence
The Hanafi proof is an a fortiori (qiyas awla) argument: if fainting at night (non-fasting time) does not require make-up, then fainting during the day has even stronger grounds for exemption.
This is a rational proof based on the principle that a lesser reason implies the greater.
3
Note the exceptions
If the person is dissolute (munhatik) or a traveler, there is no assumption of valid intention, so make-up is required. Also, if it is known that he did not intend, make-up is required.
4
Compare with Maliki view
Imam Malik requires make-up even when intention is present, because he holds that intention must be renewed for each day of Ramadan. The Hanafi response is that intention at night suffices for the entire day.
The practical difference: Hanafis do not require make-up; Malikis do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدث في ليلته، أي يقضي ما فات عنه في رمضان إلا إذا علم أنه لم ينو . (ط، ع) أي يقضي الصوم الفائت بالإغماء سوى يوم حدث الإغماء في ليلته، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحكم لا يختلف بحدوث الإغماء في اليوم؛ لأنه إذا لم يجب القضاء بحدوثه في الليلة مع أنها غير محل للصوم فلأن لا يجب بحدوثه في اليوم أولى، ووجه عدم القضاء: أنه نوى من الليل حملاً لحاله على الصلاح حتى لو كان منهتكا يعتاد الأكل في رمضان أو مسافراً، قضى الكل؛ لعدم ما يدل على وجود النية، فالحاصل أنه إذا علم أنه نوى فلا شك في عدم القضاء، وإن علم أنه لم ينو فلا شك في القضاء خلافاً لمالك، فإنه يوجب قضاء اليوم الذي حدث فيه الإغماء أيضاً، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط النية عنده لصوم كل يوم وهو خلاف ما سبق عنه. (عيني، فتح)
He makes up for what he missed in Ramadan except if it is known that he did not intend (the fast). He makes up the fast missed due to fainting except for the day on which the fainting occurred at night. The ruling does not differ if fainting occurs during the day because if make-up is not required for night occurrence (which is not a time for fasting), then it is even more not required for day occurrence. The reason for no make-up: he intended at night, assuming his state to be righteous. But if he was dissolute (used to eating in Ramadan) or a traveler, he must make up all, as there is no evidence of intention. So if it is known he intended, no make-up; if known he did not intend, make-up is required, contrary to Malik who requires make-up for the day of occurrence. This implies that in Malik's view, intention is required for each day, contrary to what was stated earlier.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on fainting (ighma') during fasting. The key principle is that fainting itself does not break the fast or require make-up as long as the person had a valid intention for the fast from the night before. The commentator explains that the ruling is the same whether fainting occurs at night or during the day, using an a fortiori argument: if fainting at night (when one is not actually fasting) does not require make-up, then fainting during the day should not require it either. The basis is that we assume the person's intention was valid unless he is known to be careless (e.g., habitual eater in Ramadan) or a traveler, in which case there is no evidence of intention. Thus the duty to make up depends on knowledge of intention. Imam Malik's opposing view is noted: he requires make-up even when intention is present, because he requires intention for each day's fast.

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighmā'): Fainting; a state of unconsciousness that disrupts fasting only if intention was absent.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention; the prerequisite for valid fasting, made at night for the following day's fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, when does a person who faints during a Ramadan day NOT have to make up that day?
A) If he fainted at night before intending.
B) If he had made a valid intention for the fast at night before fainting.
C) If he is a traveler who fainted.
D) If he is dissolute (munhatik).

Key Takeaway
The ruling on fainting during a fast depends on whether intention was made; if intention was present, no make-up is required; otherwise it is wajib.

Insanity and Qada' of Fasting: The Hanafi Distinction
Explains types of insanity and their effect on qada'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between continuous and non-continuous insanity regarding qada' and apply the Hanafi ruling to cases of original vs. incidental insanity.

Core Idea
Continuous insanity covering the whole month exempts from qada'; non-continuous insanity requires qada' by analogy with sleep.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the nuanced Hanafi position on insanity during fasting. It explains how the school distinguishes between continuous insanity (which exempts from make-up) and non-continuous insanity (which requires make-up by analogy with sleep). It also addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute over original insanity, settling on the view that no make-up is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the Hanafi ruling for each type of insanity: continuous (mustaw'ib) covering whole month → no qada'; non-continuous → qada' required; original (asli) → no qada' (according to the relied-upon view).
The ruling for original insanity is disputed within the school; the text declares al-ashah (most correct) as no qada'.
2
Locate the dalil
For non-continuous insanity, the proof is qiyas (analogy) with sleep: both negate the ability to perform but not the basis of obligation. For continuous insanity, the proof is hardship (mashaqqa).
The proof is aqli (rational analogy) rather than naqli (textual).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Sleep: obligation of performance is lifted, but the missed fast remains a debt (dayn) owed to Allah; therefore qada' is due. Similarly, non-continuous insanity does not erase the cause of the missed fast (the fast itself), so qada' must be made.
This counters the Shafi'i argument that qada' is contingent on capacity for performance.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold no qada' for any insanity because the insane lack capacity (ahliyyat al-ada'). The Hanafi response distinguishes between the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada') and the obligation of make-up (wujub al-qada') which arises from the cause.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis require make-up for intermittent insanity while Shafi'is do not.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute
For original insanity (al-junun al-asli), Imam Muhammad exempts from qada' by analogy with a child; Abu Yusuf requires qada' like incidental insanity. The relied-upon view (al-ashah) follows Muhammad.
No direct narration from Abu Hanifa exists; later scholars settled on no qada'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجنون غير ممتد: اعلم أن الجنون ينافي النية التي هي شرط العبادات، فلا يجب القضاء الممتد منه مطلقا، مع أن يكون مستوعبا لشهر رمضان كله للحرج وغير الممتد جعل كالنوم؛ لأن الجنون لا ينفي أصل الوجوب، والمراد باستيعابه أن لا يفيق مقدار ما يمكنه إنشاء الصوم فيه حتى لو أفاق في ليلة أو في آخر يوم منه فقط لا قضاء عليه، وعليه الفتوى سواء كان أصليا أو عارضياً أي يقضي ما فات بجنون غير مستغرق للشهر، وقيل: هذا إذا بلغ مفيقا ثم جن، أما إذا بلغ مجنونا وهو الجنون الأصلي ثم أفاق في بعض الشهر فعن محمد أنه ليس عليه قضاء ما مضى إلحاقا له بالصبي، واختاره بعض المتأخرين، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجب عليه قضاء ما مضى من الشهر كالجنون العارض ولا رواية فيه عن الإمام، والأصح أنه ليس على المجنون الأصلي قضاء ما مضى، وقال زفر والشافعي: يسقط القضاء في جنون غير ممتد أيضاً؛ لأن القضاء فرع وجوب الأداء وهو منتف لعدم الأهلية فكذا ما يبتني عليه، ونحن لا نسلم أن القضاء يترتب على وجوب الأداء بل يجب في الذمة لوجود السبب وهو ألا تری أن النائم يجب عليه القضاء، وهو مرفوع عنه في حق الأداء، وكذا المسافر يجب عليه القضاء دون الأداء، وإذا تحقق الوجوب بلا مانع يتعين القضاء، وقال مالك: يقضي في الحالين. (عيني، فتح)
And as for non-continuous insanity: Know that insanity negates intention which is a condition for acts of worship. So for continuous insanity covering the entire month of Ramadan, make-up is not required at all due to hardship. Non-continuous insanity is treated like sleep, because insanity does not negate the basis of obligation. By covering the entire month is meant that he does not regain consciousness for enough time to initiate fasting; even if he regains consciousness for one night or only the last day, no make-up is required. This is the fatwa, whether the insanity is original or incidental. He makes up what was missed due to non-covering insanity. It is said: this applies when he reached puberty while sane then became insane. As for one who reached puberty while insane (original insanity) then regained consciousness for part of the month, according to Muhammad, he does not have to make up what passed, by analogy with a child. Some later scholars chose this. According to Abu Yusuf, he must make up what passed from the month, like incidental insanity, and there is no narration from Imam (Abu Hanifa). The correct view is that one with original insanity does not have to make up. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: make-up is waived for non-continuous insanity as well, because make-up is a branch of the obligation of performance, which is absent due to lack of capacity. But we do not accept that make-up is contingent on obligation of performance; rather it becomes due in the dhimmah due to the existence of the cause (i.e., the missed fast). Do you not see that a sleeping person must make up, even though the obligation of performance is lifted from him? Similarly, a traveler must make up even though he is not required to perform. So when obligation is established without a barrier, make-up is determined. Malik said: make-up is required in both cases.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of insanity (junun) on fasting. The Hanafi position distinguishes between continuous insanity covering the entire month (mumtadd mustaw'ib) and non-continuous insanity. Continuous insanity exempts from make-up due to hardship. Non-continuous insanity requires make-up by analogy with sleep: the obligation is not negated, but performance is temporarily impossible. Within the Hanafi school, there is a dispute regarding original insanity (al-junun al-asli): Imam Muhammad exempts from make-up by analogy with a child, while Abu Yusuf requires make-up. The relied-upon view (al-ashah) is that original insanity does not require make-up. The passage also refutes Zufar and al-Shafi'i who argue that make-up is only necessary if performance was obligatory; the Hanafi response is that the obligation to make up arises from the cause (the missed fast), not from the obligation of performance, as exemplified by the sleeping person and traveler.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junūn): Insanity; a condition that negates intention but does not negate the basis of obligation for fasting.
ممتد (mumtadd): Continuous insanity covering the entire month; exempts from make-up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of insanity does NOT require make-up for missed fasts?
A) Non-continuous insanity lasting one day.
B) Insanity covering the entire month of Ramadan without any lucid interval.
C) Incidental insanity that does not cover the whole month.
D) Original insanity that ends before Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires make-up for non-continuous insanity but not for continuous insanity covering the entire month, with original insanity also exempt.

Refraining Without Intention in Ramadan: The Necessity of Niyyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of fast without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires intention for a valid fast and evaluate the differing views of Zufar and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abstaining without intention is not a valid fast; intention is the essence of worship.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: if someone refrains from eating and drinking during Ramadan but without any intention to fast or break the fast, what is the ruling? The Hanafi position, based on a Quranic verse requiring sincerity, insists on intention. However, Zufar and the Sahibayn offer nuanced alternatives with different practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is physical abstention (imsak) during Ramadan without any intention (niyyah) to fast or to break the fast. The dispute is over whether this counts as a valid fast and what obligations arise.
This scenario is distinct from forgetfulness or coercion.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa (followed by the majority), such a person must make up (qada) the day but does not owe kaffarah. The fast is invalid because intention is a pillar.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Examine Zufar's argument
Zufar argues that the fast is valid without intention because the required duty is mere abstention. He draws an analogy with zakat: giving away the nisab fulfills the obligation regardless of intent.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response cites Quran 98:5: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion.' Sincerity (ikhlas) requires intention, so abstention without intention is not worship.
The dalala of the verse is zanni but sufficient for istidlal.
5
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequences differ: according to Zufar, if the person does not eat, no make-up is required; if he eats, kaffarah is due because he broke a valid fast. According to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah because he was not fasting. The Sahibayn split based on time: if he eats before zawal, kaffarah; after zawal, no kaffarah.
Understanding the thamarah clarifies why the dispute matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبإمساك : يعني من لم ينو بالإمساك صوما ولا فطرا رمضان يقضي ولا يكفر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية؛ لأن المستحق عليه الإمساك، فعلى أي وجه وجد يقع عنه كما إذا وهب كل النصاب من الفقير ولنا أن المستحق عليه هو الإمساك بجهة العبادة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ ﴾ (البيئة:٥) والإخلاص لا يكون بدون النية، وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في لزوم القضاء ووجوب الكفارة، فإن لم يأكل لا يلزم القضاء عنده، وإن أكل تلزمه الكفارة؛ لأنه صائم عنده، وعند أبي حنيفة : الحكم على عكسه؛ لأنه غير صائم وعندهما إن أكل بعد الزوال فكما قال أبو حنيفة: لا كفارة عليه، وإن أكل قبله تجب عليه الكفارة؛ لأنه فوت إمكان التحصيل. (مستخلص، فتح) وفطر: أي بلا نية فطر، وقال زفر: يتأدى صوم رمضان بلا نية من الصحيح المقيم. (مسكين)
And regarding refraining (without intention): meaning one who refrains without intending fasting or breaking fast in Ramadan must make up and does not have to pay kaffarah. Zufar said: the Ramadan fast is valid without intention because the required duty is refraining (from eating), so no matter how it occurs, it suffices, just as if one gives away the entire nisab to a poor person (to avoid zakat? unclear). But our argument is that the required duty is refraining with the mode of worship, because Allah said: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion' (98:5), and sincerity cannot be without intention. The practical consequence appears in the necessity of make-up and the obligation of kaffarah. According to Zufar, if he does not eat, no make-up is required; if he eats, kaffarah is required because according to him he is a fasting person. According to Abu Hanifa, the ruling is the opposite because he is not fasting. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if he eats after noon, it is as Abu Hanifa said: no kaffarah; if he eats before noon, kaffarah is required because he forfeited the possibility of rectification. And regarding breaking (the fast) without intention of breaking: Zufar said: the Ramadan fast is valid without intention for a healthy resident.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage deals with a person who abstains from eating, drinking, etc. during Ramadan but without any intention to fast or to break the fast. The Hanafi ruling is that such a person must make up the day but does not have to pay kaffarah. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagrees, arguing that the only requirement is physical abstention, so the fast is valid even without intention. He supports this with an analogy: if someone gives away the entire nisab to avoid zakat, the zakat obligation is fulfilled regardless of intent. The Hanafi response cites the Quranic verse requiring sincerity, which necessitates intention. The dispute has practical consequences: according to Zufar, if the person does not eat, no make-up is needed; if he eats, kaffarah is due because he was considered a fasting person who broke the fast. According to Abu Hanifa, no kaffarah because he was not fasting. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) have a nuanced position: if he eats after zawal (noon), no kaffarah (like Abu Hanifa); if he eats before zawal, kaffarah is required. A similar dispute is mentioned for one who breaks the fast without any intention to break.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-im'sāk): Physical abstention from eating, drinking, etc.
الفطر (al-fiṭr): Breaking the fast; here refers to the act of intending to break without actual consumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa), what is the obligation of someone who refrains from eating and drinking in Ramadan without any intention?
A) No make-up, no kaffarah; the fast is valid.
B) He must make up the day and pay kaffarah.
C) He must make up the day but no kaffarah.
D) He must pay kaffarah but no make-up.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, intention (niyyah) is a necessary condition for the validity of the Ramadan fast; mere abstention is insufficient.
